The RED PAGE
A Shavian interlude
Bernard Shaw's last three plays— Getting
Married, The Doctor’s Dilemma, and The
Showing Up of Blanco Posnet have not yet
been published, but there has been a great
deal of Shaw in the London newspapers lately.
He has demolished the arguments of the
asinine Censor, and incidentally punctured
the self-esteem of H. G. Wells. In the in-
tervals of his letter-writing he has thrown off a
one-act farce, a Shavian “open letter” about
suffragettes, the conscription, and a number
of other things, and has called it Press
Guttings: A Topical Sketch compiled from the
editorial and correspondence columns of the
Daily Papers. (Constable, Is. net.)
In 1912 martial law has been proclaimed
in London on account of the violence of the
unenfranchised, and on the Ist April the
Prime Minister, Mr. Balsquith, has to dis-
guise himself as a woman in order to get
through London, and tie himself to the door-
scraper to get into General Mitehener’s rooms,
The .situation is serious, and the Prime
Minister wants Mitchener’s advice. The
advice he gets is very simple instead of
sending the misguided women to Holloway,
and killing them slowly . . . shoot a few
promptly and humanely. . . ,
Balsquith : But public opinion would never
stand it.
Mitchener (walking about and laying down
the law) : There’s no such thing as public opinion.
Balsquith : No such thing as public opinion ! !
Mitchener : Absolutely no such thing. There
are certain persons who entertain certain opinions.
W ell, shoot them down. When you have shot
them down, there are no longer any persons enter-
taining those opinions alive; consequently, there
is no longer any more of the public opinion you
are so much afraid of. Grasp that fact, iny dear
Balsquith, and you have srrusped the secret of
government. Public opinion is mind. Mind is
inseparable from matter. Shoot down the matter
and you kill the mind.
Their discussion of the German invasion
and army discipline is interrupted by a depu-
tation of anti-suffragists. Balsquith receives
them while Mitchener calls the charwoman,
Mrs. Farrell, to get his uniform. She tells
him that the only uniform available is that
of General Sandstone, and complains of the
way that General swears at her.
Mitchener : When a man has risked his life on
eight battlefields, Mrs. Farrell, he has given
sufficient proof of his self-control to be excused a
little strong language.
Mrs. Farrell : Would you put up with bad
language from me beeause I’ve risked me life
eight times in child-bed?
Mitchener : My dear Mrs. Farrell, you surely
would not compare a risk of that harmless,
domestic kind to the fearful risks of the battle-
field ?
Mrs. Jarrell : I wouldn’t compare risks run to
bear livin’ people into the world to risks run to
blow dhein out of it. A mother’s risk is jooty ; a
soldier’s is nothin’ but divilment.
Mitchener (nettled): Let me tell you, Mrs.
Jarrell, that if the men did not fight, the women
would have to fight themselves. We spare you
that, at all events.
Mrs. J arrell : You can’t help yourselves. If
three-quarters of you was killed we could replace
you with the help of the other quarter. If three-
quarters of us was killed, how many people would
there be in England in another generation ? If it
wasn t for that the men’d put the fightin’ on us
just as they put all the other dhrudgery. What
would you do if we was all kilt? Would you go
to bed and have twins?
Mitchener: Really, Mrs. Farrell, you must
discuss these questions with a medical man. You
make me blush positively.
Mrs. Farrell (grumbling to herself): A good
job, too. If I could have made Farrell blush I
wouldn’t have had to risk me life so often. You
'n’ your risks V your bravery V yourself-conthrol
indeed ! “ Why don't you conthrol yourself ?” I
sez to J’arrell. “ It’s agen me religion,” he sez.
Mitchener (plaintively): Mrs. Farrell, you’re
a women of very powerful mind. I’m not quali-
fied to argue these delicate matters with you.
Mitchener hears his disrespectful orderly’s
statement of the feelings of a common soldier
deprived of political rights before the fero-
cious Mrs. Banger and atrocious Lady Fan-
shawe, of the Anti-Suffraget League, finally
drive the poor man to desperation. He con-
fesses to the latter ,: —
I am not clever at discussing public questions,
because, as an English gentleman, I was not
brought up to use my brains.’ But occasionally,
after a number of remarks which are, perhaps,
sometimes rather idiotic, I get certain convictions,
j. hanks to you, I have now got a conviction that
this woman question is not a question of lovely
and accomplished females, but of dowdies. The
average English woman is a dowdy, and never has
half a chance of becoming anything else.
Mrs. Banger sits on old Sandstone’s
head until he proposes ; Mitchener then asks
the practical Mrs. Farrell to marry him,
and Lady Fanshawe consents to become
Balsquith’s Egeria, and tells him that he may
sit at her feet.
Balsquith (doing so): That is my ideal of
romantic happiness. It commits me exactly as
far as I desire to venture.
The Censor refused a license for Press
Guttings, and, as iin the case of Blanco
Posnet, he stated his reasons for doing so.
That was a mistake. An autocrat should
not argue or explain. It is likely to un-
loosen the foundations of respect. The
Censor stated that he would pass Blancc
Posnet if certain passages were omitted. He
did not object to the obvious immorality
of one of the characters, but to the
statement that she had had “ immoral
relations ’; he did not object to the
general irreverence or blasphemy of the
play, but to three or four irreverent
sentences. As The English Review says :
“ What sort of person is" Mr. Redford ? He
will license any kind of play turning upon
leering vice; he revels—as a Censor—in
what silly young men call ‘dining with
actresses. * He will pass any kind of breach
of the seventh commandment, as long as the
‘guilty parties ’ escape punishment.”
Press Cuttings was censored because it
contained “ personalities expressed or under-
stood, and the Censor's department had a
rule that living persons are not to be repre-
sented on the stage. For other reasons
Granville Barker’s IVaste was tired out, and
ltd ward Garnett’s The Breaking Point
thrown after it. It is stated that the Censor
objected to the expression “amorous god-
desses ” in one of Yeats’plays on the ground
that “ it would offend many people and give
pleasure to none ! ” Garnett relieved his
feelings in a paper read before the Playgoers’
Club, which is printed in the July Fort-
nightly. Barker is printing his play as
Shaw has done : but Shaw also organised a
Civic and Dramatic Guild to give a techni-
cally private performance of Press Guttings
at the Royal Court Theatre. Shaw delights
in shindies and in advertisement. He is
certainly getting plenty of both.
Mixed Australian
One passage/in Miss Mordaunt’s Rosemary
stands-out surprisingly from the graceful
frippery of the rest :
I would rather have coarseness than inanity any
day. I would rather fill my days with the broad,
robust writings of Smollett and Fielding than the
graceful fripperies, the genteel refinement of the
modern American authors. I used to adore
artistic people ; I do not now ; I would be better
pleased any day to spend my time among rough
sailors, with the pungent salt winds whipping life
into us, hardening our hands and strengthening
tree™ th ’ wi v^ r | ti!i the soil and fell the
2 he Animals of Australia — Mammals, Hep-
tiles and Amphibians —are described in popu-
lar and scientific fashion by A. H. S. Lucas
Souef (author of
Wild Lite in Australia), and illustrated
with about a hundred and fifty photographs
(YVhitcombe and Tombs). This is a volume
which all “Abo.” readers should have, as i
gives information about our fauna that some
of them are always seeking. There is an
account of the White-footed Jerboa Eat, who
\Seems to be a link between the pouched anc
unpouched mammals, and whose ancestors
have been found in the Pleistocene oi
N.b. W. The Nest-building Jerboa combines
with his fellow rats to construct a tenement
house—beehive-shaped, with compartments
one for each family. “Abo.” contributors,
if not already satisfied, are authoratively
informed that the young of the kangaroo
are not born in the pouch ; and may
get here pretty well all that is known
of the boougarry, or tree kangaroo.
An anecdote is quoted to illustrate
the sagacity of the opossum, which,
like the cat, came back home from 65 miles
away. The acrobatic feat of a tiger snake,
observed by a Government Surveyor near
Mount Wilson, N.S.W., takes some beating.
It was enjoying a sun-bath—stretched along
the top wire of a fence, the folds of the body
being perfectly adjusted to maintain the
delicate balance.
Among the interesting freaks are the
knobby-tailed lizards, the moloch horridus,
and the lung fish, which has a section to
himself. The platypus, which is our prize
exhibit in natural history, gets more space
than any other ; but all are treated with
sufficient fulness to be easily identified.
The book has evidently been written with
great care and is printed excellently. For
scientific students it will take an honored
place with such monographs as Froggatt’s
Australian Insects , Stead’s Fishes, and
Krefi't’s Snakes.
A volume called Sunny South Australia
(Hussey and Gillingham, Adelaide) has come
to us from the authoress, Mrs. May
Vivienne. It is as full of information as
a guide-book, but much more brightly writ-
ten and most gorgeously “ gotten up ” in
cream and gold. Besides, it is profusely
illustrated. The information was gathered
by the authoress, who travelled some 4000
miles to get it; <• and what there isn’t in it
about S.A. can’t be worth knowing. Mrs.
Vivienne is generous with her adjectives in
referring to the people she met; and is
unusually modest (for, an author) in placing
her portrait at the end of the book instead
of the beginning.
SNOW.
“Who affirms that crystals are alive?”
I affirm it, let who will deny:—
Crystals are engendered, wax and thrive,
Wane and wither; I have seen them die.
Trust me, masters, crystals have their day,
Eager to attain the perfect norm,
Lit with purpose, potent to display
Facet, angle, color, beauty, form.
Water-crystals need for flower and root
Sixty clear degrees, no less, no more ;
Snow, so fickle, still in this acute
Angle thinks, and learns no other lore :
Such its life, and such its pleasure is,
Such its art and traffic, such its gain,
Evermore in new conjunctions this
Admirable angle to maintain.
* • * «
Worlds of men and snow endure, increase,
Woven of power and passion to defy
Time and travail: only races cease,
Individual men and crystals die.
Jewelled shapes of snow whose feathery showers,
Fallen or falling wither at a breath.
All afraid are they, and loth as flowers,
Beasts and men to tread the way to death.
Once I saw upon an object glass,
Martyred underneath a microscope,
One elaborate snow-flake slowly pass,
Dying hard, beyond the reach of hope.
Still from shape to shape the crystal changed.
Writhing in its agony; and still,
Less and less elaborate, arranged
Potently the angle of its will.
Tortured to a simple final form,
Angles six and six divergent beams,
Lo, in death it touched the perfect norm
Verifying all its crystal dreams 1
Such the noble tragedy of one
Martyred snow-flake. Who can tell the fate
Heinous and uncouth of showers undone,
Fallen in cities ! —showers that expiate
Errant lives from polar worlds adrift
Where the great millennial snows abide ;
Castaways from mountain-chains that lift
Snowy summits in perennial pride;
Nomad snows, or snows in evil day
Born to urban ruin, to be tossed,
Trampled, shovelled, ploughed and swept away
Down the seething sewers : all the frost
Flowers of heaven melted up with lees,
Offal, recrement, but every flake
Showing to the last in fixed degrees
Perfect crystals for the crystal’s sake.
(From John Davidson’s posthumous volume—
“Fleet Street and Other Poems.”)
CHILDREN OF TOIL.
London Orphan Asylum.
.Breaking from the lap of the lowland pasture,
Sudden as a foam-crest single on a
Dreamy in the rich noon the old grey school-house
Palisades the heavens with spires slim and free.
Creeper-clad the walls breathe ripiing crimson,
Fiery as Youth all athirst for its day . . .
Oh, that the day might be such an one that sunset
Comes neither feared nor reproached for delay !
Fleeing from the schoolroom’s velvet coolness,
Light lulling croons to the hill’s head are spun.
Upward to the teacher demure little pupils
Turn timid faces, as buds to April sun.
Cheerily they pass through the merry lanes and
meadows,
Sombre are their dresses, their faces are as
bright;
Dreamy, glearuy Springtime is in their hearts,
but shadows
Hover in their going to strangle their delight.
When to the moon she unfolds her gardens,
Starred through and through with the pale
passion-flower ;
Then on the night hangs the life-breath of Child-
hood ;
Move with the spent leaf; it is her little hour,
Soon shab bier sweetness be yielded to the moon-
light,
Ay, the meiting moonlight soon alone shall
know
Hopes and fears and fancies, for swallow-swift
the years fly ;
Early toil is theiri, poor joy and petty woe /
Thomas Burke ('sometime charity child , working
lad. unemployed , and under-ranker) — from
The English Review.
Probably Bulvver Lytton’s The Coming
Race is not much read nowadays. It does
not occupy a high place amongst the accounts
of imaginary communities; yet it is not
without interest, and the following extract
from the remarks of one of the inhabitants
seems to be prevision of radio-activity :—
When you talk of .matter as something in itself
inert and motionless, your parents or tutors surely
cannot have left you so ignorant as not to know
that no form of matter is motionless and inert :
every partiele is constantly in motion and con-
stantly acted upon by agencies, of which heat is
the most apparent and rapid, but vril the most
subtle and, when skilfully wielded, the most
powerful.
{The Coming Race, however, was published as
late as 1871. Dr. John Masson’s new work
on Lucretius: Epicurean and Poet finds the
atom and electron, besides not a few modern
ideas, in De Rerum Natura, published some-
time B.C., and Lucretius owed a good deal
to the more ancient Democritus, to whose
shade Carl Snyder dedicated his book on
The World Machine.
The title of Elenor Mordaunt's musings in
pioso, Rosenuiry — That's for Remembrance
(*-• C. Lothian), is just the sort of senti-
mental title a woman would select for this
kind of book. It is an unpretentious collection
of notes on tilings in general, neatly written ;
but as tame and colorless as Ophelia herself*
I here is a remembrance of Elizabeth and her
German Garden, and Ella Maitland’s Day
Book of Bethia llardacre about it; and echoes
from other books start from its pages, for the
writer says truly, “My thoughts and my
memory are stimulated continually by what
other people have said about things, often
the most trival, even ludicrous.” It will be
remembered that Charles Lamb asked why
we should not have a grace before Milton
and Shakespeare, a devotional exercise pro-
per to be said before reading The Faery
Queen. Perhaps the memory of Lamb’s
essay moved Elenor Mordaunt to say :
What responsibility the writing of a book brings
with it! It seems as if before starting on so
solemn a task one should prepare oneself, as did
the kmghts of old in the solemn quest for the
'croiaen brrail, by fasting, by sacrament, by prayer.
We wonder if Miss Mordaunt indulged in
much fasting and prayer before writing Rose-
mary, and whether she or the printer is re-
sponsible for the frequent errors in names
quoted in the book.
It is in such a spirit that one takes up a
book like Alfred Searcy’s In Australian
1 Topics (George Robertson & Co.) with
pleasure. That such a book should have
gone into a second edition proves that a good
thing finds appreciation sometimes. The
preface, by E. Wlutinglbn, mentions that—
Louis Peeke and Mr. Frank Bullen
told Mr Searcy, after reading his pamphlet
(the skeleton of the present book), that with
such abundant experiences to work on
they could have knocked together a dozen
books.” “ Knocked together ” —exactly !
Becke and Bullen are knocking together
books of a rather flimsy character out of ex-
periences already used and beaten out thin.
Searcy, however, has poured into one book
the story of his lengthy career in the
Northern Territory, a story filled with real
adventures as exciting as Be Rougemont’s
imaginary ones. The anecdotes are given in
the simple fashion of one who is talking to a
mate about old times.
Essex Evans’ Queensland Jubilee Ode,
Queen of the North , printed in the London
l imes, has been published by Diddams and
Co., Brisbane, in an attractive booklet, with
illustrations by Alek Sass. After hailing
the State as the “Radiant One,” “Daughter
of the Sun,” and “ Queen of the North ” in
the manner permissible in Jubilee verse, the
ode sounds a note of warning about the open
back gate of Australia :
Sit thou no more inert of fame,
But let the wide world hear thy name.
See where thy realms spread line on line—
Thy empty realms that cry in shame
For hands to make them doubly thine—
Fill up thy frontiers ; man the gate
Before too late !
Prepare, ere falls the hour of Fate
When death-shells tain their iron hate,
And all in vain thv blood is poured—
For dark aslant the Northern Gate
I see the Shadow of the Sword ;
I hear the storm-clouds break in wrath,
Queen of the North !
The decorations by Sass are, with one excep-
tion, very effective, and the booklet has been
turned out as neatly as anyone could desire.
Opcdodes—-Patriotic ' and Miscellaneous
Verses, by Heber H. Booth (Powell and Co.,
Brisbane), informs the reader in the dedi-
cation: “Camerado, this is no book, who
touches this touches a man.” A man, may
be; but no poet. Some of the verses are
“after Longfellow,” others after Lawson or
somebody else, and these lines from the
author’s Edelweiss really define his position
as a versifier:—
I claim the right, although so crude my art,
That I a.11 rashly act the pupil’s part,
Who, with his unskilled how, would fondly deem
To wake a Stradivarius from its dream.
And thrill with melody each listening heart.
The latest addition -to the N.S.W.
Bookstall Co.’s shilling novels —A Chinese
Vengeance , by It. A. Kent, is up to the level
of its predecessors in thrill and workmanship.
The action takes place in Canton, the high
seas and England, and is very willing all the
way. Though the cover design is striking
enough, the illustrations are poor. One
shows a wax match throwing as much light
in a dark place as an arc lamp ; another," of
a group, has one carefully finished head ;
the rest caricatures. If worth illustrating
at all, a book deserves better drawings than
these.

A COMPLETE CURE.
“In the background of my memory there is a
distinct recollection of many days and weeks of
physical adversity, such as I almost dread tore-
call,” said Mr. Frederick William Martin, of No.
51 Pitt-street, Waterloo, when requested to give
an outline of his infirmities, now happily over-
come, “But,” he went on. “since anything I
tell you may prove a source of comfort and en-
couragement to other poor mortals. I’ll gladly do as
you wish. Yes, I was ailing for a very long time.
A more bilious subject than I used to be you
would have to travel a long way to find, and then
I doubt if he could speak of as many disappoint-
ments connected with his illness as I can—how I
gave first one medicine and then another a trial,
meeting failure after failure, till one could scarcely
believe there was anything in an apothecary’s
shop that had not been resorted to, and all to no
purpose, when I would give all the drugs and pills
and things up in digust, only to start again later
on, through the demands of my sufferings, and go
through the same experience again. At last I
consulted a doctor, a vain hope it appeared to me
after what I had taken already, and so it proved
t.o be, for my health was no better than before.
To get up of a morning and feel that there is no-
thing pleasant to be done or thought of during the
day, may not seem in itself a very serious affair ;
but it indicates that a person’s health is not what
it should be, and if allowed to go unchecked
through neglecting to take some good medicine,
the consequences may prove more serious than
you would imagine.”
“You believe, then, that if you had taken a
remedy when you found yourself suffering from
lassitude and inability to eat and sleep properly,
you would have avoided the sickness you en-
countered?”
“ Most decidedly I do. I verily believe that if
I had taken a bottle or two of Clements Tonic at
the outset it would have Saved me pounds and
pounds, besides the intense misery I endured.
This is whfit I always tell my friends, to attack
their ailments at once with Clements Tonic, not
to wait till they get a firmer hold of the system,
because the troubles then take longer to eradicate.
Mv appetite faded, till it had completely vanished,
and I simply forced myself to eat; ray nights be-
came more restless, and I got up feeling that I
did not care whether I stood on my feet or sank
to the bed again. A dimness affected my eyesight,
a bitterness spread on my tongue, which was
always coated white, and from daylight till dark
I was never free from headaches. Small amounts
of food made my stomach most uncomfortable,
and between the shoulders it felt like a sharp-
pointed instrument tapping there every now and
then. And how fearfully dizzy I used to get be-
fore Clements Tonic got to work, besides which
my nerves were as shaky as could be. Anyhow,
I was not much good for work in those days, and
felt as wretched as though I were dead to the
world, which I practically was. Thanks tci
Clements Tonic, though, I quickly had some life
and energy infused into me, and felt that I wanted
to live and enjoy the world’s blessings, like any
other person —the reverse of what I had been be-
fore using Clements Tonic, which braced my
nerves and invigorated the system throughout, in
a manner that astonished all who had observed
my frail condition a little while previously.”
“Have you had a return of any of your old
troubles, Mr. Martin ? ”
“I can safely say that since Clements Tonic
completed my cure at the time I speak of, no
body could wish for better health than I have en-
joyed, which shows that there was no patchwork
about that wonderful remedy’s way of doing
things. If I hear a person say a headache is at
work, I recommend Clements Tonic, because it
cured mine ; should the complaint be about giddi-
ness, flatulence, kidney troubles or drowsiness, or
discomfort after eating, I tell them to use Clements
Tonic, as it cured me of those ills, and the proof
of the pudding is in the eating thereof.”
“So we can publish what you say?”
“My permission is yours—in any way you like
to conceive.”

VARIOVS VERSE
The Imported Servant.
The blue sky arches o’er mountain and valley,
The scene is as fair as a scene can be;
But.l’m breaking my heart for a London alley,
And fogs that shall never come back to me.
I choke with tears when the day is dying;
The sunset’s grand, and the stars are bright—
But its Oh ! for the smell of the fried-fish frying
By the flaring stalls on a Saturday night!
And this, Oh ! this is the lonely sequel
Of all I pictured would come to pass !
They are treating me here as a friend and equal,
But they’d say in London that they’re “no
class.”
At the thought of their kindness my tears flow
faster;
The girls are free, and the chaps are grand ;
It’s “the boss” and “the missus” for mistress
and master,
And they may be right—but I don’t understand.
I see the air in its warm pulsation
On sandstone cliffs where the ocean dips;
But I’m miles and miles from the railway station,
Where trains run down to the wharves and
ships.
Those streets are dingy and dark,and narrow,
. The soot comes down with the rain and sleet;
But it’s Oh ! for the sight of a coster’s barrow
And Sunday morning in Chapel-street!
N.S.W.
HENGY LAWSON.
The Lay of Lost Ideals.
Forasmuch as I have drunken of the bitter dreg
of life ;
Forasmuch as I have suffered for a cause ;
O, Lord of Lost Ideals, put a term upon the strife,
For your creature is a thing of countless flaws.
It was infinite, the promise, when the battle was
begun ;
But with conquest came the vision of the slain,
And we tried to hide their faces from the staring
of the sun,
And we dug their graves and buried them in
vain.
It was infinite, the failure, when the battle reached
its close ;
For the ones we strove to succor all were dead.
They were dead and very peaceful—just as peace-
ful as their foes—
And the silent fields were echoing our tread.
I, a soldier in the army that would right the
ancient wrongs ;
. I, who sought to lift the burden from the slave ;
Who went forth to tilt with evils, crying rousing
battle-songs,
Hear the voices of the vanquished from the
grave !
Yea, I slew an evildoer, and his children wailed
for bread.
Yea, I put a bloody tyrant to the knife,
And his helpless serfs were ousted from their
meagre board and bed,
And I heard the anguished wailing of his wife.
O, with fiery tongue and eloquent I lead a hungry
band
To the oasis of justice and content ;
But the way was long for many, and their bones
bestrewed the sand ; '
And the remnant reached it never, being spent.
Now, O Lord of LostTdeals, I would fain lie down
* and sleep;
Such a sleep as knows no gnawings of despair.
Let me slumber with my fellows in a coma, calm
and deep,
In the camp of all the Men Who Do Not Care !
VICTORIA.
G. G. A. COLLES.
Rosette.
You knew each dainty art and grace
Desire to whet ;
No lure of silk or scented lace
You’d e’er forget.
When to the old, familiar chase,
Equipped, you set—
A demon with a woman’s face,
My pale Rosette.
You wept upon my heart your love ;
With tender clasp
You played the pretty wounded dove,
And hid the asp.
I grant that, over and above,
No lover saw,
Beneath your faintly-perfumed glove.
The waiting claw.
But not in Passion only lay
Your witch’s lure :
Far-hidden from the light of day
In paths obscure—
As waits the were-wolf for a prey—
Your soul impure
Cajoled a victim on the way
Of anguish sure.
A path of pain and penitence,
And flint and thorn,
His flagging feet have trodden since,
With step forlorn ;
And Honor, Truth and Innocence
Betrayed, foresworn,
Like ravished virgins, bid him thence
With eyes of scorn.
Oft-times he marvels in his shame
And black regret
From what dread depth thy spirit c urie ;
What Master yet,
Fresh-risen from the Nether Flame,
To claim a debt,
In-that dark-hour shall also claim’ *"
Your Soul, Rosette.
VICTORIA.
E. J. BRADY.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed, to the Editor,
but to the Manager. 'To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin.” is the proper
superscription for “copy.” Delay, and even loss, is
soinetimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there ; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be ref used.
The contents of this paper are. copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right, to reproduce or translate any part, of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor ivill carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short 'Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
twe columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
Tins Bulletin ivill return all unsuitable MS. ivhen the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But no
MS. ivill be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. 'is forwarded, without stamps
for return it 'will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 9, 1909.
MELBOURNE’S “WILD AND DESERTED”
BACK-DOOR.
Tait’s train fell through a bridge the other day,
itnd it was only by special good fortune that no
lives were lost. Tait is the Canadian railway
manager who was imported to show the Vic-
torians how to run their lines. He had his train-
ing in a country where the railway slaughter is
about the worst —if not the very worst —on earth,
and quite a rush of railway burials have attended
his footsteps in the land to which he came. One
of the reasons assigned for the railway slaughter
of Canada and the U.S. is the building of cheap
bridges, with very little margin of carrying power,
and the bridge which doubled up beneath the
Maryborough train is said to have been built on
Canadian principles. But these are side issues.
The accident took place at McCallum’s Crossing,
near Maryborough, and the scene is thus described
by the Age :
The locality is wild and deserted, there being no houses
within two or three miles of the Crossing.
That statement is an absolutely true bill. McCal-
lum’s Crossing lies in the fertile county of Talbot,
not far from Melbourne. Talbot used to be well-
peopled, but it isn’t so now. The lack of a
graduated land-tax or any other precaution against
the buying up of small farms to make big estates,
the disease of earth-hunger, the growth and mul-
tiplication of the earth-hog, the curse of the House
<>f Rich Landlords, the curse of Bent and Alfred
Deakin have all fallen heavily on Talbot. The
McCallum’s Creek breakage happened in the
middle of a huge estate which was built up by
long persistent swallowing of farms that were
once clean and well-cultivated and well-inhabited.
Now the unwieldy property grows thistles in
plenty, briars with frequency, and rabbits in
abundance, and it has produced in the last 40
vears or so a certain quantity of wool and three
•Jiildren. It isn’t much in the way of children
or an estate which is so large that, when you.
are in the centre of it there are no houses within
two or three miles. The most adjacent dwellings
outside the property are those of two farmers who
have reared big families, one on 123 acres, the
other on less, fighting the while a desperate battle
with the thistles and rabbits' that pour in on them
from the great untaxed holding alongside them.
Probably they too will be swallowed up in time,
for the swallowing progress is very continuous.
* * * *
It is difficult to say exactly when the big land-
grabber first began to exterminate the population
of this fertile county which lies at Melbourne’s
back door, but here is part of the record :
Population op County of Talbot.
IS7I .. 84,762 I 1891 .. 59,088
1881 .. 67,963 | 1901 ... 57,398
It is a dreary, horrible record, especially for a
county which is intersected by four separate
railways, and which has had railway communica-
tion for 35 years ; but there are so many records
just like it in land-monopoly-cursed Victoria that
it excites little surprise. By reason of the growth
of huge estates there is little for a young Vic-
torian to do except go to Melbourne and turn
artisan, or else get out of the State and seek for a
farm in some place where the land-stealing dis-
ease isn’t yet quite so bad. The following
official estimates of population show how the
curse of the big estates is working out to-day,
despite the pretence of the Bent Government
that land-jobbing was presently to make the
desert blossom like the rose

It is a miserably diseased condition of affairs
when nearly three-fourths of the new population
which Victoria has acquired in the past seven
years has gone to bloat one huge city. It only
requires another 40 years like the last seven,
and Melbourne will stand for more than half
the population of the State. And the pro-
cess is growing worse, despite the hypo-
critical pretence of Bent and the Argus that
great things are being done in the way of
land settlement, and the hilarious lie of the Tory
paper about how the big squatters are meeting
the demand for cultivable earth. Last year, when
Melbourne added to its population 4437 more
people than all the rest of Victoria put together,
showed the bloating disease in a more aggravated
form than in 1907 or in 1906. Of course, land
monopoly isn’t wholly responsible for the enor-
mous dropsy of either Melbourne or Sydney ;
part of the trouble is due to a nefarious system
which makes these cities the only great railway
centres of their respective States, which gives
special rates in order to drag all the country’s
trade there, and which concentrates there all the
machinery of government. But land monopoly is
very largely responsible in Melbourne’s lament-
able case. The farmer has been steadily shoved
for years further and further back, until now
he has reached the dry and treacherous
Mallee and such-like places. And things are
so bad in his present haunts that it only
requires one bad season like that of 1902, and for
the three succeeding years the whole rural
population, and for the two succeeding
years the aggregate population of the small
towns, is on the down grade. Then, with
the advent of better seasons, the farmers take
heart and begin to crawl back to their arid
stronghold in the Mallee ; but when it is noticed
that the increase of rural population was 1148 less
in 1908 than in 1907, it seems as if another exodus
was coming in sight.
So it is no wonder that when Tait’s train, run-
ning not far from Melbourne, was ditched that
wet night, it found itself sitting among its own
ruins in a locality that is “wild and deserted,
there being no houses within two or three miles.”
Does Alfred Deakin propose to do anything
about it? And are John Weary’s utterly in-
adequate progressive land-tax proposals even
intended to do anything except pour a little oil
on the clamorous sea of protest ?
THE FINDING OF THE NORTH POLE.
The North Pole has at last been found, or at least
an assertion is made to that effect. The alleged
finder is a Yankee explorer named Cook—
the name used to be Koch —from Brooklyn,
where Preacher Talmage and Rev. Henry Ward
Beecher (the man who “sat on the rugged
edge of despair”) hung out. Two years or
more ago Cook, with a mate, sundry Eskimo,
and certain dogs and provender, vanished from
human ken into the top of Greenland. The mate
came back, suffering from cold feet; but Cook,
who used to be Koch, has just emerged with his
story. The dogs can’t corroborate it. The Eskimo
can’t corroborate it either, for Eskimo don’t take
observations with the sextant, and probably don’t
know that there is a Pole. A photograph of the
stars, taken exactly at the Pole, would be good
evidence; but it was during the long day that
Cook says he got there, and even if it had been night
the apparatus required to take an adequate
star-photograph would weigh about three tons, and
when a man is struggling over leagues of ice
and dodging between bears he can’t carry much
apparatus. Doubtless if the uncorroborated Cook
really got to the Pole he built the usual cairn and
left the usual record. If the next successful
traveller finds these things, and is an honest
traveller, he will come back—unless he freezes or
becomes a bear’s dinner on the road—and say
that he found them, and thus clear away any sus-
picion that may hang round the tale of the
uncorroborated Cook. On the other hand, if he
is a liar he will make a large hole and bury Cook’s
records, and declare that there was no sign or
footprint of Cook, and claim that he was himself
the first finder of the Pole. And, again, he may
come back and say quite truly that there was no
sign of Cook, yet Cook may have been there ;
bears may have dispersed his cairn, and eaten or
obliterated his evidence, and stamped with their
big feet or sat down with their obscene hind-
quarters on the honors which were rightly his.
Or the records may have departed on the moving
ice. Cook says that the ice at the Pole is moving
eastward, though it is hard to see how that can be
since there is no east at the North Pole, and no
west and no north—only south. Thus Cook may
become another Bacon-Shakspeare controversy.
Of course, there is no reason why Cook, even
though he comes from Brooklyn, should not have
found the long-sought-for Pole. It is one of the
peculiarities of life that hordes of notable men
struggle for centuries to achieve the apparently
impossible, and then someone named Smith or
Jones goes out and suddenly does it. In this
respect, the fact that the explorer’s name was
Cook is distinctly in his favor—barring the fact
that his name is really Koch. It is also in his
favor that both Poles are now about due, for
recent expeditions have got nearer and nearer —in
fact, they got so near that unless the Poles
packed up their portmanteaux and fled to the
interior of Africa it was hardly possible for them
to hide themselves much longer. And it should
also be noted to Cook’s credit that he is of Bel-
gian extraction. This seems to be the day-out
for the so-called Latin races. It was an Italian
who invented wireless telegraphy, and it was
another Italian who got nearest to the Pole previous
to Cook’s exploit. Also an Italian would have
won the first English Marathon race if he
hadn’t fallen down. A Frenchman has just shown
that the invasion of England is possible via the
upper air, by flying over the English Channel.
Seeing that a Spaniard or a Portuguese
never does anything remarkable, it was up to
a Belgian to do something; and Cook has
either done it, or has said »he did it. The
public had just assimilated the idea that
Cook was another evidence that the Latin races
were having their day out when a statement
arrived that Cook (formerly Koch) isn’t of Belgian
descent at all, but is a German Hebrew of a
Frankfort family ; but that doesn’t really make
much difference. As no Jew has yet found a
Pole, it is plainly up to a Jew to find one now.
Yet, whatever Cook’s origin, it is a grievous
handicap to come back from such an ex-
pedition without any reliable corroboration. The
explorer who returns alone is in a position to say
he has been anywhere even if he hasn’t; and if he
is sufficiently learned and intelligent he leaves no
holes in his narrative through which lie can be
bowled out. Of course, if he is a mere lout like
the sea-captain in Besant’s tale “ The Captains’
Room,” he falls in. That person professed to go
to New Guinea to hunt up the heroine’s lost sweet-
heart. He said that he crossed a desert covered
with ostriches and giraffes. Next he climbed over
a mountain 25,000 ft. high, the same being infested
with bears, wolves and pemmican. From the top
he distinctly saw Hong Kong in the distance.
Finally he reached the court of the King of New
Guinea, a young man who wore his golden crown
regularly, and there he learned that the missing
man had just been eaten by a lion, in consequence
of which the Princess Royal, who was much
attached to the victim, had “gone off her royal
chump.” In proof of these statements he tendered
a cap, several sizes too small and so new that the
shopman’s ticket was still in it, which cap was
supposed to have been torn from the jaws of the
New Guinea lion after the wearer was eaten ; and
on the strength of these statements he offered
himself as a husband in place of the missing man
—the girl being a desirable person with money.
But it is only an explorer of fiction or a Loois DE
Rocgemont who comes back with so thin a
narrative as that.
In this matter of the Pole the English race
has a right to be aggrieved. It has been strug-
gling round the Far North ever since the days of
Martin Frobisher, or even earlier. It never had
much luck in the North, but when Shackleton
the other day made his famous dash for the South
Pole he got nearer to it than any traveller on
record had ever got to any Pole before. He failed
because a Siberian pony which it was proposed to
use as food on the land journey fell down a
crevasse and was lost, and a foolish sentiment
kept the party from eating the baronet who
accompanied the expedition. Yet why the baronet
should not have been eaten it is hard to say. His
kind are plentiful enough, and one can easily be
replaced. A man is the work of God, but a
baronet, like a peer, is “created” by the King.
A really far-seeing Polar expedition would take the
King along with it, and when provisions ran short
the royal personage would “create” a pair of
baronets, and they would serve as provisions. If
the party got tired of eating baronets the royal
explorer could “create” a duke by way of a
change of diet. The creative powers of a monarch
seem to be practically unlimited.
It may yet be necessary to sink a large
number of holes to decide who owns the North
Pole. If these holes reveal land under the ice all
the way from Greenland to the Pole, then Den-
mark, as the possessor of Greenland, has a title to
the property. If the Pole is an isolated institu-
tion, then it belongs to whoever can get it. The
U.S. Attorney-General already says that the
U.S. Constitution follows the flag to the Farthest
North; but as it is impossible to run a corner in
wheat or to lynch a nigger up there, it is difficult
to follow the Attorney-General’s process of reason-
ing. Most probably the question of owner-
ship won’t be fully settled until the earth’s climate
undergoes a great change, which is a thing that
may happen any day. There is reason to believe
that even 900 years ago the climate of the old
republic of Greenland was very much warmer than
it is now. At all events there were churches and
small towns and an alleged cathedral and even
bishops in Greenland, until the Black Death
broke out among the colonists, and the Skraelmgs
came down from the north—apparently from the
Pole—and raised Cain. (Other authorities say
the Skraelings made their attack from the south.)
In those days, according to the northern propo-
sition, the Pole was accessible by any vigorous
pedestrian. Consequently, the theory has arisen
that both Poles were comfortable places until
lately, but that ice is now steadily accumulating,
and that this heaping up of dead weight may end
by destroying the balance of the globe. If that
happens the globe will suddenly do a half-turn
and revolve on a new axis, and inside a crowded
half-hour or so the Poles will become the Equator,
and the Equator will be the Poles. It is calculated
that the shock will tear the seas out of their
beds in so alarming a fashion that everybody
will be drowned save the people up in the Andes
and the Himalayas. Then the ice at the Poles
will naturally melt, and when the dead weight is
thus removed the earth will whirl round again to
its old basis with a renewal of the marine disturb-
ance. About this time the Poles will be perfectly
accessible, but probably the few survivors of the
human race will be too busy prodding in the mud
for lost property to take advantage of the oppor-
tunity. Whether the catastrophe could be averted
by sending great expeditions to attack the Polar
ice with burning glasses, and thus shift the super-
incumbent mass, is an open question. If the
burning glass was big enough and numerous
enough it could shift all the icebergs on fiarth,
always provided the half-melted icebergs didn't
fall on the machinery and make a ruin of it.
Possibly when the manufacture of optical instru-
ments is further advanced there will be an excel-
lent wheat crop at the Poles, and the hay in
Greenland will be something above the average.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Starving the Watchman.
One of the reasons why the Commonwealth is
being.bled white by the States is that, when the
States have a business out of which they might
reasonably make a bit of money, the revenue from
that source is cut and chopped until it barely
meets the expenditure. The N.S.W railways are
a case in point. Though they still have a big
accumulated deficit to wipe out, yet on the
strength of two or three good seasons, the imported
railway potentate and the Wade Government
between them are cutting down rates with the
deliberate intention of preventing any future
surplus worth mentioning. This would be
all very well if the State hadn’t borrowed for
anything except rail ways and other more or less
genuinely permanent and reproductive works;
but as it has borrowed for a host of other things,
then clearly one of two courses has to be followed :
either the interest on the things that won’t pay
for themselves has to be paid by the things that
are profitable, or it has to be provided out of
taxes. Hitherto the yarn has been that, taken in
the lump, the N.S.W. loans are self-supporting ;
but they can only be rendered self-supporting
by making the profitable things pay for the
unprofitable. Here, for instance, area few items
of total loan expenditure taken from the N.S.W.
Auditor-General’s last report:—
Court-houses, gaols, etc £1,022,106
Museums, schools, etc. .. .. 1,114,103
Hospitals and asylums .. .. 749,056
Roads, bridges and punts .. .. 1,801,497
Immigration 569,930
Fortifications 1,457,536
These are only six items out of dozens which don't
produce enough revenue to pay for paint; and
yet this small bundle amounts in the aggregate to
£7,014,228, on which the annual interest bill is
over £290,000. If the revenue from the few things
which do pay is to be cut down until they only pay
for themselves even in good years, then for a start
this £259,000 must inevitably be made up
out of taxation; and that, of course, means
that N.S.W. must throw over once and
for all the ancient gag that it has only
borrowed for permanent and reproductive under-
takings—they obviously will not be “reproduct-
ive ” if a million or so of the interest has to be got
directly out of the taxpayers’ pockets. The State
never should have borrowed for any other class of
undertakings ; but as it has done so, it must put
up with the consequences. And one of the conse-
quences is that either it must make the good help
to pay for the bad, or the people must be prepared
to tax themselves. If, however, the State won’t
do what any business man would do, and make the
good help to pay for the bad, it is a monstrous
thing that the Commonwealth should be called
upon to suffer. If a merchant buys some goods
so badly that they presently land him in a
loss, and if he buys some other things
at a price at which he can make a handsome
profit, he makes the profit and wipes out the loss.
The idea of Johnson, the imported railway
potentate, and also of the vote-catching Wade
Government, is to cut down the selling price of
the goods which have been well bought, so that
they shall not show any profit either. Then,
being left with no profit on one lot of goods and a
loss on the other lot, the idea is to go to the land-
lord and say “ I can’t pay you your rent,” and to
the watchman and say—“l’m hard up, so I’ll
have to starve you.” If a business man proceeded
on those lines he would be regarded as either a
rogue or a fool; and yet these are exactly the lines
followed by the N. S. Wales Government in par-
ticular, and all the other State Governments to
some degree.

The Same Delusion.
State Premiers and such-like are not the only
people who fancy they have a right to abolish the
popular vote and prevent the people governing
themselves as they please. Suburban mayors
suffer from the same complaint. The Greater
Sydney movement, which is now in very consider-
able blast, is bitterly opposed by almost every
suburban mayor, and by almost all the suburban
aldermen who either have been or hope to be
mayors. This is not remarkable, for if Sydney
has one municipal council instead of 41, it is plain
that 40 mayors will lose their jobs. The average
mayor, in the fury of his opposition, seems to
have a mistaken idea of his importance in
the scheme of things. What the reformers
propose is that a municipal constitution for
Greater Sydney should be drafted—by a popu-
larly-elected convention, or by Parliament, or by
some authority or another—and then submitted
to the people for acceptance or rejection, that sort
of direct vote being the only way in which the will
of the people can be really ascertained. The
view of the infuriated horde of suburban mayors
and aldermen is that nothing of the kind should
be permitted. They see no reason why the people
should have a Greater Sydney, whether they
want it or not, seeing that suburban mayors don’t
want it. They can’t understand what right the
people have to even express their views on
the matter seeing that the suburban aider-
men don’t want them to express their
views. In this respect they stand alongside
Gregory Wade, who reckons that even if
the people of Australia want Federal control of
industrial legislation or a referendum to decide
the site of the Australian capital it would
be an outrage if they got it, seeing that
he (Wade) doesn’t want them to have it.
Both parties say the change is not according
to the Constitution, which is true enough;
and both Wade and the suburban mayors
hold that the people shouldn’t be allowed
to amend their own Constitution unless Wade
and the mayors are agreeable, which is sheer
folly. Wade stands for State rights. The subur-
ban mayors stand for back-lane rights. They
have exactly the same delusion, but Wade looks
rather kindly on the Greater Sydney idea, and
probably can’t see that anybody has a right to
have his delusion but. himself.


A Scheme to Mix Oil and Water,
“ D.” writes
Defence Minister Coos said this, among a number of
other ill-judged things, last week
“ We shall need a naval training-school of first-class
quality, in which our naval cadets may be trained to the
same standard as those at home. We shall thus be able
to open, to our young men a career in the navy. And by
the navy I mean the entire Umpire navy. One of the
fundamental principles of this education will be that it
shall fit those undergoing l it for any position anywhere
where the Union Jack flies ; the boats, the weapons, the
men should be interchangeable throughout.”
The idea of a local naval training-school for the instructing
of Australian naval cadets in what is going to be the busi-
ness of their lives is practicable. The idea that cadets so
trained are going to be interchanged satisfactorily with
cadets trained according to Cold Land traditions isn’t.
Pei pend while I state facts which will appear as
hoary platitudes to those with knowledge of life
on a British man-o'-war. Than the British naval
officer there is no more conservative human, in
matters of social conduct, on earth. He is
caught while a mere infant, and run into the R. N.
College, the modern substitute for the old Britannia.
This is an English public school—only considerably more
so. F rom the time he gets up till the time he goes to bed
the embryo sailor is hedged round with a set of com-
and, to the Australian boy, wholly purposeless
conventions. There are some to whom he may not
speak under pain of assault, unless he has first
been addressed by them ; others whom he may
accost only in set terms and manner. There are
articles of clothing which he may not wear,
forms of speech he may not employ, subjects (harmless in
themselves) which he may not discuss—in a thousand
ways he must go warily till he acquires the precise atti-
tude towards life and his fellow-tar that is demanded of
him by the service at large. When that time comes he is
—unless one of amazing individuality—indistinguishable
in almost every important respect from his colleagues the
seas over. He may drink or smoke a little more or a
little less than the next man, or have less or more cash,
or be keen on cricket instead of cards, or prefer racing to
social gaiety ; but, broadly, he is according to pattern.
His manner, his way of speech, his habits, his ideas, his
lines of thought, his prejudices are identical with those of
his companions who have been tutored in the same school
he has.
Consider the position of an Australian-born and edu-
cated cadet in that environment. He may be a good,
earnest, well-behaved and able lad. He may be com-
panionable and tactful, and hence popular among his
own friends. Yet he will find nothing in common with
British young men in his walk of life, and they will find
nothing in common with him. Each is bound to mis-
judge and dislike the other. The trying association of
shipboard life demands that resemblance of type which
the British naval training system gives, since without it
harmony is impossible. The Australian of the present
generation has already got far enough away from the
Briton to have evolved a manner, a voice, views, customs,
a slang and a hundred other characteristics of his own.
He is as alien from the Briton as both he and the Briton
are from the American—and how wide that last gap was
became plain to anyone who watched the commissioned
boy of the Bulldog breed striving hard to get on terms
with the U.S.A. officers here last year, and failing miser-
ably every time.
It may be argued that the R.N.R. man whose appren-
ticeship is served, not on the banks of the Dart, but aboard
a deep-sea vessel, doesn’t find himself at a disadvantage
among regularly-trained British naval officers, and that,
therefore, there is no need to fear Australians are going
to be. I hold that he is ; and go so far as to say that
whether he comes from a P. and O. ship or from one of
the less aristocratic of the merchantmen, he remains
always, among 90 percent, of “ pukka” naval men whom
he meets, an outsider—not to say a pariah—to the end.
It is impossible to dispute the' seriousness of this ten-
dency in its bearings on the Deakin-Cook idea of inter-
changing British with Australian officers throughout the
Imperial navy. In Maoriland last month a youngster
deserted his ship and deliberately faced expulsion from
the service to escape what he described as “ the hell of
the life on board.” He had nothing against his fellow-
officers. They had nothing against him. Simply he
wasn’t their sort. A lad trained at the R. N. College
would have found a Yankee battleship the same intoler-
able place. An Australian-trained youth would so regard
a British warship. If there is one aspiration more hope-
less than another in the Deakin-Cook naval programme,
it is this of mixing for long periods of British and Australian
human elements in the confined space aboard ship. The
dictum that officers, whether naval or military, of dif-
ferent nationalities, tastes and points of view can’t frater-
nise in the real sense for any length of time is as true and
as old as Gospel.

Boots, Hobbles and Other Things.
Customs Minister Best, who is as good a Protec-
tionist as a Fused politician may be, lately met
certain foolish boot manufacturers, who asked him
to alter a clause in the Amending Patents Bill so
that American manufacturers of hired-out patented
boot machinery might not be affected by it.
These foolish manufacturers are not even allowed
the freedom of contract they profess to love;
they can only hire the machines necessary for
their business from the United States Boot do. or
its offshoot, the British United Co,, both of them
belonging to a combine of the most restrictive
sort; they must also contract to use no machinery
made by any ©.■ npany or person outside the com-
bine ; and thu.-, they are practically under the
thumb of the combine. But because they had not
so far suffered under the arrangement—though
even that they can’t really know for certain—
they urgently desired things left as they are.
Best’s Bill proposes to borrow the provision
already in force in England, under which a patent
may be revoked unless within four years of
its being issued the patentee is manufacturing
the article in Australia ; and it was this pro-
vision in particular that they wanted the
Government to throw overboard. Best, however,
viewed therequestcoldly, remarking that no patent
would be revoked if satisfactory reasons were
given why the article could or should not be made
here; also he pointed out that if, as asserted,
these machines could not be made here, then the
revocation of the patent could do no harm to any-
body. In fact, if the machinery couldn’t be made
here, he didn’t know what the makers wanted a
patent for at all. There is a dim suspicion that these
established boot manufacturers have an arrange-
ment with the machinery co. by which competition
is stifled ; if not, why their excessive eagerness to
smooth the path for the foreign monopolists?
But whether that be so or not it is to be hoped
Best and the Fused Ministry will continue
their hostile attitude to the suave and gentle
monopolists. This new Patent law doesn’t
concern itself merely with boots and the hiring
of foreign machinery; it is chiefly intended
to develop the manufacture of intricate, up-
to-date machinery in the Commonwealth;
to train, or to encourage the immigration of
clever, scientific artisans; and to give the Aus-
tralian inventor a chance to apply his brains to im-
prove even the choicest work of the brainiest
American mechanics and engineers. The Aus-
tralian boot-manufacturer may suffer temporarily,
though even that is unlikely ; but he will certainly
profit in the long run, while the Australian con-
sumer and artisan will gain in every way. Mean-
while, this unusual attitude of Private Enterprise,
in jumping on freedom of contract and asking to
be allowed to keep its neck under the heel of the
monopolist, requires a good deal of explanation,
especially as the monopolist himself has so far
said nothing.
About a Potential Commissioner.
Chaffering, lobbying and underground engineer-
ing proceed apace in Melbourne in regard to the
forthcoming appointment by the Fused Govern-
ment of an Australian High Commissioner. There
is a dark dread abroad that the job may be
brought to the level of vaudeville by the granting
of it to G. Reid. Indeed, if the Fusees were sure
they could hold the rickety East Sydney seat with
any other candidate than Reid, there is little
doubt that they would hand him the office right
away. The fact that they aren’t sure puts
John Forrest well in the running for a
billet after which his soul has hungered
in frank and public fashion for years past.
And what an appalling possibility that is!
Reid, with all his disabilities—his grotesqueries,
his incurable habit of clowning, his recently-
acquired and deplorable passion for reeling off
scale moral and philosophic platitudes—has cer-
tain more or less admirable qualities. For ex-
ample, his mentality along certain lines com-
mands respect. There is reason to think that his
Toryism is to an extent bogus ; and that he takes
a broader view-point politically and socially than
he is forced, for party purposes, to seem to do.
Not so Forrest, who is a foolish and blundering
old man these times; and an unashamed reaction-
ary-. He never had any mental grip worth men-
tioning. Barring his unshakable resolve to hold
office, there outstands little definite in him nowa-
days save his wild dread of the exploded menace,
Socialism—and his money. These are the assets
which his friends count upon to tip the Commis-
sionership scale in his favor. An incident which
occurred in the Australian Parliament during the
debate on the Coinage Bill suggests one of the
many good reasons why John Forrest, apart
from his quite patent disadvantages otherwise, is
a hopeless candidate for the post he seeks. The
question arose as to whether the phrase “King
and Emperor” should appear on the new Aus-
tralian coins. Members on both sides of the
House agreed enthusiastically that the idea of
stating, by implication, on our coins that Ed.
VII. is Emperor of Australia was absurd, not
to say insane. Forrest didn’t attempt to
dispute that obvious contention. But he stuck
with a doggedness that was none the less effective
for that it was largely inarticulate to the attitude
that it had to be so, till the House started to grow
suspicious and to probe for reasons. Then it got
them. The Colonial Office, it appeared, had given
Forrest his orders in the matter. Said Crouch,
M.H.R., wildly :
The answer of Mr. Forrkst . . . places on record the
opinion of the Commonwealth Treasurer that the views of
the Colonial Office govern this Parliament.
Of course, as a matter of fact, the views of the
Colonial Office don’t; they merely govern For-
rest. In Forrest’s opinion the sun rises and sets in
the Colonial Office. It is not unnatural that
this should be so. Years ago, before Responsible
Government came to Westralia, John Forrest
was a high official there, in the employ of
the Colonial Office, which administered that
section of the earth as a Crown Colony. Then
Westralia got a Parliament and a Cabinet. And
John Forrest, because of his services in the
Crown Colony days, became a Colonial Office
pensioner at £BOO per annum. He is still in
receipt of that handsome income when he is not
in office. Is it to be wondered that he truckles
to the egregious London institution which
blunders so consistently each time it essays
to handle matters of Australian concern?
Furthermore, is it to be expected that the institu-
tion which controls Forrkst in the capacity of
Cabinet Minister from a distance of 12,000 miles
won’t control him with a far firmer grip if he is
High Commissioner within a shilling cab-drive of
Downing-street ? The appointment of Forrest,
the old Crown Colony official, to this supremely
important post is almost tantamount to appointing
one of the permanent Colonial Office officials, and
that is about as wild a travesty of an appoint-
ment as is conceivable.

Progressive Land-Tax in Victoria.
Land monopoly has made such an utter wreck of
the small towns and agricultural districts of Vic-
toria that the Murray Government has felt called
upon to do something, and a Progressive Land-
Tax Bill has been introduced. It is a somewhat
gradual measure which is to come into force, if it
passes, about 16 months hence, and its principal
features are these :• —
The tax is to be as follows :
When the total unimproved value of the land of any
owner is—
More than And not more than
£SOO .. .. .. £2,500 .. id. in the £
2,500 5,000 .. |d.
5,000 10,000 .. Id.
10,000 15,000 .. ljd. „
15,000 20,000 .. l|d.
20,000 25,000 .. lfd. ~
25,000 30.000 .. lid. ~
30,000 35.000 .. lfd.
35,000 40,000 .. lfd. „
40,000 45,000 .. l|d. ~
45,000 50,000 .. 2d.
50,000 60,000 .. 2fd. ' ~
60,000 70,000 .. 2|d.
70,000 80,000 .. 2§d.
80,000 ..3d.
Absentee landlords to pay 50 per cent, extra.
And then exemptions and weak points begin to
come in :• —•
Lands used for mining, religious and educational pur-
poses are exempt.
The Income-tax Commissioner may make assessments
where he thinks necessary, but apparently he may also
accept the existing assessments made for Local Govern-
ment purposes, which often run to about a quarter of the
real value. And he can’t make a fresh assessment more
than once in two years.
The landowner has two separate appeals against the
assessment.
In case of fire, flood, drought, etc., where it seems that
the tax would be a serious hardship, its payment may be
remitted or postponed. This is a vast and dangerous
power to place in the hands of any official.
There are certain remissions of income-tax which will
in many cases largely counter-balance the new land-tax.
The tax is supposed to yield £318,000 a year, but out of
this the Murray Government is pledged to return £50,000
to the country landoumers in reduced railway charges.
The keynote of the whole business is the desire to
be “moderate,” and in such a matter moderation
is an absurdity. Victoria is driven to the con-
clusion that it trust cut up its huge empty estates
and make room for population, or else decay into
complete unimportance. Therefore it is proposed
that the estate worth £200,000 shall pay 3d. in the
£. Assuming that the valuation is correct, and
that the income derived from the land is 5 per
cent. (Is. in the £), this tax is one-fourth of the
income. Why should it not be half the income,
or even more ? If the monopolist cuts up his huge
estate and sells it for purposes of settlement he
won’t pay anything, and if a man pays nothing it
doesn’t matter to him whether the amount he
doesn’t pay on the land he has sold equals
25 per cent, or 100 per cent, on the rental
he would have received if he hadn’t sold
the land. When a man pays nothing the
fact that under totally different circumstances he
might have had to pay a larger or smaller amount is
of no importance to him, and the whole purpose
of a progressive tax should be to bring about a
state of things under which the present big land-
lord won’t pay anything. Therefore moderation,
which aims at reaching a point at which the
monopolist won’t really be driven to sell, but will
only be made angry and cantankerous, is quite out
of place. The Bulletin fully recognises that the
Murray Government’s Progressive Land-Tax Bill
is the best thing of its kind that Victoria has yet
seen. All the same, it propounds these theories :
(1) If revenue is wanted it should be obtained by a land-
tax which will apply to- large and small estates equally—
which can’t be evaded by cutting up the property.
(2) Apart from this there should be a progressive land-
tax which isn’t calculated to yield any revenue at all
(3) The exemption should be larger than £soo—neriiaus
£IOOO. Remaps
(4) After that the graduated tax should begin at 2d. in
the & and mount up rapidly to the whole shilling—in
other words, to the whole rental value of the land.
To put it another way : If £750 or £1000 worth of
land is a living area for a man with a wife and the
sort of family he should have—for a man who
doesn’t live on the sweated labor of his children,
on the underpaid labor of his hirelings, and by
harassing the Treasury for seed wheat, road and
bridge grants and absurdly low railway charges
—then the reward for restricting himself to a
living are.r should be complete exemption from
a tax which aims solely at the cutting up of land.
And as £20,000 worth of land is a monopoly
and a public wrong, its reward should be such a
tax as wdl make the cutting up of the property
not merely probable, but as certain as death or
sunrise. The assumption that the proposed tax-
will yield £318,OOO a year as a steady thing merely
signifies that it is supposed to be that much short
of the sort of tax it should be. Yet, with all this,
it a great advance on anything yet attempted
in Victoria, and deserves commendation accord-
ingly. The only pity is that it doesn’t deserve
even more commendation.
Apprenticeship versus Technical College.
A number of master tailors, holding forth last
week at the N.8.W. Industrial Court, com-
plained that the operatives of to-day are
not in the same street as those of 10 years
ago. Judge Heydon got interested, and
wanted to know why. One employer said
the introduction of machinery had much to
do with it; but, in the end, he and the rest were
fain to admit that the piecework system, under
which the men have to pay and supervise the
apprentices, is the real cause. When the em-
ployers paid wages and supplied “improvers,”
the tailors taught their apprentices carefully, for
they wanted the, best work out of them. But
where the men are paid on the quantity of work
done they hustle all the time, and if they stop to
instruct the apprentices it is their loss. They
prefer to get what work they can out of the boys
with a few perfunctory directions. The result is
that the work is bad, but still it isn’t worse than
the average, and above all it is swift. Exactly
why the men should be expected to prepare
the rising crop of operatives for the bene-
fit of the employer is not clear. From
whatever standpoint it is looked at the appren-
tice system is a very unsatisfactory one. It
was originally designed to keep down wages. A
youth became an apprentice and was bound,
mostly for seven years. During that time, no
matter how efficient he became, he had to work
for the wage of a learner, which varied from
nothing to kicks per month. Usually it worked
out at about 41 years of skilled labor for a boy’s
wages ; and naturally the boss loved it. It helped
to make England great, and in the days when
workshops and factories were small, and most
things were done by hand, and one man made a
boot or a suit of clothes from beginning to end,
and the apprentice consequently had a chance to
learn the whole trade, it answered well enough.
But that such a system should still linger in these
days of the subdivision of labor is an amazing
anomaly. The proper place to learn trades and
crafts is the Technical College. There the pupil
receives instruction in every branch of his
trade. That is how they do things in
Germany, and when the German youth has
passed through three or four years of this training
he is subjected to a test. The work he turns out
is branded with his name and number, and kept
at the College for reference for a number of years;
after which it is handed to him. He receives a
certificate of merit setting forth what he has
learned and what he has done under test
conditions. Armed with this he goes into
a factory and starts at a journeyman’s wage
from the first day of liis industrial life. By
this system both masters and journeymen escape
the task of preparing the rising generation of
operatives, for the apprentices are instructed in
their crafts by men trained to impart knowledge!
In the workshop the apprentice learns only the
things that are done in that shop. In the
Technical College, as organised in Germany, he is
taught everything that in any way bears' on his
“Here Died.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Theru’s many a school-boy’s bat and ball that are
gathering dust at home,
For he hears a voice in the future call, and he
trains for the war to come ;
A serious light in his eyes is seen as he comes from
the schoolhouse gate ;
He keeps his kit and his rifle clean, and he sees
that his back is straight.
But straight or crooked, or round, or lame—you
may let these words take root;
As the time draws near for the sterner game, all
boys should learn to shoot.
From tiie beardless youth to the grim grey-beard,
let Australians ne’er forget,
A lame limb never has interfered with a brave
man’s shooting yet.
Over and over and over again, to you and our
friends and me,
The warning of danger has sounded plain—like
the thud of a gun at sea.
The rich man turns to his wine once more, and
the gay to their worldly joys,
The “ statesman ” laughs at a hint of war— but
something has told the boys.
The school-boy scouts of the White Man’s Land
are out on the hills to-day ;
They trace the tracks from the sea-beach sand
and sea-cliffs grim and grey ;
They take the range for a likely shot by every
cape and head,
And they spy the lay of each' lonely spot where an
enemy’s foot might tread.
In the cooling breeze of the coastal streams, or out
where the townships bake,
They march in fancy, and fight in dreams, and die
for Australia’s sake.
They hold the fort till relief arrives, when the
landing parties storm,
And they take the pride of their fresh young
lives in the set of a uniform.
Where never a loaded shell was hurled, nor a rifle
tired to kill,
The school-boy scouts of the Southern World are
choosing their Battery Hill.
They run the tapes on the flats and fells by roads
that the guns might sweep,
They are fixing in memory obstacles where the
firing lines shall creep.
They read and they study the gunnery—they ask
till the meaning’s plain,
But the craft of the scout is a simple thing to the
young Australian brain.
They blaze the track for a forward run, where the
scrub is everywhere,
And they mark positions for every gun and every
unit there.
They trace the track for a quick retreat—and the
track for the other way round,
And they mark the spot in the summer heat where
the water is always found.
They note the chances of cliff and tide, and where
they can move, and when,
And every point where a gun might hide in the
days when they’ll fight as men.
When silent men with their rifles lie by many a
ferny dell ;
And turn their heads when a scout goes by, with
the cheery growl “All’s well ” ;
And scouts shall climb by the fishermen’s ways,
and watch for a sign of ships,
With stern eves fixed on the threatening haze
where the blue horizon dips.
When men shall camp in the dark and damp by
the bough-masked battery,
Between the forts and the open ports where the
miners watch the sea ;
And talk perhaps of their boy-scout days, as they
sit in their shelters rude,
While motors race to the distant bays with ammu-
nition and food.
When the city alight shall wait by night for news
from a far-out post,
And men ride down from the farming town to
patrol the lonely coast —
Till they hear the thud of a distant gun, or the
distant rifles crack,
Aud Australians spring to their arms as one to
drive the invaders back.
There’ll be no music nor martial noise, save the
guns to help you through,
For a plain and a shirt-sleeve job, my boys, is
the job that we’ll have to do.
And many of those who had learned to shoot—and
in learning learned to teach—
i’o the last three men, aud the last galoot, shall
die on some lonely beach.
But they’ll waste their breath in no empty boast,
and they’ll prove to the world their worth,
When the shearers rush to the Eastern Coast, and
the miners rush to Perth.
And the man who fights in a Queenscliff fort, or
up by Keppel B iy,
Will know that his mates at Bunbury are doing
their share that day.
There was never a land so great and wide, where
the foreign fathers came,
That has bred her children so much alike, with
their hearts so much the same.
And sons shall fight by the mangrove creeks that
lie on the lone North Coast,
Who never shall know (or not for weeks) if the
rest of Australia’s lost.
And far in the future (I see it well), and born of
such days as these,
There lies an Australia invincible, and mistress of
all her seas ;
With monuments standing on hill and head,
where her sons shall point with pride
To the names of Australia’s bravest dead, carved
, under the words “Here Died.”
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Indifferentist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am not interested in the Pole ;
I do not care a bit if it’s been found
Or stands, as it has hitherto, from bole
To top with ice and isolation crowned.
I never felt the wish to emulate
Nansen, D’Abruzzi, Peary and the rest;
I simply loathe the thought of tempting Fate
In regions where one cannot change one’s vest.
I consequently bear no jealousy
To Dr. Cook. In bringing off his coup
He’s not forestalled or got ahead of me —
The feat is one I never meant to do.
I’ve always liked to view fresh hills and plains ;
Yet are there certain limits, so I hold ;
For instance, I have ever taken pains
To shun extremes of either heat or cold.
The reason why folks journey to the Pole
Has ever baffled me ; one wildly tears
Over damp, jagged ice-floes to the goal,
’Mid unwashed Eskimo and seals and bears,
Twitched by rheumatics, racked by frost, ones
toes
Inflamed by chilblains, rancid blubber fat
The staple menu-item, cheeks and nose
Whipped by unceasing blizzards. And for
what ?
Does there gleam forth, at the excursion’s end,
A pub, a gladsome place of warmth and cheer,
Wherein the voyager may rest and spend
A pleasant week or two with food and beer ?
Does he find bars and girls, and is he buoyed
With daily sheets of moral eminence?
Not so. The Pole is utterly devoid
Of any up-to-date convenience—
That is a point on which the warmest friends
Of Polar exploration are agreed.
At Parallel Eight-eight all comfort ends—
A painful thought which makes my heart to
bleed.
No, as I said before, this feat of Cook’s
Leaves me unmoved and cold as any clam.
I neither care for him nor for his books,
Nor for the place he’s found—no, not oned —mu.
N.S.W.
