Wanted, a Federal Capital Referendum.
A small procession of members of the N.S.W.
• State Parliament went down to Canberra a few
days ago, just after the heavy rains had made
things look fairly green, and the S. M. Herald
said in its leading article of last Monday that they
were pleased with the “ wide and gently undulat-
ing plain,” and other circumstances. At the
same time the Herald gently hinted to them that
it would be a mighty bad thing if they weren’t
pleased, for the job had to be rushed through
while circumstances were favorable. As the paper
put it : “ What we all have to see to is that delays
do not arise,” etc. The State members may, or
may not, have been pleased. But as members
who mostly represent districts close to Sydney;
who are fully apprised of the advisability of having
tiie capital as close to Sydney as possible, and also
of the advisability of securing the support of the
Sydney daily papers at next election ; and as mem-
bers who are liable to be aware that the Sydney
dailies can hardly do otherwise than approve of a
capital within the immediate range of their busi-
ness and circulation, they possibly tried to look
pleased even if they weren’t. At all events, they
didn’t contradict the daily paper statement that
they were pleased. According to the Herald’s
leader of Monday, “there can be no doubt that an
ample water supply can be obtained” at Canberra.
This assurance is eminently gratifying ; but the
paper might have spent a moment in explaining
away the recent unanimous report of four N.S.W.
officials, who declared that Cotter Creek, the
boasted source of supply of the proposed Canberra
capital, can’t be relied upon to furnish water fora
city of more than 50,000 people, and that so little
is known of the other principal streams there-
abouts that it is impossible to say, without elabo-
rate investigation, what their value maybe. And
the Herald might also have explained why the
Fused Government is demanding the right ito
bring water power, at great cost, from the Snowy
River at Dalgety, nearly 100 miles away, on
account of the unreliability of the Cotter and the
other creeks in the Canberra country. These are
awkward circumstances; but in an inadvertent
sort of fashion the S. M. Herald shows a way
out. It says :
Fortunately, the claims of Canberra are so strong that
there is no need for hesitancy on either side as to the
wisdon) of the choice. ... It seems to be agreed that the
site fulfils every qualification for the home of a great city.
The matter being so absolutely clear and so free
from all suspicion of doubt, the Herald can have
no objection to the proposal that the various sug-
gested sites should be submitted to a referendum,
and that the one which the people decide upon
should be the location of the Austratian capital.
It can hardly say that the people who buy the
Herald are such an incapable crowd that they
can’t be trusted in a simple matter like this. Nor
can it say that it is an utterly disgraceful
liar which is constantly declaring that the
people want the Canberra capital, while it is so
fully aware that they don’t want it that it dare
not let them have an opportunity to freely and
directly express their views on the matter. And
it can’t shirk the question by not saying anything
without bringing suspicion on its religious and
upright proprietary. The principal reasons which
this paper advances in support of the proposed
Monaro capital are that it is in a healthy and pic-
turesque locality—so healthy and picturesque that
the N.S.W. Government persistently advertises it,
even to the extent of a whole page in The Bulletin,
as the great sanatorium and holiday resort and
picture land of the State ; that it has such an
abundant water supply and allowance of water
power that a capital there might easily become
one of the greatest manufacturing cities of Aus-
tralia ; and that it is so far away from
Sydney and Melbourne and Adelaide—from
the offices of the Herald and Telegraph
and Bulletin and Age and Argus and
Advertiser and Register— that it must de-
velop an independent press of its own as a
Federal capital ought to do. Also that, being in
a region that is now comparatively empty, the
establishment of a capital there is calculated to
promote the settlement of the uninhabited
parts of Australia —a matter about which all
papers and politicians make a pretence of being
interested. The Herald doesn’t profess to object
to that kind of a capital; it merely says that its
favorite site, which for business purposes is at the
back door of its office, is on a “gently undulating
plain ” —Australia is full of gently undulating
plains—and that “the claims of Canberra are so
strong that there is no need for hesitation.” But
it certainly doesn’t say that the claims of Can-
berra are so strong that they can be submitted
without hesitation to a referendum of the Aus-
tralian people.
At last even the N.S.W. Auditor-General has
had to draw attention to the fact that every time
Ma goes on the London market nowadays Bull
Cohen wants a few ounces more of her flesh by
way of interest and charges. There was a time
when it wasn’t necessary to even get the loans
underwritten ; but now the underwriting shark
has to be called in, and, as more and more of the
loans are left on his hands, he steadily puts up the
price at which he will take the risk and supply
the temporary accommodation. So the Auditor-
General says:—
Even the issue of 1893, the crisis year, was obtained on
more favorable terms than the short-dated 4 per cent.
Debenture Loan of £2,000,000 raised in 1905. . . . The
review shows that the State was able in former years to
raise capital in very many instances on favorable terms
up to 1898 without the aid of underwriting ; but that
from the commencement of the system in the issue of 1901
each successive loan has been raised on a hardening
market, notwithstanding the national prosperity which
has been so evident of late years.
What would have happened if there hadn’t been
this national prosperity, Heaven only knows ; and
what will happen in the future, when the State
can’t always be prosperous, Heaven also only
knows. Still, whatever happens,- whether it is
prosperous or not, N. S. Wales has £9,854,508 of
trouble maturing in 1912, though for the greater
part of that it can extend the I O U if it wants
to; but in 1915 £3,881,081 falls due absolutely, and
another £12,826,200 in 1918. If the State had a
sinking fund—areal honest sinking fund, instead of
the existing small pretence —it might be able to
hold a decent argument with Bull Cohen on the
subject of a renewal; but if it hasn’t got that
fund—well, the Auditor-General’s remarks show
exactly what it may expect.
N.S.W. Assembly wasted a couple of hours over
Cook’s River one night last week, but as
a quarter-million of public money has already
been wasted there, it perhitps doesn’t matter
much. It seems that once the salt sea
water flowed into Cook’s River. Included
in the quarter-million above mentioned was
the cost of a dam Guilt by the Government to
keep this salt water out. Also, £lO,OOO has already
been spent in trying to dig a hole in the
dam to let the salt water in again, as organic
matter is depositing inside the dam, and the
whole densely-populated surrounding area is
threatened with an epidemic. Then £BO,OOO has
been spent in a stormwater channel which is add-
ing to the nuisance, and it will cost £65,000 more
to let the sea water in to clear the river. Further
there are large claims in view for compensation to
Chinese market gardeners whom the expensive
works enabled to settle on the land, but whom the
re-admission of the salt water will unsettle again.
“When I looked upon such a lot of hypocrites
for 19 days, I couldn’t help interjecting uncom-
plimentary remarks,” said Bent of Victoria to
his local greasers the other night, referring to the
Commissioners who lately “inquired” into his
land deals ; and thus he gave away the fact that
the Commissioners knew they were engaged in a
farcical inquiry, and that they knew he knew.
Under those circumstances, of course, he had
nothing to fear from the result. Yet the vain old
man went drivelling on about certain people
“who expected everything to come out,” and were
nonplussed when it didn’t ; and from the
fact that the hypocritical inquiry naturally
didn’t discover much. Bent proceeded to brazenly
assume that his spotless character was established.
This bluff of Bent’s is almost as insufferable as
the publicity given to it by the Tory press which
affects to take him seriously. Newspaper readers
in general are either very ill-informed or very for-
getful, and when Bent is presented to them as a
fine old political lion shaking the dewdrops of
calumny from his mane, they are almost per-
suaded that the picture is taken from life. And
for this lamentable public delusion John Murray
and Co. are responsible. In the records of Bent’s
scandalous reign as Premier of Victoria there was
abundant fuel for a fire at which he might have
been properly roasted. But instead of making
things hot for their old pal, whose ill-doings they
had so long sanctioned, the present Ministry has
wearily preferred to let things slide, and so put
Bent in the way to bring the Government of Vic-
toria into further disrepute.
■
Irish blight was discovered lately in some Tas-
manian potatoes. Instantly N.S.W. blocked
Tasmanian potatoes, to the great jov of its own
spud-growers and those of Victoria, who thus got
sole control of the Sydney market. Then the
Tasmanian spuds began to pour into Melbourne;
so, although the blight had been found in Vic-
toria, there was a howl for prohibition, of course,
“in the interests of the producer.” So the pro-
hibition was issued, and several thousand bags of
Tasmanian spuds were held up on the Melbourne
wharves. Then N.S.W. prohibited Victorian
spuds, whereupon Victoria complained bitterly
that there was as much blight in N.S.W. as in
Victoria. Westralia joined in, and prohibited
all spuds, and the produce merchants promptly
put up the price from £7 to £25 per ton.
South Australia next followed suit, with the same
results. Then a conference of agricultural de-
partments was held in Melbourne, at which it was
agreed that no State would prohibit the landing
of infected produce if the pest was already in both
States ; after which each department went home
—and promptly broke the agreement. - It is spuds
to-dav, and it was oranges and bananas a few
months ago, and it will be something else to-
morrow. Practically all the trouble is the out-
come of petty inter-State jealousy, a survival of
the old days of border duties. The result is ill-
feeling, derangement of trade, and dear food
for the people, while a few sharks gorge them-
selves. If the Commonwealth had control all of
this would be avoided, as blights, etc., would then
be treated from an Australian standpoint. What
with State Frights and blights, the Little Aus-
tralian party is helping the Unification and Re-
construction movement better than it knows.
■■-»-—
Britain is now holding an exhibition of sweated
industries at Earl’s Court, London, and it is hard
to say which excites the more commiseration—the
country that advertises the exhibition or the
exhibition that advertises the country. All the
exhibits represent the sweated toil of Britain’s
dregs, and tell their stories of hopeless woe.
There is an exhibit representing the work of a
girl chain-maker, who extracts a wage of 4s. 6d. a
week. There are exhibits illustrating the labor of
match-makers, who receive for making 144
boxes. There are other items referring to the work
of women who make blouses for 3Jd. apiece, and
turn out heavy motor-coats for the “ governing
classes ”at 2s. o|d. each. And there are heaps of
other things, all proving the hideous condition of
England’s wage slaves ; but England, apparently,
can do nothing for them. All that it has done,
lately, was to appoint a royal commission to gaze
upon the working of the Poor Law system. And
the commission gazed and gazed, and finally pro-
duced a monumental literary effort, the substance
of which was that thousands of pauper workers
are “ still beyond the reach of ameliorative legisla-
tion ! ” The whole vast squalid horror might be
swept away inside a year by the appointment of
Wages Boards but for one thing—under the Free-
trade system you can’t put up wages without
losing the work altogether.

“ The Flood Tide ” was presented at Syd-
ney Royal bv the Bland Holt co. on Saturday
night. It is a delirious work. If the programme
did not go out of its way to mention that the play
is “from the pen of Cecil Raleigh, Esq.,”
one might assume that it was the outcome
of a collaboration between a melodrama-
tist of the old or fruity school, a howling
farce manufacturer, a problem play specialist
and 17 lunatics. There are 50 characters (not
counting supers.), and they represent every grade
of society, from racecourse touts to titled folk,
British and otherwise. The Dago, the Irishman,
the Gipsy and the Cockney abound ; and the
action rages between mid-ocean, Brighton, Kemp-
ton Park, Maidenhead, the Coburg Hotel (Lon-
don) and Victoria Railway Station. So the
public gets variety for its money.
* * * *
Proceedings open aboard the s.s. Bolivian
en route from Chili to London. A howl off-stage
and revolver-shots denote that an inebriated mil-
lionaire—known to the programme as Mac-
Naughton, but to the characters as Thompson—
has shot himself and jumped overboard. The
Baroness Pitchioli and her daughter Maria
learn of the event with unconcealed glee. They
materialise later in the vestibule of the Metropole
Hotel, Brighton, as Mac Thompson’s mother-in-law
and widow respectively. Meanwhile the incorri-
gible tendency of the heavy stage father to mix him-
self up in financial transactions with which his
invariably low order of intellect unfits him
to grapple, has landed honest old Dick Cham-
pion—landlord of the Running Horse pub,
and father of Capt. Champion, V.C.—with-
in measurable distance of penal servitude.
The oldster does not quail personally at the
prospect of a felon’s doom, but fears that it may
embarrass his soldier son —V.C. ’s being notoriously
averse from owning gaoled parents. So he gets
the son aforesaid to expend £9OOO on a horse which
is at an outside price for a forthcoming event at
Kempton Park ; and, furthermore, he instructs
Bland Holt to proceed to Brighton as the suicided
Thompson, and on that false pretence to pick up
whatever ready cash, title deeds, mortgage instru-
mentsand the like maybegoing. Bland cheerfully
undertakes this criminal enterprise because he
loves Miss Champion. Arrived at Brighton
Bland joins forces with the Baroness and her
daughter, and in the course of 15 minutes makes
himself liable for about 50 years’ imprisonment
with hard labor. One of his most profitable
“ THE FLOOD TIDE," SYDNEY ROYAL.
Mrs. Bland and actor Brown saved by the
winning gee-gee Recruit.
coups is to lift £10,000 in ready cash off a company
■promoter on the understanding that the latter
may float Thompson’s best diamond mine as a
public concern. At this stage the audience turns
in a stupent condition and considers the fortunes
of Captain Champion, Y.C. His fellow officers
dislike him. He demands heatedly of the gallery
why this should be in the case of one who has
shed his life’s ber-lud in the cause of the Fer-lag,
and so forth. The ostensible reason advanced
is that his father runs a pub. To the
writer it seems to be more a question
of hair. The Captain of Max Maxwell has the
maddeningly loquacious and rococo personality
that habitually accompanies beautiful, long, shiny,
black head-foliage. He possesses an amazing
number of virtues, but, like the rest of his
kind, he is over-clamant about them. He
would “go” like blazes at a church
bazaar or Sabbath-school bun-fight, but in
barracks he appears as an exotic. Accordingly,
we are not surprised when his companions in
arms violently assault him in his bedroom. In-
cidentally, while doing so. they discover the
daughter of Colonel Sir Russell Corry lurk-
ing, in all innocence, in a cupboard. Next
day, the captain’s £9000-horse annexes the
big race at Kempton. His parent snares a
sufficient sum in bets to clear up his
monetary trouble. However, about here the
suicided millionaire inopportunely returns.
A while later, the millionaire, Bland, and
a foreigner named Derago eventuate in
a reservoir. The foreigner is there with the ob-
ject of killing Thompson, or Bland, or anyone
else with cash —assassination without the option
being his main hobby. Shortly afterwards the
dam bursts, and apparently hurls the trio into
eternity. Here the writer left for home mentally
defying Cecil Raleigh, Esq., to free his characters
from the maze of trouble in which they were in-
volved.
As to the acting, Bland Holt is vivacious
as Clipp, and Mrs. Bland is worthy, honest and
affectionate as Miss Champion. Champion,
senior, in the hands of Charles Brown, is as
innocent an old party as ever aided and abetted
crime. Max Maxwell portrays his son, the Y.C.,
as a beautiful young actor-man chock-full of fire
and soul and eloquence. Arthur Styan’s dipso-
maniacal millionaire, MacNaughton-Thompson, is
an interesting creation, and the adventuress of
Harrie Ireland is a satisfactory sample of the
breed. There are some pleasingly-filled ingenue
parts, a number of unusual, not to say incredible,
British officers, and many earnestly-handled
minor rdles. The scenic effects are excellent.
* * * *
“ The King of Cadonia,” at Her Maj. (Syd-
ney), resembles “Hamlet” minus the Ghost, the
Wade Government devoid of a grievance, or any
other incomplete thing, for Bert Gilbert is out of
the cast. The diverting mime is ill, and his place is
filled temporarily by another —one G. Nable. The
rest of the company is in good health, and the
throne of Cadonia will be filled by its present
occupant till further notice.
* * * *
At Sydney Criterion, “Cinderella ’ con-
tinues to attract the dross which is the root of
all drinks, regardless of the fact that it (the
play, not the dross and the drinks) has been
running for seven weeks. Several new songs
have been introduced, and on a recent night
a rumor was current along the length and breadth
of Pitt-street that a perfectly fresh jape had done
a preliminary canter at this playhouse. Be the
report true or false, it is a fact that the houses
lately have been amazing.
* * * *
Captain Winston’s seals impart quite a
religious flavor to the bill of Sydney Tivoli. They
look like sleek sea-curates as they bark hoarsely
over their pulpits, and their flippers suggest the
cloth-encumbered arms of the clergyman in his
working garb. To complete the resemblance,
their strange terminations are like the lower ends
of elongated surplices. If the Truth is ever
planted in the Polar regions, The Bulletin is
willing to lay six archbishops to one .vicar that
seals will do the job. However, their well-oiled
religious aspect doesn’t prevent them saying
“D—n” publicly. During their performance,
when the small humorous seal is trying to
get the big, soft globe balanced on his snout,
he has to chase it down to the footlights,
nip it with his teeth, toss it into the air, and
catch it. It is the difficult preliminary to their
best concerted trick, and he generally has to make
three or four attempts. Usually, after the second
unsuccessful try, he whirls round to the audience
and shouts “ D n ! ” It is a loud, wild, human
“D n,” and the fact that it is said in Sealish
makes no difference ; it is so plain that no trans-
lation is needed. The three Sprightly Sisters
have a change of programme, including a clever
doll dance. They are a joyous trio, and when
they rush through a romantic burlesque it is like
three clever girls romping at home. One of the
great charms of their performance is that they
seem to enjoy their own fun. Other cheering
turns are supplied by theKremka Brothers, Blake
and Granby, and Ronald George, a young instru-
mentalist from London.
* * *-
“ A Village Priest,” following “The Flag
Lieutenant” at Melbourne Princess’s, is as wel-
come as the flask of whisky that waits upon the
water bottle. Sydney Grundy’s sterling play,
prompted by a trashy French melodrama, has an
ardent spiritual nature, the spirit being a few
degrees overproof. It isn’t an absolutely great
play. The miracle at the end of the fourth act is
unnecessary to the completion of the plot, and is
an artistic defect in an otherwise reasonable story ;
so is the suggestion of the Abbe still feeling
a pain in his good old heart when he is re-
minded of his youthful fondness for the future
Madame D’Arcay 40 years ago. Naturalism is
sacrificed to theatrical effect every time the Abbe
pauses before telling a pious fib, as though pious
prevarication were not the Church’s business.
Nevertheless, “A Village Priest “—they call it
“The V.P.” nowadays—is a strong, well-knit
drama, wherein the eternal verities are sternly
maintained. The sin of the Comtesse finds her
out and shows her no mercy. For the crime of
the late Judge D’Arcay several virtuous people
have to suffer. Nevertheless, histombstone will con-
tinue to record him as an irreproachable husband,
a pattern father, and an ornament to the Bench.
The guilt of that exalted citizen is no sooner
brought to light than it is hidden away again.
All of which is as it is, and ever shall be, world
without end.
The present revival has some special points
of attraction. Titheradge is an even better Abbe
than of yore. His handling of the character is a
little more gentle and paternal; there is no shade
of insincerity, no suspicion of stage craft in his
utterances, even when he has to pause, by stage
directions, on the verge of telling a tarradiddle.
Cyril Maclcay, as the Judge’s son, gives an earnest,
well-restrained presentment of the character. He
is the enthusiastic lawyer, open to conviction from
the start and quick to perceive the possibility
of truth in Torquenie’s protestations. Gregan
McMahon, as the convict, wears a more crushed
and soiled appearance than his predecessors ; he
is less combative, and therefore more likely to
return to prison and wear a “ martyr’s crown.”
Though his elocution is somewhat artificial
and his recognition of old D’Arcay’s por-
trait doesn’t produce the thrilling effect
that Robert Brough achieved when he shook
his fist at the painting, McMahon gives full
value to the part. Miss Emma Temple, a trifle
hard in manner, speaks the lines of Madame
D’Arcay feelingly, except when she asks the Abbe
if he wants more money, in the tone of an un-
cheerful philanthropist. Miss Ethel Warwick, as
Jeanne Torquenie, also errs on the side of severity,
whilst Miss Elwvn Harvey is getting the sym-
pathy of the audience for her winsome sketch of
Marguerite. The strong part of the Comtesse
falls to Miss Dorothy Grimston, who looks it
admirably and plays it not very well. There’s an
unpleasant harshness in her voice when she grows
emotional. Her sob is real enough, but her
violent agitation, terminating in the orthodox
stage swoon, is only half expressed. Miss Ger-
trude Boswell, as the Abbe's housekeeper, dabbles
in farcical comedy with ill-success; and the
scenerv is good enough. When “The Village
Priest” has breathed his last amen, another
revival of “Sherlock Holmes” will transpire.

A trouble with “ The Duchess of Dantzic ”
is that the opera has a lot of consecutive
narrative which overshadows the music, and
which lost its novelty long ago. As an opera,
“Dantzic” lacks frivolity—the most successful
number is Lefebvre’s sturdy drinking song in the
first act —and as “ a drama, interrupted by' music,”
it is poor in dialogue. Moreover, the poorest lines
are spoken by the almighty potentate who rules
the social storm. His final words are “Get
up, Duchess, you shan’t die. You shall live
to tell the world that you have beaten
Napoleon.” One can hardly feel much re-
spect for a Bonaparte who resembles the
Face at the Window and talks pretentious
nonsense. An im-
pressive Nap. might
have gone a long
way in popular esti-
mation ; but Wv-
bert Stamford
merely provokes a
smile at certain
moments when he
is supposed to be a
cause for serious
dismay. Another
grave mistake in
the Napoleonic con-
nection is a color-
less Josephine, ad-
dicted to sneering
somewhat at Flor-
ence Young. She
ought to have been
Marie Louise. Mel-
bourne has taken its
notions of Josephine
from “ A Royal
Divorce,” and an
opera that puts her
in an unfavorable
light would have
to be very tuneful
to condone the
offence. “Dantzic”
is less tuneful than
might be expected
of “Ivan Caryll,”
the composer. But
it is supported by
the Williamson
chorus, in gay
dresses. And the
little lot of alleged
milliners’ assistants
whocOme bounding
into the gardens at
Fontainebleau are
one of the most
pleasing interrup-
tions that ever
earned a double encore at Her Majesty’s.
“ Othello ” will desist from breaking re-
cords at Melbourne Royal after Friday night.
Meanwhile, the dusky Moor is the topic of the
tea-table and the observed of all observers.
People are struck by the fact that he is the happy
medium between Irving Saylesand Mourzouk, the
stout Algerian gentleman who used to be paid a
salary bv wrastler Carkeek to suffer defeat on the
mat. Also, it is remarked that the warrior
never appears anywhere without something on
his head. He gets his hair off, in a figura-
tive way of speaking, but he always wears
a weird hat or cap or a turban on the place
where his wool ought to grow. The exact
nature of the vegetation (if any) on Othello’s
scalp will be a theme of Shakspearian debate
for another day, and then the disciples of the Bard
will once more bump up against the question as to
what becomes of the exiled ducal party in the
Forest of Arden whilst their clothes are being
washed and ironed. Likewise the lovely appear-
ance of Orlando, who has left home without any
luggage, will call for scholarly explanation. “As
You Like It” is billed for Saturday, and all the
seats have, presumably, been booked in advance.
Needless to say that Miss Lily Brayton will be
Rosalind. lan Penny, who plays Montano in
“ Othello,” will burst forth as Orlando on Satur-
day, whilst Oscar Asche assumes the character of
the dyspeptic Jacques, and drops into melancholy.

At Melbourne King’s there was a strong
demand for real Australian goods during Agricul-
tural Show week, and “ The Bushwoman ” sup-
plied the want. Jo Smith’s drama doesn’t dis-
improve on acquaintance. Its cheeks glow with
local color. There are gum leaves in its hair, and
hayseeds in its whiskers. And its horses are now
behaving admirably under trying circumstances.
One of the gee-gees brings Dalgleish into notice
from time to time; and when Dalgleish appears
as a mounted trooper and takes out his notebook,
the present writer reckons that he is looking at
an Australian cop on whom there are no flies.

At Melbourne Opera House the small
dogs of RosinaCasselli are repeating their remark-
able performance with the same elegant fittings
and appointments as before. And the rest of the
bill is what it was a week ago, plus Eva Lee and
her Twothpicks.
** * *
Tait’s entertainment at Melbourne Athe-
naeum is commended to the recognition of all
juveniles between six and 60. This week there is
an abundance of primitive, picturesque gentle-
men who have tastes for wild love-making and
untidy homicides. There is, for instance, a char-
acter named Morgan, who knifes his way to the
rescueofadamsel, and finishes a festival of slaughter
with an expert hanging sensation. There is a
cowboy yarn full of dashing bronchos, Injuns and
doormat pants, which occur on the rolling prairie,
seemingly within a train ride of the Parisian
boulevards. There is still further a narrative of a
nice girl, a motor-car johnny and a Yankee
“ bad man,” and in that the bad man gets a
bullet, while the johnny takes the girl. But the
most interesting picture is “The Salvation
Army Lass,” where one of Booth’s female
warriors converts the “bloke” who has belted
her —which is a dastardly reflection on the sanity
of the superior sex.
* -* * *
West’s pictures at Melbourne Olympia
continue to be bright and topical. On Saturday
there were extracts from the cross Channel aero-
plane flights of Bleriot and Latham, who appear
to have made space their metropolis, and left the
earth merely outer-suburban. There were also
snapshots of a Russian religious festival
(wherein each priest is a pageant in himself).
and of some unpronounceable places in
Mexico, which are simply heretical in the
fashion of their hats. “Doomed,” which begins
with an idyll and ends up in the basement of the
Pit, is a disgraceful libel on the supernatural
powers of Mephistophele.s; while “The Son’s
Return ” is an exemplary study in parental
affection. When the good son returns unrecog-
nised to his papa and mamma, his welcome is
gratifying and unexpected. He is tapped on the
cranium for the contents of his wallet.

The Tivoli is Adelaide’s leading theatre for
the moment. Its prices are up, while those of the
Royal are temporarily' down; and still Manager

Her latest and most Heaven-gazing photograph.
Photo, by Lafayette , Melbourne.
Marcus is turning money away. The magician
Chung is the attraction. He has now carefully
explained his pedigree ; and the Scotch-engineer
strain is visible in all his works. They are mar-
vels of mechanical ingenuity and good stage-
management. The Empire has a popular variety
show at about half Tivoli prices, the Tybells
getting the largest type for their iron-jawed
work in mid-air; the Royal is playing a
melodrama, “Married to the Wrong Man”
(recklessly called “ the present sensation of
England”); and West’s Pictures unobtrusively
contrive to keep the Town Hall crowded, though
Amy Castles is about to turn them out of it. Also
the leading amateur crowd, the “ Appendreena,”
has given a really good and well-balanced rendering
of that fine but difficult play, “ The Idler.” The
characters are few, and there was not a weak spot
among them.
Judge Murray, of N.S.W., worked off a
bright saying the other day, which wasn’t really
so bright as it looked. A policeman was in the
box trying to convict the accused on the favorite
finger-print evidence. He said, out of the illimit-
able knowledge which the cop gathers on his beat
in Surry Hills, that no two finger-prints were
alike. The defendant’s lawyer retorted that the
officer hadn’t examined the finger-prints of all the
fourteen hundred million people on the globe, so
that, to all intents and purposes, he was talking
through his helmet. Further, hepbihted out as an
unsatisfactory element in thecase.thattheliterature
on the finger-print subject was mostly'written by
policemen for policemen. Then Judge Murray
heaved a remark off his chest : “I believe,” he
said, “there are text books which are written by
astronomers for astronomers and they are the best
books.” The defendant’s lawyer is said to have
been squashed by this huge observation, but it is
difficult to see why. If an astronomer made his
living by getting the planets and the fixed stars
into gaol, then his book showing how to convict
Sirius of furious driving among the constellations
might also be viewed with suspicion. But as the
astronomer can’t have any ulterior motive, he
hardly comes into the same category as the police
theorist with his finger-print theory.
A game old battler gave some improbable-
sounding evidence before the Undertakers’ Wages
Board in Sydney last week. His tale set forth
that he is 56 and is a “casual” in the planting
trade. He earns 2s. 6d. for a “ carry,” the same
for a “mind ” (i.e., for guarding the hearse while
the buriers are away committing deceased to the
cemetery, lest small hoys should play “tig” round
the blackened vehicle), and ss. fora “drive.” If
the “drive” is a four-horse affair the fee amounts
to 6s. 6d., on account of the risk —the hearse-
horse being liable to bolt. The witness said that
he snares, all told, by these services a trifle under
£1 weekly. Out of that he pays 9s. a week rent,
supports a wife and daughter, and gives them “meat
once a day.” And he owes no bills of any kind, nor
has done so during the six years and upwards that
he has been a supernumerary undertaker. It
seems essentially a field wherein the Socialistic
tiger might roar to advantage ; in fact, if the
clamor rose to a scream, and resulted in the wages
of undertakers’ “casuals” being raised 100 per
cent, or so, a compassionate and fair-minded
public—which will have to die itself some day,
and won’t wish to be attended in that dark hour
by a villainously sweated “ minder ’’—would pro-
bably greet the reform with relief.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Mrs. Nordica, the prima donna, was married in London
the other week to George Washington Young-, of New
York ; and, although the wedding was “ very quiet,” a
duchess or two figured among the audience. Nordica’s
maiden name was Norton, seemingly, for the certificate
described her as Lilian Doeme, formerly Norton, divorced
wife of Yoltan Doeme, and daughter of Edwin Norton,
deceased. Neither was the bridegroom a novice at nup-
tials. George Washington was described as divorced
husband of Natalie Young, formerly Bray, son of Peter
Young, deceased. Evidently he is in a position to support
a prima donna, for his place of residence prior to the
ceremony had been the Hotel Ritz, where only million-
aires can meet the bill without a shudder, and his present
to the bride was a rope of pearls.
“0.K.”: Foster Fraser, lecturing on “America Up
To Date,” is probably Foster Fraser at his weakest, from
an Australian point of hearing. It is the Briton trying
to draw as many impartial comparisons as are possible in
favor of England, and as few as possible in favor of
America. His views are mostly superficial, and his con-
clusions are apt to be contradictory. Seeing that his lec-
ture on America “goes” well enough, and his heavy
touches of humor excite some laughter, one is compelled
to admit that Foster Fraser knows his business. He is
fluent without being eloquent: positive, yet not egotis-
tical ; interesting rather than instructive. As for the
lantern-slides that illustrate his discourse, they fall short
of the demands of these cinematographic times. To the
people who sit regularly in front of the cine, screen,
lantern-slides are no longer exciting.
Typhoid has proved fatal in Westralia to Alec Verne,
who was managing- the Maggie Moore-Roberts company
for Clarke, Meynell and Gunn. Verne entered into
management about 10 years ago with J. F. Sheridan (The
Widow O’Brien). He was but 40 years of age when he
passed out.
“J.K.”: Charles Bailey', the Melbourne “medium,”
and his “spirit controls” Abdul, Selim, Dr. Wbitcombp,
Professor Denton and co., have been putting in a short
season in Wellington (M.L.). Bailey’s stock-in-trade com-
prises a small collection of “apports,” which the spirits
dematerialise (in the dark), a smattering of “psychic”
jargon, and a number of wealthy and other cranks. The
pro -eedings at the stances have been on the old lines—
examination of the medium, darkness, and addresses by
“spirit controls”; restoration of the gaslight, and the
amazing discovery that the spirits have produced a bird,
or an egg, or a foreign mat-apron ! And the believers
murmur “Wonderful!” while the sceptics ask questions
which Bailey displays much resource in fencing. Chiefly
the sceptics have asked for more convincing “ apports.”
If the spirits can produce a bird, why not an elephant?
Why not something that could not by any means be con-
cealed about the person? At one sbance, the usual de-
mand went up for a copy of that day’s London Times.
Bailey said he could produce the sheet, but he had pro-
mised that it should be done in the presence of Mr. Stan-
ford, his wealthy patron, and it would plainly never do
to break faith with the man of money. When the
questions became too inconvenient, the faithful, headed
by W. McLean, ex-M.P., chairman of the Wellington
Spiritualists’ Association, would come to the medium’s
rescue with polite cries of “Shame ! ” A local Doctor of
Philosophy asked the spirit where Stanley was born, and
was informed that it was in America. When the
questioner pointed out that the explorer was born in
Wales, McLean said this was “ irrelevant to the address.”
“ Dr. Whitcombe” was requested by the local scientist to
explain the difference between “induction ” and “ deduc-
tion,” but McLean then rose in a hurry to complain that
this sort of cross-examination was “ disturbing- the vibra-
tions.” A Wellington conjurer has put up £lOO as a
wager that he will do any trick Bailey’s spirits are capable
of. McLean has covered the money, but the usual haggle
has started about the conditions.
“ Billy T.” : Much severe censure of town-bred Austra-
lians was scattered through “ The Bushwoman ” for the
benefit of Show visitors in Melbourne the other week.
The Australian from the bush and range and plain and
desert was eulogised as the noblest work of God; the
townie was sternly rebuked as a miserable sinner, tailor-
made and seething in conceit, who lives on the tiller and
the grand, still toiler who handles Cow. There were
bitter references to shocking injustices inflicted on
Farmer Billjim by,town-made legislation, and t,he rural
invasion pounded the floor with its solid feet, and ex-
pressed its appreciation with intolerable noise. A be-
whiskered bushie next me was enormously delighted with
all the guff about the splendid independence of the bloke
on the soil. I learned in the interval that he was expect-
ing to get a lump of money as consolation for his recent
flood troubles ; he had done well out of an artificially-
cherished butter industry; more than once he had
received seed wheat gratis; and twice he had been
liberally compensated for damage done by bush fires that
vastly improved his grass paddocks.
“Parisine”: Re Napoleons of the stage—in Australia
there have been a number. I think Stamford’s, in “ The
Duchess of Dantzic,” is the most convincing for appear-
ance. For one thing he is short enough. His acting is
not so impressive as J. Knight’s, but then he has nothing
like so good a play, from a Napoleonic point of view.
The peculiar semi-nasal delivery of Knight seems as if it
might have been Bonev’s. Cecil Ward was creditable.
Atholwood was better. Brough could not be Bonaparte,
except that his elocution, though different from that of
J. Superfluous, seemed also as if it might have beep
Napoleon’s. Irving’s performance was an amazing feat,
like his Robespierre—both mere curiosities. Gomersal,
at the London Astley’s, was the accepted Napoleon.
Duquesne is about the best in Paris. He was here with
Bernhardt. By the way, he figures as ex-Sultan Abdul
Hamid in West’s biograph picture. Has anybody heard
0 f Abdul Hamid lately? What did he die of?
“S.S.”: Saw the “Australian Dartos” again at
Rickards’ Melbourne house. They have improved out of
sight, have worked new gyrations into their cyclonic
dances, and are now whirling through a breathless turn
that should shake up a vaudeville audience anywhere on
earth. Give the auburn-haired Edie M‘Lean two years
more to bring out all the qualities that are in her, and
this little part.y should take Paris by the hair. But every
day the folly of the name they jig under becomes more
apparent. They were the Baby Dartos a few years back,
and their turn was only a clever imitation. Now they
are almost grown-ups, their dancing has taken on indi-
vidual quality and original features, and in calling them-
selves “The Dartos” they still bear the brand of slavish
imitators, and invite odious comparisons. It would be
fatal to go abroad so dubbed, and it is time for the young
Macks to make a name for themselves.
“Floater”: Miss Amy Castles maybe a great singer,
which one naturally deduces from her singular hatred of
advertisement, but I should say that she is a much greater
writer. I have seen her ravishing autograph, which
strikes me as most accomplished. Further, it has such a
profusion of flourishes, and such a suggestion of having
been done with a mapping pen and a blacking brush, that
I should be glad to know what nib she uses. I should
love to be able to write my name like Miss Amy. Some-
times I marvel at the time, trouble and concentration
s e must have spent in practising that signature. But I
marvel more at the divine gifts of invention and imagina-
tion that enabled her to conceive it. It is convincing.
She writes “Castles” with battlements, moats, draw-
bridges and everything complete.
Beerbohm Tree held his “ Shakspeare Festival ” at Her
Majesty’s (London) in July. The festival is now a regular
annual happening, beloved of the people who form
societies for the consideration of the Bard’s hidden mean-
ings and last possible comma. Within a fortnight eight
plays were produced. Two or three different companies,
and a varied selection of ladies for the heroine parts, were
brought into action during the two weeks. When
“ Hamlet” was produced, Marie Lohr got a show as
Ophelia ; and young Marie Lohr in these youthful parts
(which have hitherto been monopolised by middle-aged
actresses) is a delightful novelty. Charles Tait, just back
from Europe, tells of how he was charmed by Marie as
Lady Teazle. When she first appeared he couldn’t accept
her as a real Lady Teazle. She seemed too light and
irresponsible. All the other Lady Teazles he had ever
seen were so different from this young girl. Presently he
arrived at the conclusion that none of the others had been
a little bit like the real Lady Teazle, whereas Marie Lohr
was just she. Within her powers as a light emotional
comedy actress, Marie is always just She.
“Melb.”: There are amazing coincidences between
Randolph Bedford’s “White Australia”and West’s bio-
graph of a Japanese attack on California. Yet neither
could possibly have been drawn from the other. In
“ White Australia ” a Japanese spy is a kind of butler to a
squatter. In the biograph a Jap spy is butler to General
Wayne, a sort of squatter. In “White Australia” the
Jap spy kills the telegraph operator at Daly Waters and
cuts the wire. In the biograph he kills a sentry
at Santa Barbara telegraph station and cuts the
wire. In “White Australia” the Jap spy schemes
to get the plan of defence and the key of the airship. In
the biograph he steals the plan and sends it by airship
to the Japanese fleet. In “White Australia” a woman
is killed by the side of a cannon among the defenders.
Exactly the same thing in the biograph. The scenes of
the entry of the Japanese troops are much alike, except
that the Yankee is privileged to bring in a lot of cavalry.
“ E.M.K.” : Consider the case of the Native Mumming
Bird. Importation’s got him in a bad way ,and he’s likely
to be worse before he’s better. Along with the entrance
of Meynell and Gunn’s Oscar Asche co. of Englishmen
happened the exit from the same management of some 15
Australian mummers—no further use for them, they were
told. J.C.W. is sending out a complete company of
Frohman’s, and three other English lots are to follow.
Our own, our very own, bellowdrama king, Billanderson,
has sickened with the disease ; his leading man is im-
ported, and now come two Englishwomen for his new
play—“ The Bush woman,” of all plays ! A sinister rumor
floating about Poverty Point tells of the brutal utterance
of the senior partner of a certain management: “I
wouldn’t have an Australian in my companies if I could
help it.” Yet it was a play financed by Australians and
produced by an Australian that set this manager on his
feet. Some years ago another manager was credited with
a similar sentiment; since then he has lost, it is said,
£50,000. He is now being put on his feet by an Australian
star who stuck to him through all, and saved him from
going completely down and out. Before too late we
might preserve at least one or two specimens of the Aus-
tralian Mumming Bird as curiosities for future genera-
tions to amuse themselves with.
A recent sensation in the way of things theatrical in
the Big Smoke is a play bearing the grandiloquent title,
“ The Earth.” It is a very realistic skit upon Harms-
worth and his Daily Mail. The leading character, Sir
Felix Janion, is so easily recognisable that the man in
the street never troubles to call him Sir Felix Janion.
As in “An Englishman’s Home ” the audience always
speaks of the unknown invaders as German, so in “The
Earth” no one refers to Felix, but always to Harmsworth
or Lord Northcliffe. Half of the play is clever, giving,
with but slight exaggeration, the actual daily happen-
ings in an up-to-date newspaper office. The plot has to
do with the paper’s fight against the Sweating Bill
that certain members are trying to rush throug-h
Parliament. But when Janion stoops to blackmail, and
uses information acquired as a host in order to ruin his
guest, the play falls down and never quite recovers. It
is there that it becomes unreal, stagey, libellous and
utterly fantastic. Yet nothing could be happier and
more diverting than the delightful caricature of Harms-
worth as given in the first scenes. Of course he triumphs
over the Sweating Bill, and then before the final curtain
there comes the finest sarcasm of all. While the world is
ringing with the news of his victory he sternly silences
the jubilations of his writers over a thing that is past,
and summons them to listen to his latest idea—an
“Infants’ Encyclopaidia,” describing everything in the
world and illustrated in colors !

THE BURGLAR MIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I Early last week the house occupied by Mrs. T. Garvin,
in Park-street, Brunswick West, was entered by burglars,
; who took Mr. and Mrs. Garvin’s marriage certificate. The
, burglars returned on Saturday morning, with the follow-
ing letter :
Dear Madam, —
I herewith take the earliest opportunity of forward-
ing private manuscript of yours, which has, as you
are aware, fallen into my hands. One thing, my mate
and I have come to the conclusion is that your little
child is about the sweetest little thing in the world,
watching us enter the room, and never a murmur
from it.—Burglar Bill.— News Item.
And so the long-expected has arrived ;
The day of stress is here ;
The mummer (who, till now, by Art contrived
To snare his daily beer)
Has had to drop the trade at which Kean thrived
In order to appear
With jemmies, bulls-eyes, “ sneakers ” and the like
Upon the world’s broad stage
In the “ fat ” r6le of burglar. He must strike
For a fair drinking wage,
E'en though in taking such a step, belike
He makes the public rage.
Yet am I fearful that his burgling run
Will turn out to be brief.
He who adventures forth at set of sun
To play the part of thief
Needs to elude and baffle the John Dunn ;
Also his watchful chief.
The actor can’t—no matter how he pines.
He’s patent as the day.
Stage custom aye his burgling undermines.
Take, in this case, the way
He venerates the Che-ild and Marriage Lines—
It gives him right away.

“SKY.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The balance of the show is sky.—Bulletin, 12.S/'09.
Dear Bulletin, —If I might paraphrase
The Bard (he’s mightj- popular these days),
I’d say : “ I thank thee, B., for that good line,”
It likes me well ” (the Bard again) ; it’s fine.
How often —oh, how oft!—right through a show
I’ve sat —a patient martyr, steeped in woe !
“ All stars,” the programme told me. ’Twas a lie !
One constellation shone, the rest was “ sky.”
Apt word, long wanted ! I’m in sympathy
With him who coined it—hence my thanks, dear B,
All kinds of much-boomed entertainments I
Have been to —for my sins—when all was “ sky.”
And shall ag'ain, perchance, for shows must be
Well-trumpeted to bring- the “ brass,” d’ye see 1
The day-bill draws us and our seats we buy—
We don’t knoiv till ive get inside it’s “ sky ” /
But on occasion when outside once more,
We’ll meet the manager just by the door ;
I wonder how he’ll take it if we try
On him this phrase : “ Your show is blooming sky !”
N.S.W.
E. T.

“EAST LYNNE ” IN THE SUBURBS.
BARBARA HARE: “ Alas! Alas! Why did he
pass me over for a baby-faced chit like that ? ”
On two occasions lately the Seven Seas
might have been blown out of their Seven Beds
and awakened in a smother of foam to find stars
raining down on them from the cracked and
•splintered heavens. The first time was when the
fSteamer Borkum, with 4000 cases of dynamite
aboard, caught tire. The second was when the
Indralema of the Tyser line, with a record cargo
<of 600 tons of the same substance below hatches,
ran into a jumble of sea so awful that the captain
could only attribute it to a seaquake. It took 24
hours at a speed of nine knots an hour to cross the
rough area, and during all that time the propeller
only managed to grip the water now and again,
owing to the fact that the ship only hit the sea
now and again—the rest of the time it was in the
air. Every joint of the vessel shrieked aloud
during the jolting, and the thought of 600 tons of
dynamite taking leaping exercise below suffused
the occasion with doubt and made the garboard
strake think about its past life. Quite a number
of terrified passengers remarked wildly then that
the importing business was overdone, and that
Australia could easily make its own dynamite.
The scientific world seems to regard Dr.
Cook’s yarn about having reached the North Pole
with a slightly-fishy expression, and shows a
desire to ask for proofs. So far, the number of
Polar hoaxes has been remarkably small; yet
there were few directions in which a really cir-
cumstantial liar could have gone as far without
fear of contradiction. The only Far Northern
hoax the writer can remember was that of the
Yankee who dropped overboard from a whaling
ship, or something of that sort, a curiously-made
copper cylinder which contained an elaborate
account of how he had, alone and unaided,
reached the Pole, and had found there a balmy
climate. He discoursed at length about the
liquor, the absence of poverty, the amusements
and the life generally. Quite superfluously, he
begged that no expedition should be sent to
rescue him, as he would not return, and the Polar
people might not like too many strangers drop-
ping in. No expedition was sent. What knocked
the bottom out of the yarn was that the copper-
smith who made the cylinder recognised the pic-
ture of it in the papers, and furnished the date on
which he supplied it. There was a horrible dis-
crepancy when that date was compared with the
one on the letter inside the casket.
Melbourne’s old historic City Watchhouse
has sheltered its last drunk, and a new. clean
edifice, built at the back of the original Watch-
house, is meeting public requirements. There
are padded cells, hot baths and up-to-date lighting
arrangements in the new premises. Lodgers suf-
fering from acute thirst can touch a button and
get a long drink of water. The lot of the tem-
porary visitor is quite luxurious. Nothing is want-
ing to make the place all that a watchhouse ought
to be save suitable associations. The lack of un-
hallowed memories is so noticeable that some of
Melbourne’s most habitual drunks were melted to
tears the first time they were bundled into the
modern lockup. One old lady complained that
it didn’t even smell homelike.
* * * *
The Yic. Assembly was loitering over a
Bill to amend the Marriage Acts, the other day,
when Labor member Solly brought up the question
of the physical condition of intending brides and
bridegrooms. Solly suggested that something
should be done by the Legislature to prevent con-
sumptives from marrying consumptives, and so
on. Premier Weary very nicely agreed with
Solly ; but he was not prepared to do the some-
thing. In the interests of high morality he was
then engaged in squelching the marriage-shop
scandals; but in the interests of health and the
future he remained profoundly sleepy and in-
different. Yet if it comes to a choice between the
wedding of irresponsible drunks in a “shop”
and the marriage of irresponsible diseases in a
church, the latter is liable to be infinitely the
worse. The first may result in temporary curses
and regrets ; but the second may carry the curses
and regrets through several generations.
* * * *
If the reports are true, wireless has at last
made the big jump, and the submarine cable is an
out-of-date institution. According to the Times,
of Manila, the U.S. transport Sheridan lately
contrived to talk to San Francisco across 3270
miles of sea—a record-breaking conversation which
is all the more remarkable since ships’instruments
are very much weaker than those of the long-
distance stations, where the operator has as much
power as he can yoke up. Up
till quite recently the effective
range of powerful shore stations
was reckoned at about 1000 miles.
Last year the operator on the
Bremen, of the N.D.L. Co.’s
Australian service, just managed
to catch a whisper or two from
the German wireless headquarters
whilst the liner was at Port Said
on the outward voyage. But he
could not talk back, as his in-
struments were good only for
about 250 miles—which was a
couple of thousand miles short.
Hitherto the best wireless work
has been done mostly by war-
ships, which can spare more
power and space to the wireless
men than can the average hurried
liner. The big shore jump of the
wonder spark is bound to happen
soon, and it would be rash to set
any limit to it. Even if it doesn’t
circle the earth in one flash, it looks highly pro-
bable that it will spring across a continent or an
ocean or two; and, in the light of that reflection,
the deep sea cables begin to look very old property
indeed.

* * * *
Newspaper controversy on the burning
topic of the “Pitfalls of Youth ’’ has established
a sassiety dictionary meaning for the term
“flapper.” A giddy girleen of over 12, but not
yet “come out,” is technically known as a flapper.
One of them, who has put her hair up and been
converted to the true maidenly faith, signs herself
“A Flapper Grown Older,” and gives the game
away in a letter to the Argus, like a (Salvation
Army young woman at a testimony meeting. Her
advice to “ parents in society ” is the very essence
of personal experience :
Don’t give flapper dances unless there are enough
chaperones to prevent couples from disappearing instead
of dancing. It is quite customary for youngsters over 12
to “sit out” in the dark, and their parents would be
horrified at the conversations that take place. Don’t
allow picnics to string on after it is too dark for play.
Children start flirting nowadays at 12 or so. Public
schoolboy s start it, as a rule ; and you have only to talk to
a boy of 19—young enough to be confidential—to find out
what goes on outside the ballroom.
She lays down other golden rules for the manage-
ment of flappers, one of which, alas ! can be quite
as easily honored in the breach as in the observ-
ance. For instance :—Make your sons call for
their sisters when quite small. They then
take it for granted as they grow older, and
the girls are not seen home by the fascinating
male of the season.” But trustfulness in the
fraternal propriety of male youth can be so easily
abused, for if two flapper chums are called for by
their respective brothers the relations are apt td
get mixed, and an all-round oath of secrecy holds
good until the flappers fall out and start to tell on
one another.
By “ Foreign-Going Master ” : —-
The VVaratah is now seven weeks overdue, and, despite
well-meant attempts to keep hope alive, anxietj 7 is giving;
way to despair. Other vessels have remained out longer
than the Lund liner, but there are two ugly facts to be
accounted for. The Waratah was on a short voyage,
much like one between Australian ports, and vessels that
were out at the end of July report fearful weather—not
the sort for a broken-down steamer to roll about in with
impunity. Most seafaring men, when asked their views,
reply with a platitude—if the reply is meant for publica-
tion —about broken tail-shaft, rudder or engine. What
they privately fear is that the VVaratah turned bodily
over. Of late the tendency has been to build passenger
liners up—up and up. Look at the new Orient boats
lying at Circular Quay, Sydney. They are much in
appearance like the Lund boat, and their hurricane decks,
bridge decks, boat decks and so on, are like castles.
They offer a wind surface greater than that of many
a full-rigged ship with all canvas spread. What old sea-
dog would expect his ship under full sail to stand against
a howling gale such as raged on July 27? But the Wara-
tah could not furl its bridge deck, or snug down its
tremendous top-hamper. Give in three things—a tem-
porary breakdown, bunker coal just under the bridge
deck, and empty ballast tanks ; add to these the gale that
was raging, and the Waratah would be in a position in
which only good fortune could avail. The breakdown is
assumed as the best available explanation of the vessel’s
absence. The coal is admitted. The empty ballast tanks
arfe easily possible. In order to trim a ship to its
best draught ballast tanks are often filled and
emptied, and it is quite possible they were empty at
the moment of the hypothetical accident. Whether
the Waratah turns' up or not there is need for inquiry into
the safety, of the hew kind of steamer that looks like an
Alp in the water—the one with the huge wind surfaces
and the funnel which extends up almost to the snow
line. Wireless telegraphy is essential, but it isn’t quite
everything.
* * * *
That suspense which is worse than a
knowledge of the worst is reaping a sad harvest in
Melbourne. Buoyed by the well-meant assertions
of those who must have known better, the wife of
one Melbourne passenger by the Waratah clung
despairingly to the feeblest hopes till, one day,
she found her children putting themselves into
mourning. Then the cord snapped, and now there
is only a poor woman groping in the mental dark.
Another passenger’s relative hung round the office
day after day till she became a pathetic calamity.
Then a brave and kindly skipper took her aside
and told her in so many words that there was no
hope—none whatever. She brokedown completely;
but it probably saved her reason.

A DIFFERENT ROUTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Asked if, in effect, the agreement did not closely
approximate to the Labor party’s Brisbane Conference
proposal, Mr. Deakin admitted that in some measure it
did, though the conclusions arrived at had been reached
independently of that conference’s views, and along
different lines altogether. —Melbourne Age.
Say you have some great objective.
Very well. Be calm, reflective ;
Make no vulgar show of vigor ; ’dsn’t good.
Do not rush the thing directly ;
But approach it circumspectly,
As a gentlemanly politician should.
Though certain consequences hinge upon the laws you
make,
Your prestige in high politics rests with the road you
take.
For the common sort of fellows,
With enthusiastic bellows,
Hush about and shout their schemes in ev’ry ear ;
In their shirt-sleeves, toiling, fretting,
And most vulgarly a-sweating,
Quite without a thought or care how they appear.
And if they do arrive at things a trifle in advance
Their strenuous endeavors go to prove their ignorance.
Have a care for 3’our appearance
If you claim the least adherence
To the genteel game of politics as played
By right-thinking politicians,
Who “ consider their positions ”
Once a week, while common business is delayed.
And shun, O, shun that fearsome fellow eager for a spurt,
And the man who, metaphorically, labors in his shirt.
What though others rush before you ?
What though busy folk ignore you?
Draw your gloves on carefully and take your stick.
Having chosen your direction,
Then proceed, with circumspection,
Stepping out with dignity—but not too quick.
If mere workers are before you, that is what you must
expect;
But reflect, with satisfaction, that your route is more
select.
Then, pray, have no hesitation —
Should you find your destination
Is the same as that of him that humps the load —
In declaring that your action
Gives you perfect satisfaction,
As you reached the place by quite another road.
Ignore his paltry claim to being first—such was his
whim ;
But emphasise the fact that you disdained to follow him.

Were, in the happy days gone by, a dainty

What’ er before me was set down I didn't care
a rap,
When Nellie wore a cotton gown, an apron

For Nellie’s smiles were very sweet and some
were kept for me :
They flavored all / had to eat —made nectar
of the tea.
I envied nobody in town, with springs of

When Nellie wore a cotton gown, an apron
and a cap.
She married well, and left the scene of edibles
and tea

No! No! No! No!
This word is used four times by Prof,
W. Hodgson Ellis, Official Analyst to the
Canadian Government, in reporting the
result of his analyses of Sunlight Soap.
“No unsaponified fat ” ; that means no
waste.
“ No free alkali” ; that means no damage
to clothes or hands.
“No loading mixture ”; that means every
atom is pure soap.
“No adulteration whatever ” ; that means
pure ingredients.
Try Sunlight Soap, and you will see
Prof. Ellis is right. He should know.

Premier Wade, in his capacity c as N.S.W.
Attorney-General, has blundered into further
heavy law costs in connection with the efforts to
extradite Septimus Smith. Septimus, with other
enterprising people at Capetown, sold some land
—through a fictitious person, so it is alleged—to
certain municipalities. It does not appear that
anyone was defrauded of a cent, and the buyers
got what they paid for ; and it seems immaterial
whether the go-between was flesh and blood or
only ink and paper. In fact, the defence theory
is that Smith simply took a Dutch name in order
to appeal to the prejudices of the Dutchmen he
was dealing with. After making the deal Sep-
timus came to Australia, and the Cape people
wanted him returned. He said he felt more com-
fortable here. A magistrate then ordered his
extradition. The Supreme Court (Wade opposing)
ordered the magistrate to re-hear the application.
He was again ordered to be extradited, and the
Supreme Court (Wade still opposing) directed his
release. Wade then appealed to the High Court,
which endorsed the decision of the Supreme
Court, with the usual old Costs. Just by way of
showing hovy utterly incompetent the High Court
and the Privy Council are, somebody blight to ask
in Parliament how many of Wade’s appeals have
been upheld since he has been Attorney-General ;
likewise what the incompetence of the High Court
and the Privv Council have in that wav cost
N.S.W. in Costs.

“ Polsfcan,” from the scene of the wreck :
I visited the scene of the McCallum’s Creek (Vic.) rail-
way smash the day after it happened, and was deeply
impressed with many things. Everybody who was in
that train ought to have a go at “ Tatt.’s ” while his luck
is still in. As a wreck it would be hard to beat, and
yet only minor injuries were received. The driver
was flung into a steel sheep truck, and not through the
door either; he apparently de-materialised himself and
slid between the bars. Half the train bal.nced itself on
the edge of the crazy Yankee bridge, the startled pas-
sengers gazing into a torrent 20ft. deep. A big corridor
car bridged the gap between the break and the bank, one
end resting on a slender hope and the other on the base-
less fabric of a dream. So much for the wreck. Next day
a navvy overbalanced off the.giddy height and smashed on
to the bottom, nearly 60ft. below. Then he got up and
wondered why he wasn’t a remains. A few days later,
the big car slid from the bridge into the gorge, and almost
the only damage it got was a broken window. As for
the construction of the bridge, it amounts to a scandal.
The big stone piers stand almost broadside on to the cur-
rent, and every log or dead animal that ambles down the
stream catches one of the piers on the chest. It vvas this
silly fault in construction that most likely caused the
accident. The steel-work of the bridge is of the flimsiest
description; there being no stays or trusses of any kind.
I’ve seen stronger angle-iron in windmill towers.
Had the upper works been properly stayed and supported,
the loss of a single pier need not have caused the collapse
of the bridge. The crime was imported from America—
why, Heaven no doubt knows. Nobody else seems to.
Pretty well every throne has a Pretender
of a sort hanging round it; and unless he happens
to pretend in Morocco or Turkey he is treated
with a good deal of deference, and has a middling
good time. Bu-Hamara, the spurious Moorish
sovereign, is stated to have borne up and hooted
back at the jeering crowd when he wobbled into
Fez in an iron cage lashed to the back of the
Smell of the Desert. It isn’t even asserted by
the cable that he grovelled when the news was
broken to him about the wooden shirt studded
with nails which the Sultan was fixing up for him
with a view to extracting some information re-
garding his bank account. Probably the thought
that one of his numerous friends might yet do the
same for M. Hafid helped to keep him cheerful
and upright under adverse circumstances. Out-
side Morocco the average Pretender leads a safe
and dignified existence, and even the press of
foreign countries pays him considerable respect.
One of the English illustrateds lately published
the portrait of a society woman with the tag:
“ , who is engaged to Don Oashsale,. Pre-
tender to the throne of Portyerhelm.” It is a
decayed little throne and its salary is mostly in
arrears, but its Pretender is recognised to the
extent that his girl’s photograph is labelled with
his unofficial rank and title.

There was married the other Saturday
afternoon, in the Church of Christ, Campbell-
street, Sydney, a full-blooded Chow to a young
white Australian woman. The bridegroom was a
scholar in the church-class, and the bride was one
of its teachers. This is the third time that the
same thing has happened in this church—the third
time that a young white woman has been handed
over to the embraces of the yellow person ; the
third time the healthy race instincts have been
thrown into the gutter, and the hopes of breeding
a pure white race in these parts have been
smudged. If a White Australia really is this
country’s policy, what’s wrong with fixing up
something sudden in the way of six months’ gaol
or so for the parson who performs a ceremony of
this sort?
“Phil Mite”: Apparently because the
Labor fellow’s opinion is what it is, the Right
Thinking Person nine times out of ten harbors a
sneaking regard for Ah Hook and Turban Singh.
Again and again in country police-courts I have
seen magistrates reluct-
antly fine colored offend-
ers a few shillings for
offences which would
earn white sinners a
“ month’s ” hard and the
wrath of his Worship.
The average policeman
takes his cue from the
Bench. I saw an instance
of it at a Riverina show
a few days ago. Singh
came along to inspect
the fat cow and the prize
bull, but got stranded at
the booth and “had it
the too much beer.”
Then the Singh party
split itself into several
sections and started to
fight. Turbans were trail-
ing all over the place,
and the crowd got ex-
cited. The police came
up majestically, secured
several of the unwashed
rioters and—led them
gently off the ground.
They came back again
and started to trail their
turbans some more, and
the casual white Austra-
lian squirmed as he saw
Singh being again nicely
escorted to the gate and
once more set free. If
a plain white “drunk”
had done half as much
he would have been
handcuffed in about five
minutes and dragged off
with great display. Next
day he would have been
asked to plead to drunk-
enness, riotous behavior,
horrible language and
“tearin me uniform.” And Heaven only knows
what might happen to him at the hands of some
of the J’s.P. in these Cow districts.
The industrial position of the Melbourne
Metrop. Gas Co.’s suburban employees bears a
drearily close resemblance to that of the Sydney
clerks. Like the clerks, the majority of the gas-
men yearn for a Wages Board to regulate condi-
tions in their trade. As in the case of the clerks,
there are, among the gasites, a certain number of
trucklers who howl aloud that they don’t want a
Wages Board, and that their only desire in life is
to graft doggedly on at whatever screw the co.
sees fit to offer. The co.’s generosity doesn’t
appear to go many miles. Thus unpleasant
accusations are rife in gas circles concerning seven
days-a-week work; overtime without extra pay; not
to mention an alleged disproportion of boy to full-
grown labor and other items of the sort. Mean-
'vhlleT"'l!,ch is / et another point of resemblance
to the oydney cleric s position—a circular or peti-
tion repudiating all desire for a Wages Board
drifts about among the employees and cordially
invites their signatures.

A NATURAL WOMAN.
HE: “ Very true to nature that Madam Darti.”
SHE: “ Hm-m, yes; if it's true there’s no waist in nature .”
Capt. Neitenstein, Controller of the Ma
State’s Prisons, says that N. S. Wales was the first
country in the world to adopt the whole-hog
“ Kathleen Mavourneen”—the imprisonment that
may be to-day and may be for ever. Every
country that tried it before had, he says,
a maximum and a minimum ; but N. S. Wales
just shoots them in, and makes no promise one
way or the other. In that respect, it treats them
just the same as it does the insane person
whose bee buzzes some other way. When a count
was made the other day there were 32 “ Kathleen
Mavourneens” in force, and it was quite certain
that a good many of the 32 people who owned
them ought to have been in a lunatic asylum long
ago—among them at least two sexual perverts
who have been convicted again and again of rape
or attempted rape. First cousin to the “Kath-
leen Mavourneen ” is the Ma State’s new system
under which the Court can declare a prisoner an
habitual if he or she —mostly she—has been “up”
three times inside a year for drunkenness,
plain or striped ; but 12 months is the most
for which the Court can invite the victim
inside. A few amazing records have come to
light under this system. There is one woman
of 37 who has served 240 sentences, practically all
in the last 18 years—mostly plain drunk ; only
once has she been charged with crime. Even
that record is knocked out by another lady who
has had 337 convictions, and not a crime among
them ; but she has had 46 years to do it in.
Another, only 28, has had 95 convictions; but
there is a bit more of the criminal about this one.
If the N.S.W. prison system can make anything
respectable out of this materia], it will deserve to
rank as the most ingenious manufacturer in the
Commonwealth.

SHORT SOUP.
[For The Bulletin.!
Ah Hing was charged with having sold intoxicating
liquor without a license. A revenue officer, in company
with a detective, visited his shop and called for Short
Soup. They were supplied with several bottles of ale.—
News Item.
The heathen Chinee
Is “ peculiar,” so we
Were assured long ago
By one in the know ;
In his very dark ways
He’ll employ a dark phrase
To deceive—that’s his trade—
Thus the law to evade.
But I praise him right here,
For his new name for beer—
Which he sells when he ain’t
Got a license—it’s quaint
Short Soup!
I thank you, Ah Iling ;
It’s a singular thing,
I’ve wanted to use
A fresh name for Booze
(Vulgar word !), I may say,
For many a day ;
And now when I roam—
Very late towards my home,
And the tottering street
Throws me off my feet,
And the cop asks “ Wot.’s up?"
I’ll reply : “ Had a cup
Sor’ Shoop! ”
It’s so easy to.say,
In an innocent way.
When I get to,the door
Of my mansion at four
* A.m., and then see
Wifey waiting for me.
I shall have a new wheeze
To crack—sure to please.
(If it don’t, Ah Hing may
Hear from me next day !)
She’ll sniff and guess “ Beer !”
I’ll correct her : “ Wrong, dear—
Sor’ Shoo’! ”

A year ago the Yankee Fleet
Was welcomed in our bays.
We kissed the sailors in the street,
We gave them fatted calf to eat,
And pampered them with praise..
And many little damsels sweet
Returned their ardent gaze.
Yah ho 1 for warm confiding lips,.
Yah ho ! for budding charms.
With ready jests and Yankee quips
Those rapid wooers from the ships
Allayed the girls’ alarms
Who now bewail their little slips
With baby in their arms.
The lass who loved a sailor bold
Wears ashes on her head ;
The fires of yester-year are cold ;
Though only seventeen years old,
She wishes she were dead ;
But baby's worth its weight in gold,
And baby must be fed.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Up to the present the season is shaping very
well. There have been from fair to extra-good rains in
almost all parts, and if another little lot falls in a couple of
months, Australia should have about a record wheat
harvest, and the squatter should be also feeling good.
True, so far as the squatter is concerned, prices are not
going the right way. In S. Australia especially there has
been a heavy slump in lambs, The wool market is all
right ; but many breeders have stocked up so high that
they can’t carry the lambs as well as the ewes, and the
market has been knocked clean out. There has been a
craze lately for lamb-breeding ; but already some people
are finding that the game isn’t all that it looked. How-
ever, generally speaking, the outlook is good, and State
revenues are buoyant:—
State Revenues for August.
1908. 1909.
N. S. Wales ~ £1,010,064 £1,084,036 1nc.£73,972

The previous month wasn’t so good ; hence the next com-
parison is less cheerful: —
State Revenues for Year so Par.

N. S. Wales .. £2,079,381 £2,047,224 Dec.£32,157

From the Commonwealth, N. S. Wales has got £33,428
less, while Victoria has got £34,321 more than in the first
two months of the previous year. This isn’t, however,
another injustice to the Ma State. From first to last the
Commonwealth overpaid the States £6,059,088; and of
that lot N. S. Wales got £3,033,972, or more than half.
S. Australia is still too busy attending the tea-meeting to
furnish figures oftener than once in three months.
There are mostly blanks where the State ex-
penditure figures ought to be. Three States alone furnish
particulars:—
State Expenditure for Year so Far.
July-Aug., July-Aug.,
1908. 1909.
N. S. Wales .. £2,201,516 £2,036,959 Dec. £164,527
Queensland .. 513,444 508,036 Dec. 5,408
Westralia .. 489,833 492,070 Inc. 2,237
That, of course, is apart from loan expenditure ; also in
the case of N. S. Wales it is apart from expenditure out
of the Public Works Fund. Out of the latter £193,820
has been paid this year. This fund is fed by votes from
the revenue, and it also gets a fraction of the proceeds of
land sales. On loan account N. S. Wales has so far spent
£353,113 this year, as against £372,722 in the first two
months of last year. In both cases the expendi-
ture is at the rate of over two millions a year.
For some time past, instead of actually raising the money,
the State’s Loan account has been borrowing from the
accumulated surpluses (including proceeds of land sales)
of the past few years ; but as the toe of that stocking was
in sight, and as J. Bull is steadily putting up his rates, the
Treasury lately decided to offer J per cent, more to the local
Money-bags. Queensland is again rushing out loan money
—£217,669 has been spent in the two months. Presumably
some of this is old loan money which has been released
by the Q. N. Bank under the agreement fixed up by the
canny Eidston two or three years ago. No other States
furnish any loan figures whatever. They are probably
ashamed of them.

The profits are shown net—that is, after a half-yearly
appropriation for wear and tear of machinery. For the
past term this was £3OOO ; for the corresponding half of
1908, £2500. Whether anything else is charged direct to
profit and loss or working account does not transpire.
Something certainly ought to be thus charged. The
greater part of the assets are lumped in one item,
“ Brewery, plant and machinery, goodwill (£22,500), roll-
ing stock, freehold and leasehold properties, £341,494.”
Leaseholds are a wasting asset, and should be steadily
written down. Presumably they are —there is no
evidence one way or the other. Still, lease-
holds were not a big item when the co. was
formed in 1902; the assets were then valued at
£477,932, of which only £14,250 was represented by
leaseholds, all leaseholds having less than 21 years to
run being given in. On the strength of that valuation,
and on the strength also of average annual profits of
£55,001 for the previous 31 years, the co. was floated with
a capital of £550,000, of which the Tooheys took 375,000
shares and £175,000 in cash. Somehow the previous
profits were never realised ; and so in 1906 the capital
was written down to £400,000, the Toohey estates sur-
rendering 50,000 shares, and all other shares being writ-
ten down from 20s. to 16s. Originally the goodwill item
had totalled £72,068 plus certain preliminary expenses.
When the revaluation took place, the total item was
written down to £22,500; and against that there
are now: Goodwill reserve, £7500; formal reserve,
£22,500; undivided profit, £13,677. Wiping good-
will out altogether, reserves of £21,177 are left.
Besides earning the dividend and the special appropria-
tions which have been made against depreciation, £37,408
has been stored up in the past 3£ years. No doubt every-
thing really depends on the valuations. City and suburban
freehold hotels in 1902 were set down at £124,300 ; and in
these latter days of special licensing courts and the
wholesale reduction of licenses, very serious things could
happen to £124,300 worth of pub property. Nor is it
certain that the reductions have reached their end. But
while this may capsize the value of the hotel premises, so
that large amounts of profit may have to be written off
to make good the shrinkages, it isn’t at all certain that
the beer-brewing profits will be much affected ; for on
the Maoriland figures it is clear that if people can’t drink
in a pub they will drink at home, supposing drink is to be
got; and while some won’t drink as much, others will
drink more. One more Toohey comparison, and then we
can turn off the tap (it isn’t worth while going back
beyond 3906 on account of the revaluation which hap-
pened then): —
July, 1906. July, 1908. July, 1909.
Liabilities, less cash £55,569 £71,133 £49,851
Brewery, hotels, etc. 322,139 338,178 341,494
Stocks.' 50,945 67,444 59,836
Book debts .. .. 100,882 105,348 105,197
Gross Profits on
Brewings, etc. .. 31,629 41,480 44,3/1
So, though there has been a slight reduction of debt,
practically all the undivided profit is being put back into
the business; and that takes some of the gilt off the im-
provement in net profits. For obviously it would be
better if the profits went on improving notwithstanding
that the undivided balances were put into public stocks
or other gilt-edged securities. As it is, the profits in-
crease, but the risks increase too.
