The original co. was an amalgamation of the shipping
businesses of H. R. Reid and Co., A. and J. Mclntyre,
and Deane and Co. The capital was £75,000, and all the
shares were cut up among the vendors. Later on—in
1904—the properties having been put in at a low valua-
tion, and the co. having had a good deal of luck, the
£75,000 of reserves were capitalised in a nominally-new
co., making the total capital £150,000. Then, 12 months
ago, £20,000 more was raised by the issue of 2000 shares
at par. On the whole £170,000 the present div. is at the
rate of 8 per cent. ; on the old shares their part
of the div. is equal to 16 per cent. The pro-
fits and reserves stated above are open to much
suspicion. Steamships, freeholds, works, etc., are taken
in at cost; and that means cost to the new co. Of the
present total of £226,840, this co. in 1904 took over
£127,118 from the old co. Those assets, after five years’
humping, are not likely to be worth £127,118 now.
Against the assets “at cost” the co. has a depreciation
reserve of £38,550, £5OOO having been added during the
past six months. That £5OOO is equal to a shade under 4£
percent, per annum ; and if that is a fair thing—and it is-
probably more than enough, seeing that the total in-
cludes freeholds—then the actual net profit last half-year
was £8405. The co. has gone ahead rapidly. As shown
just now, steamships, freeholds, works, etc., have moved
along from £127,118 at the end of 1904 to £226,840 now,
part of the increase being provided out of the new capital.
But this shows the movements better: —
Dec.,’o4. Dec.,’oB. June,’o9.
Liabilities £12,796 £34,922 £32,160
Steamers, freeholds, plant,
etc 127,118 225,815 226,840
Stores 8,881 14,596 17,532
Book debts 9,267 27,917 20,846
Liquid assets .. .. 30,904 3,364 9,592
Even though a fair amount of the £226,840 may have to
be written off for wear and tear, still the figures show a
very large increase in earning power. And so long as the
inter-State shipping cos. keep their hands off one
another’s throats, as they have been doing since
they fixed up the agreement a few years ago,
there seems to be a good time still ahead of
them ; for Federation brought inter-State freetrade, and
brought it for keeps. And nobody benefited by it more
than the shipping cos. Certainly this co. doesn’t work
under the shipping agreement; but it doesn’t work
against it, and the fact that it isn’t formally a member
seems to make no difference.

Just before the foregoing list opens the co. had over-
hauled its affairs, and found it necessary to write
£12,500 off capital, and £5200 off reserves. Up to that
time the capital had been £112,500; but as £62,500 had
been provided by the sale of some of the co.’s land during
the boom, the writing-down in 1902 wasn’t so distressing
as it might have been. When the capital was “ watered ”
the balance of the land was valued on the boom basis ;
and that is really the chief of the co.’s troubles—the tre-
mendous load of capital and debt represented by land
and premises. Down to 1902 the Flinders-street land was
valued in the books at £48,000, and the store premises at
£53,976, their cost. A trifle was written off these amounts ;
but the real estate item still stands as high at £103,625,
and that is apart from furniture and fittings. This is
equivalent to an excessive rent-charge on the business
of, say, £5500 a year. The capital in the land and
premises is greater than the co.’s own capital ; therefore
all the other capital necessary—in the shape of stock,
book-debts, etc.—has to be represented either by reserves
or liabilities. As reserves have grown, it has been possible
to pay off some of the debt. This shows the movement:
July, July, Jan., July,
1902. 1908. 1909. 1909.
Mortgage debt .. £69,500 £64,500 £63,500 £62,500
Other debts.. .. 15,761 20,226 22,478 22,677
Freehold, plant, etc. 110,000 113,990 115,953 115,803
Stock 59,736 60,330 63,121 64,029
Book debts .. .. 20,616 25,442 23,838 25,090
But, though the reserves grow, the liabilities can’t be
reduced at quite the same rate, because the business calls
for more capital in other ways—in stock, for instance,
and in financing customers. Still some progress is being
made, and everj' step forward, by reducing the interest
bill, makes it easier to take another step. Just at present
the co. is adding to its premises ; and very wisely, in view
of the high figure at which property already stands, a
start has been made to pay for it out of profits : £l4OO is
thus written off this half-year, and there will remain
about the same amount to be dealt with later on. In the
last few years the profits have picked up wonderfully,
assuming that the stock valuations have been made on
the same basis. (Apparent profits can be inflated, of
course, by over-valuing stock ; and as to that one has to
trust the management entirely.) The margin of profit in
excess of the dividend is now wide, and it was wider in
the past term than for any term of which this paper has
a record ; and that is over 15 years.

Fire years ago there were 20,000 ordinary shares in this
co. paid to £4. In January, 1907, more capital being re-
quired, a preference issue was decided upon; 20,000
shares were authorised, but so far only 12,915 have been
issued, and are paid at present to £2 ss. The ordinaries
are liable for £l, and the prefs. for £2 15s. each ; and
against this liability £7456 has been lodged on deposit.
In 1907-8, apparently on account of the temporary slump
in wool, profits were unusually good ; but during the
past year higher prices for materials and the generally
distressed condition of Maoriland make things look very
sick again. In fact, they are probably sicker than the fore-
going table shows, because of the amount written off £2OOO
came off merchandise stock, and that might very well have
been chopped off profits at once. It simply means that,
on revaluation, profits are found to be £2OOO less than they
have hitherto been reckoned. However, the item goes in
among the amounts written off because it was probably
in previous years that the over-valuation of stocks, and
therefore of profits, was made. Another £127, “ Pref.
share flotation expenses,” is shown as written off. This
only leaves £lOOO provided to cover the year’s wear and
tear of plant, etc., which is probably insufficient. Still,
assuming that it is sufficient, there was a net profit of
only £3277 ; and yet the co. pays a dividend of 6 percent,
all round, ealling'for £6114. If the co. wants to get into
difficulties it seems to be going the right way about it.
One thing in its favor is that by getting in the new
capital and by reducing stocks it has been able to ease off
its liabilities a little ; but they are still substantial: —
1904 .. £35,795 I 1907 .. £70.602
1905 .. 42,862 1908 .. 67,359
1906 .. 63,724 1 1909 .. 42,516
Maoriland’s imports of woollens and stuff that masque-
rades as woollens are still heavy, which suggests that the
Dominion hasn’t yet hit the foreign shoddy manufacturer
hard enough. In 1898 woollen piece-goods valued at
£266,516 were imported; last year the total was
£318,669 ; and in 10 years the total for apparel—
which doesn’t include drapery, linens and haber-
dashery—has sprung from £506,620 to £972,775.
If they had the market to themselves, the Maoriland
woollen cos. might still do fairly well even during the
period of hard and imperative economy which is certainly
ahead of the country ; but the 20 per cent, duty clearly
isn’t enough to secure the market in view of the good labor
conditions which Maoriland insists on in its own factories,
but which it unfortunately can’t insist on in the other
fellows’ factories.

Of these reserves £2015 is a provision for the interest pre-
sently payable on debentures, and would probably be
just as well out of the way. The other reserves are—For-
mal reserves, £45,000 ; dividend equalisation, £5OOO ; de-
benture redemption, £4000; and undivided profit, £3542.
TwelvO months ago it was casually remarked here that
profits, of course, depended on the valuation of stock,
etc. ; if the valuations were over the mark, then the
profits had been over-stated. During the year—probably
in consequence of the death of Mr. Jas. Macdougall, for
many years the co.’s managing director —a re-valuation
was made ; and besides the £4041 written off out of the
current year’s gross profits, it is found necessary to write
off £7971 of previous profits. This is a thing that is always
liable to happen in connection with a business carrying
heavy stock-in-trade and plant. The co.’s paid capital is
£111,225 in 8898 shares of £l2 10s. each, and liable for
another £l2 10s. There is £50,000 owing on debentures,
which have a mortgage on the uncalled capital; and other
debts and sundry movements are shown hereunder :
1902. 1905. 1908. 1909.
Debentures .. £50,000 £50,000 £50,000 £50,000
Other liabilities.. 66,304 54,305 71,020 57,827
Stock .. ... 91,356 88,480 129,694 117,874
Plant and fixtures 51,217 50,260 61,685 52,462
Freeholds, etc. .. 59,294 58,500 68,172 79,092
Book debts, etc... 60,065 49,711 35,388 31,961
From the first table it will be seen that, after the various
writings-off, £45,436 has been added to the reserves during
the past seven years, while in the same period the
dividends have absorbed only £38,927 ; that is
to say, the net profits (assuming that the valua-
tions are all right) have been more than enough to
pay 10 per cent., though only 5 per cent, has actually
been paid. Very little of the surplus profit has, however,
been available for paying off liability ; practically all of
it has gone into more plant and stock. This, of course,
is adding eggs to those which the shareholders have in
the basket already ; and that’s all right, no doubt, pro-
vided they hatch out more chickens than would other-
wise be produced. But if it takes the extra money to
produce the old profits, then the shareholders are
not much better off for having nominally earned
the extra money. What the future of this or any
other industrial business is going to be it would take a
very old-established prophet to tell ; so much depends on
the personal equation, and that is always changing. One
thing in favor of this co. is that there is no goodwill item
whatever in its books, so all the capital is represented by
things that you can feel if you run against them in the
dark.

Ik 1904 the old £5 shares were surrendered, and two £1
shares were given instead of them. The £3 per share
thus written off capital allowed of such a severe writing-
down of foreclosed properties—from £42,128 to £26,500 —
that sales which have been made since then have shown a

whether it would be worth while. To secure economy,

and shareholders, the amalgamation of two or for 6 ® °!
these much-reduced societies is called for. A good deal
has been done in that way already ; but there is still
room for more of the same kind of thing.

The capital of this co. is £50,000, in 100,000 shares paid to
10s., and liable for another 10s. For some years the
capital was £27,500 ; then a trifle more was got in and
£19,250 of the reserves was capitalised. On the amount
actually paid, the present div. is therefore equal to over
9 per cent. The co. trades largely on borrowed money.
Even now, though it has been reducing its advances and
paying off some of its own borrowings, its debts equal
very nearly £3 for every £1 of its own capital and reserves.
Its advances and properties total £209,845 ; so for every
£1 of its own it has £3 13s. 6d. out in these shrinkable
classes of security. So long as the depositors don’t want
their money—in other words, so long as the co. can nurse
the securities—everything may be all right; but if it
became necessary for the co. to get in money rapidly,
heavy losses would probably be made ; and those losses
would have to be borne by the shareholders. In good
times the shareholders have all the best of it. They get
9 per cent, while the depositor gets only from 3£ to 4$
per cent. But when the bad time comes, then the de-
positor scores, for he holds a first mortgage not only over
the profits but over the shareholders’ capital as well. In
this case the position is that the co. on June 30 owed
(apart from investors’ subscriptions) £166,217 ; its liquid
assets (including public debentures) totalled £16,360. To
represent the balance —£149,857 —there were £209,846 of
advances and properties ; which means that all these
shrinkable assets could shrink on an average about ss. lOd.
in the £ below their present book values before the de-
positors’money would be in danger. And then there is
the £50,000 of capital still uncalled; so that it would
have to be a very shocking slump—amounting to just
about 10s. in the £—before depositors would have to get
out their sackcloth and seek the ash barrel. As for
shareholders in M.L. mortgage cos.—well, it all depends
on what sort of management their affairs have had.

In the calculation of working costs in concen-
trating’ mills and workshops, every small item should be
taken into consideration. It is the small and uncon-
sidered factors, involving waste of power, that make for
high working costs. The type of bearing employed has a
marked effect on the power consumed in overcoming
friction. Recent exhaustive teRts emphasised the marked
superiority of the Hyatt Flexible Roller Bearings over all
other types of roller, sleeve, and other anti-friction
bearings. Under equal conditions the loss of power due
to friction in Hyatt Flexibles is 23 per cent, less than
with the best solid roller bearing, and 65 per cent, less
that with babbit metal journals. Messrs. W. and J.
Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, will be
glad to supply further information to those interested.*

The erection of the new 300-ton Blast Smelting
Furnace for the Great Fitzroy mine, Q., is now rapidly
approaching completion at the mine. The dimensions of
this furnace are as follows—168in. x 44in., and 24ft.
high to feed floor. It is water jacketed from top to
bottom. These jackets are made of steel plates flanged
in one piece. The furnace was designed by the mine
superintendent, and has many features of excellence and
novelty in the detail of its construction. The jackets are
made interchangeable, only one type and size of jacket
being used ; and they will further be constructed so that
they are reversible from top to bottom—thus practically
the life of the jackets is doubled. This furnace, as well as
the previous one, was built at the works of the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne.* [2

The Broken Hill South Blocks, formerly part
of the South, were taken over by a Melbourne co. in
1900 ; but by 1905 the concern had got into very low
water. The Lake View Consols then put £50,000 into the
co., which was reconstructed, the management being
handed over to 8., M. and Co. With plenty of capital
behind it, this firm set to work to develop the mine and
provide it with the most compact plant at Broken Hill.
The capital expenditure has been very heavy, but as the
following shows, a great deal of the money has been
provided by the mine itself (the first period covers 17
months): —
Written
March, Profit. Divs. off. Reserves.
1907 .. £8,033 Nil £3,487 £4,546
1908 .. 29,566 Nil 29,234 4,878
1909 .. 26,031 Nil 18,900 12,009
So £51,621 has been written off, of which £36,192 is the
amount spent during the past two years in equipment;
the balance is money spent on development. The original
“property” item was £152,445. A further £99,175 has
been spent, including £36,176 on milling plant, and
£10,571 on main shaft equipment; but all this has been
written down to £47,554 out of profits. It is plain,
therefore, that, as the capital expenditure has now practi-
cally stopped, the past results are no guide to the future ;
for the nearly £40,000 which has gone into plant alone in
the past two years will not have to be spent again.
The South Blocks’ financial position was like
this at the end of March, 1908 and 1909 respectively :
1908. 1909.
Liabilities .. ..£10,159 .. £23,521
Liquid assets.. .. 9,712 .. 33,071
Deficit .. .. £447 Surplus .. £9,550
That is very good progress considering that during the
period £10,502 had to be paid on account of milling plant
alone. The output has been gradually brought up during
the year from 7006 tons in April to 10,140 tons in June.
Taking April, May and June these results were got, esti-
mating lead at £l3, and silver at 2s. Ore handled,
28,004 tons ; net value of output, £27,855; total cost
(including development), £21,764; total profit, £6091.
London office expenses, royalty, etc., may be taken at
£650, reducing the profit to £5441 ; and that would
amount to 3s. lOd. per ton. On March 31 the ore reserves
were 421,090 tons =at 3s. lOd. per ton, £80,709. Add sur-
plus of liquid assets, £9550, and we get a total value of
£90,259. There are 200,000 shares, quoted at 335. =
£330,000. So we get this
Market value of mine £330,000
Less value of ore reserves and liquid assets 90,259
Balance to represent future developments in
mine and metal markets .. .. ~ £239,741
The future development may be very extensive ; for no
work had been done away from the shaft below 526 ft.
Yet clearly the market at present prices has discounted
the future very heavily. Chairman Govett, at the
meeting in London, worked out the profits at £2200 a
month, equal to 13 per cent, per annum. On the fore-
going, based on three months’ actual results, the profits
would be £lBl4 per month. But taking Govett’s figures,
the weak point is that the reserves would only keep the
mill going for (say) 3f years. To make the mine worth
what it stands at in the market, the ore reserves would
have to be increased from 421,000 tons to 1,669,000 tons.
No doubt the situation may be improved (1) by the co.
being able to turn the zinc tailings to account;
by improvements in the metal market; (3) by further
economies, in metal saving, etc. But it is as well to
remember that the metal markets may fall as well as rise.
These estimates are made on the basis' of lead at £l3, and
as a matter of fact lead is even now worth only £12125. 6d.
* * * *
After weary years of sinking, the Broken Hill
South Extended has struck what appears to be a lode at
a depth of about BQoft. The old shafts put down on the
property never happened on anything big enough to raise
the wind, and if the present manager hadn’t been blessed
with a remarkably complacent and affluent Board of
directors in London he would probably have been called
back long ago. At the same time it must be remembered
that the shaft in which the lode has been cut is very
small, and so far no true idea of what the ore body is
really like can be got. There was certainly no justifica-
tion whatever for the boom which took place in the
shares. At 4s. a share the show was given a value of
£135,000, for there are no less than 675,007 shares in the co.
» * * *
In consequence of the flattering but quite in-
conclusive tale told by the find in the South Extended,
Broken Hill, the dry bones of all the old mining shows in
the vicinity are doing another rattle. The mining in-
vestor who knows anything about Broken Hill conditions
isn’t likely to be easily charmed by the sound, because he
will reason that where the workable lodes are situated
1000 ft. or more from the surface, the cost of mining is
likely to be proportionately high ; further, Broken Hill
ores, in almost every case, become more and more re-
fractory the deeper you go. So it generally happens that
the exploiting of these shows is left to men who take the
bait, but know how to swear off the hook.

The new De Bavay co. to get out zinc from the
Broken Hill tailings is to be called the Amalgamated Zinc
(De Bavay’s) Ltd., and its 500,000 shares are to be cut up
this way:—
Shareholders in present co. 247,500
Underwritten at par by the Baillieus .. .. 122,500
W. L. Baillieu for guaranteeing overdraft, etc. 7,500
Held in reserve 122,500
Total .. .. 500,000
The £122,500 now to be put up will go in paying for the
new plant at the South Broken Hill and in paying for tail-
ings. The shareholders will get 2f shares for each share
in the old co.
An inspection of the .True Blue, Wyalong
(N.S.W.), has been made on behalf of the Harden G. M.
Co., with a view to purchase. The mine has a very good
surface equipment; but some money needs to be put
into it to pay off liabilities and develop more milling ore.
According to men who ought to know. Great
Cobar (N.S.W.) is going to steadily rush along its output
somehow or anyhow. Latest monthly return is 540 tons
copper, containing 14780 z. gold and 8650 z. silver. Aver-
age gold value was about £ll per ton of copper; but
unless the proportion of purchased ore, the total tonnage,
and the working costs are given, the return conveys
mighty little real information. Latterly the big mine
has been a steady buyer of copper ore and concentrates,
and as there are numerous outside shows with picked ore
for sale, a considerable customs business may be ex-
pected. Under these circumstances, the supercilious
neglect of details in the returns is to be regretted. And
while it continues the public had better be careful.

Mining cos. reserves are a doubtful quantity, as a rule ;
they consist most often of machinery and of money spent
on making holes; and sometimes the holes are of value
and sometimes they are not. In this case, the position
had better be stated this way :
Dec.,’o6. Dec.,’oB. June,’o9.
Liabilities .. Nil Nil Nil
Liquid assets .. £lOOl £5494 £2640
Stores and fuel (£1335) are not included with the assets.
So, though in 2£ years the reserves have nominally in-
creased from £7713 to £20,916, the surplus of liquid assets
has only gone up from £lOOl to £2640. All the rest has
gone into machinery or holes. The ore reserves “ capable
of measurement" amounted on June 30 to 7000 tons,
worth 535. 9d. per ton—very little, considering that during
the half-year 5896 tons, worth 84s. Bd. per ton, were
milled. Six months ago the figures were 7582 tons, worth
775. On actual figures the position is not at all good ;
but, according to the manager, “the outlook below No. 4
level is very promising, and as No. 5 level is driven it is
believed that ample reserves will be developed.” Anyhow,
the co. is risking it, and is putting in a new crushing
plant. People who handle these shares ought, however,
to realise that not more than about one-eighth of the
market value of the mine is in sight—the oth r seven-
eighths is mostly faith; and, in mining, faith can be
carried much too far.
* * * *
Horton and Freeman made a mistake over
Lobb’s Hole (N.S. W.), and as they admit their “ consider-
able responsibility,” they are ladling money out of their
own pockets so that the mine and the shareholders may
have a run. Up to the end of June they had advanced
£4520, and after two calls lately authorised have been
got in they have undertaken to finance the show till the
end of the year. It is pretty certain that it never ought
to have been passed on to a big co. (the paid capital is
£48,000). The very narrow vein of rich ore paid the
former private owners splendidly, and would have paid
them still better if the business management had been
up-to-date; but that is about the full strength of
the claim’s possibilities. Horton and Freeman provided
for treatment on the mine ; and,'especially now that the
bottom level has gone back on them, there isn’t the ore
to keep even a small furnace going. Things may improve
again ; but it looks like a fossicker’s mine. Last half-
year’s actual expenditure, including development, was
£3875; and the revenue from sales of matte was £1716,
partly from ore previously mined.
Great Boulder (W:A.) has won a ringing cheer
in the West by getting a good bore-hole development at
2600 ft., or 100 ft. below the present bottom level. About
18ft. of lodestuff was pierced by the drill, and 3ft. of it
assayed lloz. per ton. The remainder of the core, how-
ever, only went sdwt. Coming on top of good finds at
2000 ft. in the Horseshoe and 1970 ft. in the Ivanhoe, this
discovery has made Kalgoorlie feel very good. But the
line of ore-bearing country which has furnished these
developments is by far the most persistent on the field,
and, though it is likely to go down as far as it is possible
to work it, it cannot he argued from that fact that shoots
in other lines of ore-bearing country will do the same.
They may, or they may not.
Golden Links, Ltd. (W. A.), has been induced
by the Adelaide and Melbourne Exchanges to accept the
final call of Is. on shares held in Australia, making them
fully paid. Seeing, however, that the scrip on the
London register is only dealt in as carrying the liability,
and as settlements have been obtained on this basis, the
London Board advises that paid-up Australian shares will
not be regarded as “satisfactory delivery” on the
London Exchange. But what the law has to say about
this boycott is another matter. It certainly seems
absurd to say that paid-up shares are not good enough to
be accepted as good delivery. There was a Tasmanian
case which hung on t. is point some time ago. A little
crowd engineered a corner in certain contributing
shares, and when the spec.-sellers went to buy-in in order
to deliver the goods, they couldn’t find any contributing
scrip anywhere. Several of them anted-up whatever the
corner-men demanded ; but one man bought paid-up
scrip and tendered it. Of course, the corner crowd said
that that wasn’t any good to them. “ All right! ” said
the operator ; “ then sue me.” They did ; and the Court
held that the tender was quite good.
Golden Links has lately been doing some
further experimenting with the drill below 500 ft. Two
separate ore channels were passed through in 177 ft. of
boring; but beyond a few feet of payable stuff and a
high assay here and there, nothing very cheering was
met. Still on one borehole a co. cannot tell how much
gold is ahead of the pick any more than on one look a
man can tell how many stars are overhead. The mine
has good prospects in depth, and but for the delay in
erecting a mill shares would be standing at a better price
than they are.
» » » *
In order to boost along the production of pyritic
ores and their subsequent treatment at certain proposed
phosphatic manure works, the Moore Government (W.A.)
has decided to charge a specially low rate for the railage
of this class of stuff to the coast. This concession, it is
reckoned, will encourage the development of several well-
known low-grade pyritous copper lodes in the State, and
prevent the importation of such ore from other parts of
the Commonwealth.
* * * *
Wiluna (W.A.) is now being riddled with bore
holes. The first stab proved (as far as a bore can) that
the Gwalia Consolidated shoot maintains its width and
value down to 500 ft., and a second one is alleged to have
corroborated this. Other work has been promised the
drill-owner; and when the last mail left this distant
settlement, everybody was talking to everybody else
about the efficacy of bore-hole prospecting, and prepara-
tions were being made for a general and glorious drunk.
As regards the Gwalia Consolidated, a reliable man on the
spot writes to say that the property can stand the erection
of a big plant; but funds are short, and the Board won’t
hustle, anyway.
» * * *
Opinions differ widely as to the merits of
Meekatharra (W.A.). The other day the local paper
billed it as a second Kalgoorlie on the word of some
visitor ; and now one of the out-of-works, who were
attracted there by this dazzling assertion, wants to know
whether the recent visitor had an axe of his own to grind
or was paid to grind someone else’s. This particular
unfortunate alleges that there is not one defined body of
quartz or lodestuff in the whole district, and he declares
with much vehemence that Meekatharra hasn’t a ghost of
a show of ever being even a pocket addition of Kalgoorlie.
In fact, in his opinion, the reefs and lodes in the handful
of shows working there can only be trusted as far as they
can be seen.
* • • *
Hampton Plains Estate (W.A.) is again on the
cadge. The annual report submitted on July 19 shows
that the cash raised by reconstruction in 1906 has
vanished, and the directors now want more. The chief
asset is the Lady Miller, on which £lO,OOO has been spent;
and though it has, confessedly, not realised expectations,
it is now proposed to float the show as a separate under-
taking. Evidently the authors of the report think that
the hook must have more bait, so they proceed to recom-
mend the purchase of 3825 acres of gold-bearing country
in Uruguay. About £600,000, it is alleged, has been
spent on this property by French concerns;
and although no dividends have been paid, hundreds of
thousands of pounds are said to be going to waste for the
want of suitable treatment appliances. To buy a sub-
stantial interest in this dazzling venture and give the
holders of the £95,420 worth of debentures something on
account, shareholders are asked to fork out another 3s. 6d.
per share. But, in view of the oo.’s past history, share-
holders ought first to insist on having the statements and
promises supported by some independent authorities.

£70,000 of profit in the mine, though no doubt some of
this will have to go in machinery renewals, etc.
* * * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, has continued
the eastern drive from the shaft at the 830 ft. level with
poor results. The quartz vein, although it carries “ fair
gold,” is narrow, and only 10 tons have been raised.
Crosscutting into the footwall from No. 5 near the
Brilliant Freehold boundary has not put life into the
market, which is beginning to ask the Towers for some-
thing more edible than promises. Brilliant Deeps have
now joined the deep-sinkers ; the underlay is going down
below 2413 ft. vertical. There is a large reef of fair
mineral stone, but this is only another case of “ pro-
mises.” Queen Cross is driving on a formation at
2310 ft. ; the ground is hard and the vein of white quartz
is not encouraging. Bonnie Dundee, on the strength of
a good crushing and a sixpenny dividend, has been in
more favor. Three levels are being pushed out in search
of something new to the east, for the bottom levels have
been disappointing.
• * » «
Mount Morgan’s return for August was £88,300
(taking copper at £59); this is £12,000 less than the July
record. In the latter month there was an unusually large
amount of gold (6820z.)in furnace bottoms and other odd
corners. Of copper ore 20,354 tons gave 649 tons copper
and 72140 z. gold. The average value extracted was about
605., which is nearly double the average from Great Cobar
or Mount Lyell, and considerably better than Great
Fitzroy’s. Of gold ore only 16,432 tons were treated, and
the return was down to £21,880. Even this is better than
was expected some time since, thanks to a patch of good
ore lately discovered in the upper levels.
* * * *
The reported discovery of gold on the railway
construction works between Mount Chalmers and Yep-
poon (Central Queensland) is not surprising. Years ago,
fossickers were working the gullies in the neighborhood,
and hundreds got a good living for a time. Even to this
day a crushing goes through occasionally from small
leaders near Cawarral ; and it is not forgotten that when
Captain Osborne took over the Fitzroy for Philip
Charley and himself, their intentions were officially stated
to be to work it as a gold mine." Hitherto, however, the
Rockhampton district outside Mount Morgan has been
mainly notable for first-class failures and high-class
swindles.
* * * *
0.K., the North Queensland copper show which
has just emerged from reconstruction, continues its
smelting career, although there is every reason for shut-
ting down the furnace promptly. Last published return
was 693 tons of ore for 35 tons blister copper. Quite a
mouthful is still made of the gold and silver contents of
some of the copper; but it seems humorous to report
about £5O worth of precious metals, especially as they
probably cost the co. about £7O to get. For O.K. to be
smelting a 5J per cent, mixture means that the good cash
put up under the reconstruction scheme is being frit-
tered away. The reconstruction money was subscribed
(1) to pay off the debt upon the property, and (2) to pro-
vide funds for development. So far all that has been
done is to waste money in smelting, and to “get
preparations under w'eigh for the resumption of sinking
the main shaft.” The medicine that O.K. wantsat once
and wants badly is this: “Shut down the furnace, and
guard every penny jealously while prospecting work is
carried on.” Even with those measures adopted the
chances are that the reconstructed capital will be
exhausted before another good body of ore is met with.
It may be that for market purposes O.K. should keep up
the appearance of being still a smelting concern, but
shareholders will undoubtedly pay dearly for this policy.
If they are wise, those interested will for the present
treat O.K. as a purely prospecting show, and work it
accordingly.
* * * *
Energetic Geo. D. Meudell, of Melbourne, has
whipped up a strong syndicate to send him to the Malay
Pen insula on the look-out for tin, rubber and anything else
that can be turned off later on to the public. The syndi-
cate—the Malay Tin and Rubber —has big ideas for the
future. Present idea is that Meudell shall leave for the
Peninsula early next month, with Fred Brown, an ex-
Queenslander, who has been dredging' in S. America (he
was at the back of the Transpacific Co. at one time); and
Theodore Fink will probably join them. They have
already arranged for influential helpers on the spoc. Zeb
Lane, who floated the Great Boulder, and therefore cuts
considerable ice in London, leaves next month to look up
J. Bull; he is a director of the syndicate, and will be on
hand to push along any rubber flotation, supposing the
explorers find anything floatable—and almost anything
with rubber in sight of it is floatable just now.

Re the report that a further 50,000 shares in
Great Fitzroy (Q.) had been placed in London, 8., M. and
Co. tell The Bulletin that no shares have been issued
since the accounts were made up.
■* + ■*■*
Bright Star, one of the Harrietville (Vic.)
dredges, keeps on the right side of things, but can’t get
far enough over on that side to pay divs. At first, tight
ground spoiled the work, and the dredge had to be re-
modelled. When that was finished, in the early part of
1908, the financial position stood like this:—Liabilities
(including £650 payable to vendors out of profits), £2554;
liquid assets (stores), £328. Nowit is: Liabilities, £1044;
liquid assets, £492. So in 12 months the deficiency has
been reduced by £1674. The ground is, however, still
giving trouble on account of heavy boulders ; but hope
sees better things ahead. So far, only 7i acres out of the
total of nearly 80 acres secured have been chewed up.
There are 1500 fully-paid £5 shares in this co.

“ Larrakeyah, ’* from Port Darwin (N.T.):
There are over 200 men on the Umbrawarra field, ana
many new discoveries are being- made in the district. In
most cases the miners are making tucker, but a few lucky
ones earn up to £5 per week. There is a great deal of
sickness on the field, mainly among the newchums.
Umbrawarra is likely to remain a good tin-bearing district
for some time to come. Sensational reports come to hand
concerning Tanami, near the W.A. border. The latest
report says that 221b. of stone, the other day, gave 180oz.
gold. The beginning of the wet season will probably see
a rush for the district. The Government intends to send
out well-sinkers as soon as possible. According to all
reports, the nearest permanent water at present is nearly
300 miles away.

THE AXE IS MIGHTIER THAN THE PEN.
PROPRIETOR'S DAUGHTER : “ Paw , maw says can
you spare the new editor to chop some wood ?
Maw yets full, of splinters !”
PRO[?]ETOR OF THE DANDALOO DART : “ Not

properly yet.”
Some visitors to the Queensland Jubilee,
looking out of the railway carriage window as the
train passed Clifton on the Warwick-Brisbane
line, saw what they thought was a vision. The
man was a German cocky with hair like a Moorish
insurrection and a maze of whiskers like the evi-
dence in a Chinese perjury case. The vehicle was
a spring cart patched with bits of old fence and
cast-off barrel, and tied together with string.
This cart was full of produce, and Hans sat on top
of the produce. The team consisted (1) of a cow
with collar, hames and all the usual fixtures, and
(2) a plain horse. Some excited visitors from the
South wanted to buy the whole affair for Sydney
Museum.

“Tot”: Be “Vigilans” (B. 19/8/’O9).
Aborigines own and work farms elsewhere in
N.S.W. than at Rolland’s Plains, Port Macquarie.
Near here (Gladstone) six full-bloods with their
families have decent farms. Some of them possess
smart turn-outs. One of the tribe, a male aged
about 50, is to be seen daily on the local racecourse
preparing a candidate for the November meeting.
Ihe untutored nigs, (so-called) play bridge and
crib with skill and self-restraint. Surprise parties
eventuate at regular intervals. If “Vigilans”
visits this town any washing day he may gaze on
a dusky damsel of 18 summers (and 10 stone
weight) who fairly paralysed a local landlady
recently by stating her wish to always finish work
early, so‘ that she might attend her music lessons.
* « * *
“ Edyson ” : I see the pronouncement of
a Melbourne doctor that alcoholic poisoning is
the commonest form of death from snakebite in
Australia. The medical expert seeks to abolish
the generous old bush custom of filling the bitten
Christian with raw brandy and rushing 1 him across
the landscape. He has his work cut out. There
is no stronger superstition in the wild woods. If
it were abolished, lots of bushmen would not think
it worth their while to be bitten. I remember an
interesting perversion of the great Australian
faith in the cold whisky cure. We had noticed
Busby taking to drink with both hands at a
Morwell pub, and were not astonished when he
came out to camp some days later, with
ghosts in his hair, two revolving eyes,
and a mind that refused to focus on the
facts of existence. He sat around for a
few hours, nursing his head, which he seemed to
imagine might fall off if not carefully held on.
Then he took another fit, and began chasing
among the litter and logs of the surrounding bush,
lorn my lhomas, his mate, kept an eye on him,
but did not intervene till Busby put up a great
yell and began gesticulating frantically. Three
of us arrived on the spot within SOsee. Busby had
his large foot on a yard and a half of untidy black
snake. We pulled him off, and the snake loped
under a log., Busby was disgusted. “ I’m drunk,”
he said—“l’m ’bom’ably drunk, ’n’ I was goin’t’
try the snake cure. I’ve heard that if yer’bom’-
ably drunk, ’n’ you let a snake bite you, you’ll
soon be all right.” It’s a poor rule that won’t
work both ways; but we tied Busby to his bunk
till he was capable of calm reflection.

“Tracker”: Made my first acquaintance
with the Papuan native dog about a moon ago,
whilst ambling through the bush on the outskirts
of a native village. For the moment I thought
the mysterious exit of Reid’s canine (dry species)
was solved, and that fame was mine
at last; but close inspection dashed
my hopes to the loam. Compared
with the dingo, the Papuan dog is a
double-width O.S. nightmare. His
pedigree has been mislaid, but he
looks a cross between a greyhound
and a fantod. When sufficiently
energetic he stands about 18in. above
sea-level; but his chief occupation
consists of. lying down in any old
place, intimidating the manyous
mosquito with his loppy auricular
organs. Arriving at the village, I
was immediately surrounded by 468
of this brand, of every shade and
shape imaginable. They were .too
tired to baric or even growl—just
sniffed half-heartedly and laid down
in my path. After the nineteenth
fall, a woolly-headed nig, armed with
a young tree, cleared the way and
proffered a coeoanut to quench my
burning rage. The liquor absorbed,
Papua smashed the nut and hurled
it to the hounds. Instantly I knew
the sort of evolutions Bill Lyne per-
formed during the recent christening
of the “ Dickiii ” Ministry. Even-
tually learned that Papua’s pup is a
bigoted vegetarian, and lives entirely
on yams, poeoanuts, . pawpaws, sea-
weed, sardine-tins, and other fruits
indigenous to the soil. The prover-
bial drink of water is bloated com-
pared with our transparent tyke—he
is even too weak to drive Capital out
of the country. Verily, he represents
the poor relation of the numerous
dog family. Was, just leaving the
village when Papua fathered a weird
yell of joy what time he rushed to
greet the narrowest edition of dog yet pub-
lished. Theunbleaohed-oneexplained that this tyke
had been missing for five days—in fact, was a
prodigal. Then he dashed away to kill the fatted
coooanut for the refreshment of the wanderer.

“Henry Phelthatt/’,: Am ready to give
anyone a pull who says whiskers and cursing go
hand-in-hand, so to speak. Clean-shaven men,
and men with only beards (beard and whiskers
are not synonymous, mind), often spend several
hours daily committing their cursography to
memory ; but I never yet met one who could
curse sufficient to rivet the attention of an
audience. A properly constituted curse contains
88 per cent, carbon dioxide, is about as long as a
Dreadnought and resembles a crosscut saw. It
produces an earthquake effect. It will cure tooth-
ache and tiredness ; it will perform any kind of
bush work ; it will settle tradespeoples’ accounts ;
it will even bring down rain. But it must be
projected through a bin. interlocking whisker, to
give it that ragged edge which causes it to fairly
shriek as it cleaves its way through the atmo-
sphere. “Dick K.’s” combustible bullocky
evidently couldn’t curse, or he wouldn’t have set
fire to his team ; he would have shifted it. I’ve
noticed that women who sport chin foliage mostly
remain single. Perhaps they curse too ;- and a
woman who curses isn’t the.sort -.thaLyou' would -
care to ask to puf out the-forgotten milk jug. i

Regarding an old gentleman’s account of
how he polished his spectacles and annoyed a
number of peaceful lions
There is a lot of humor in modern lion hunting-. In a
recent English magazine a mighty but elderly hunter
proudly recounts some of his recent lion-shooting ex-
ploits in British East Africa, and the general impression
is that of a bloodthirsty old person, accompanied by two
savages bearing guns, tramping across a strange land and
pausing at intervals to polish his spectacles. The lion
family is discovered at intervals sunning itself unsus-
piciously on ant-hills. (The ant people are old friends of
the lions, otherwise the lions wouldn’t.) Sometimes the
elderly gentleman manages to creep up and murder one
of the lions before they take alarm. If they happen
to see him coming they amble off into the, long'
grass and make peaceably for home. Then the elderly
hunter goes after them and wades nervously amongst
muffled growls until one of the animals incautiously stops
to see if the undesirable alien is still about. Then : “At
last a head was raised clear above the grass some 80yds.
away, and I steadied myself to fire. As I was on the pdint
of pressing the trigger, one of the gun-bearers pulled my
arm down and pointed to a large lioness that was stand-
ing quite close to my left, some 20yds. away. She was in
a tall bit of grass, and I had not seen her. As I turned
she vanished. The lion in front, during the instant’s
delay, sat up on his hind legs and gave me a fair shot at
his chest, which I hit full, though I was shaking a bit and
my glasses were terribly foggy.” The lion walks away
offended, and is subsequently slain whilst endeavor-
ing to get home. Further glimpses along the
article give always the same impression—bored
and irritated animals retreating before a well-
armed old person whose spectacles sparkle mur-
derously in the morning sun : “I saw with my field-
glasses an old grey lioness’s nose just sticking over an
ant-hill about 500yds. in our front. As I looked, she drew
her head down and slipped quietly into the grass. ... At
last, as I mounted a stilflsh ridge, I had a glimpse down
below of a regular bunch of lions all trying at the same
instant to get off an ant-hill. . . . I'shot twice, as quickly
as I could, and to my delight and surprise heard each
bullet tell and two loud answering grunts.” Then follows
a four hours’ walk after the poor beasts, and more spi c-
tacle-polishing, during which the old grey-headed mother
lion misses an opportunity : “.What I had taken for a
weather-worn tree-stump vanished with a loud, angry
grunt from before me.” If the lioness hadn’t heeii
prompt, the hunter might have sat down on the supposed
log to rest —like Johnson on the N.T. crocodile. If the
great hunter keeps on bothering lions much longer, his
spectacles may one day get extra foggy and make him
mistake a group of lions for a sofa. Then there will be a
tragedy for the Evening Bloodstain to gloat over.
* * * *■
“ The Boy ” writes from Papua :
More than a few years back, in what was then called
British New Guinea, those who went down to the sea in
ships had a rather perilous time navigating the little-
known coral-strewn seas round the less-known land.
Some of the Admiralty chartfs of the period were very
primitive. A huge mountain would be shown, apparently
rising out of the sea, and perhaps located At a distance of
50 miles, with no surrounding coast line. Another place
would have a bit of land that we know now to be a cape,
looking like an island. A certain part would be marked
with bays, etc., and then abruptly.cut off. And so on.
The pioneer trader had thyse, charts, and as he came
and went on his way he dripy'daborious lines that repre-
sented the places he had b'ecitto." He in turn might lend
his chart to a man going there for the first time, and thus
it would pass from one to the other, each adding his
iittle art collection. The greatest oil painting I ever saw
in this line was an early chart of theTrobriands Islands, in
the possession of Captain Blank, who had sailed since'
very early days in New Guinea waters in charge, of One of
the mission schooners. . .
He had to go to the TrObriands, and knowing nothing
about the place tried to pick up information in Samarai
before starting. He was advised to go to “ Nick the
Greek,” who knew the place better than most.
So to Niek he went, got what information he could,
and was lent a chart that for years past had been used
by every son of Greece that had gone there. In those
days the Greeks Were the only people who went there.
The chart was such a curiosity that when he came back
the captain gave Nick £2 for it. And that is how I
had the good fortune to see it.
Every Greek had added his little knowledge and “Billy
the Greek,” and “Pat tbeGreek,” and “ Mick the Greek”
had in turns helped to decorate it with red, black,
purple, and green inks.
It was a creation. In one place a rock would be repre-
sented by a black object like a sentry-box. Coral reefs
looked like green sausages with a pain, and sand hanks
sprawled Over half the available water front in pale blue.
An object that looked like a cross between a sea-serpent
and a hump-back whale, in red, indicated to the fraternity
of traders that “ Pat the Greek ” had succeeded in hook-
ing a large kingfish thereabouts.
And then the coastline was simply superb. 11-re a
bay would be marked “ Good place for. yams.” A little
further along, “ Fine fowls here,” would stare you in i he
face. Nothing was wanting—“ Big Village,” “Natives
here cheeky,” “Two pounds of tobacco stolen here,” and
yet another, which occurred with alarming frequency,
“ Good place for women.” And, carefully written on the
borders of the chart, was a detailed table showing the
ruling price for everything in tobacco, knives, toma-
hawks, calico, and beads.
Some short while back I read somewhere that there was
a society in England, by which early charts and maps
were valued and stored away with great care. If this is
so, I consider the society in question should lose no time
in securing, in the interests of science, to say nothing of
humor, the chart of “ Nick the Greek.”

A SONG OP THE GIANT HEARTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The snow lies white on the Tasman hills and the air bites
cold and raw ;
But the log-men fight with mighty wills a-tune with the
crooning saw;
While the weaklings shiver, all woe-begone, and the sad-
eyed poets dream,
The men of might are marching on with their floating
flags of steam !
They are faring forth with the gleaming axe—in the
forest’s midmost ways
They are bending low with straining backs, and the
Nation’s track they blaze.
And a song I sing for the timber-men, who, far from the
sordid marts,
Are writing Life with a deathless pen—the men with the
giant hearts !
The band-saw roars in the timber mill, and the winch is
rattling loud ;
The blue gums crash on the skyward hill and their stately
heads are bowed,
Mo calls the odds on the crowded flat, where the jaded
fools are seen ;
And Percy’s wielding the cricket bat—but what of the
Titans lean ?
Where the tree-ferns follow the winding creeks, they are
bringing the mighty down ;
Where the “knocker ” clangs and the engine shrieks they
are raising wharf and town !
SO a hymn I raise for the heroes true—so a cheer from
Australia starts
For the nameless kings, for the fighting crew—the men
witli the giant hearts !
The pessimist croaks in the reeking bar, and he weeps in
his dismal beer ;
But the crash of the blue-gums echoing far sounds forth
like a trumpet clear !
The pinchbeck mimes of the thoughtless town, they strut
on the tawdry stage.
Where the monster trees go roaring down men fight for a
Baresark’s wage !
Where the axes flash and the cables strain, where the
“ landing"’ shakes and groans,
There’s a.nation born—of the natal pain I would fashion
in major tones
A ballad of strength and a song of hope. When the
wintry s.un departs
They are straining still on the anchored rope—the men
with the giant hearts !
Where the myrtles lift their shining heads and the sage-
, green dog-wood grows,
There the. White Man battles and firmly treads on the
vamped-up creed of woes !
There the White Man slogs in the early morn—a cheer for
his eight-foot saw —
And he turns his face with a Viking’s scorn to the vapid
priests of Jaw !
The winds may blow and the rain may come, enshroud-
ing the Tasman Hills ;
But he's marching on with a thunderous drum, and
there’s hope when the White Man wills !
So a song I sing—and fools may scoff, may jeer at its
halting parts ;
I sing of the Toilers straight and tough—the men with
the giant hearts!
Tasmania.
ORANT HERVEY.

“Jimmie Pannikin”: I want to start
another competition regarding the queerest-named
■station property in the Commonwealth ; and
hereby enter a well-known station, “ Will-I-go-
bung?” in the Tumbarumba (N.S.W.) district.

* ■* -Jr *
The farmers of Maoriland are pursuing
ithe importing lunacy again. Once it was spar-
rows, weasels, stoats, starlings and owls ; now it
•is martens and swallows that the man with straws
rin his hair is yelling for. If a farmer in Maori-
land finds two caterpillars in the corner of a pad-
dock, he rushes off to the nearest Agricultural
and Pastoral Association and demands that
■something be imported at once. What with the
things that have been imported to deal with the
insect pests, and the further things that have
been imported to deal with the things that were
imported previously, there isn’t much that
walks or flies that hasn’t been dragged into
Maoriland by the hair of the head in the
ihope that it would do something which it never
■even attempted. The African lion and the man-
eating tiger have not yet been called upon, but
some maniac will shortly suggest that they be
dragged along to deal with the imported red and
fallow deer that are already causing M.L. some
anxiety. In the meantime the marten and swal-
low proposal is being carefully considered. Prob-
ably the best thing that Maoriland can do is to
import everything from the driver ant to the boa
constrictor, and then let Nature argue the point.
By this means the country will at least get all its
importing lunacy over at once, and then it will
have time to tackle the repayment of its public
debt.

“Noah” re “ light variable, winds” :—
There is a deep-seated loyalty to the owners beneath
the stolid taciturn demeanor of the tenant of the
mercantile marine bridge. I once took a business
trip round the east coast of Tasmania, which bore
on this and other things. The trip took about a
week. The boat was somewhat smaller than a Sydney
North Shore ferry steamer. I slept on deck, and also hail
my meals there the whole time, doing biz as the boat
touched at the various ports. I lived like a king—King
Billy. The cook served his time in a tannery, so the pas-
sengers said, which was quite likely, as he could make
most durable leather from corned beef. The captain was
an uncut diamond. He did not address the passengers as
Mr. or Mrs., but usually christened them afresh. He
called me “Docker”; but I didn’t mind this, as a stiff
and starched M.P. who daily expected to become Sir
Blank was addressed as plain Brown. To hear him
address the crew was equal to the best hair re-
storer. On our return trip, the super or Chinese
steward crossed him. He threw the curry in the
Onow’s face and bang-ed him on the head with the
tin plate. He also said: “Sooner than allow such a
scurvy crew to trample me under heel I’d scuttle the
d ship.” The crew never admitted a rough trip. We
mostly met weather after rounding some point, and
thence on to another point, which I remember equally
well. I asked the skipper if he intended to put in for
shelter. He guessed'his ship (meaning the river launch .
under him) was going through, and would land its pas-
sengers in town a good hour ahead of the coach. We
shipped seasg-alore. The rowing boat lashed for’ard altern-.
ately filled, and had the water emptied out of it as'the -
ship jerked—it was too small to roll. The crew crawled
about and hung on to the rails, and the engineer down ’
below worked ankle deep in water. The skipper
got shot from the wheel once, and somersaulted back-
wards over the iron rail. He did a “Carrington” down
t lie deck on all fours after a deck chair, which, in turn,
chased a biscuit tin. By way of re-assuring the few pale
passengers who clung to the various fixtures, the captain
made an off-hand kick at the biscuit tin, but the
steamer’s funnel darted forward, clouting him smartly on
the head, and he rolled back to duty like a sack pf
potatoes. I Chen ventured to suggest that it was a bit
rough, but having to get hastily under a spread of canvas
to avoid a sea which broke prematurely and shot over the
ship, with the exception of a portion which poured down
the funnel, I only caught the words “duck pond.” I
looked up the shipping arrivals next day for curiosity.
The Swallow' (our boat) reported “ light variable winds.”
The Westralia coming down the coast was hove-to to save
the live stock on board. Some day the Swallow willmeet
a gale, but it W'on’t be the skipper who reports it; unless
lie does so to Peter or the other gentleman with the
three-pronged fork.

From Randolph Bedford :
“ A Man in the North,” in a recent Bulletin, talks of
the Eastern and African Cold Storage Supply Co. which
“commenced operations at Arafura and put up build-
ings at Blue Mud Bay in the Arafura Sea. The object
was to extract bovril and ship frozen beef.” According
to “ M. in the N.” the co. was “floated by the indomit-
able Bradshaw five years ago.” Here are the facts :H.
H. Drysdale, self and Bradshaw held the 20,000 miles of
country, and first H.H.D. and then myself went to Lon-
don to float it. After six months of humorous disappoint-
ments I left the float to Drysdale. The indomitable
Bradshaw was first manager of the co., and we lost
±BO,OOO for a start. First profit made was £17,000
assurance on Drysdale’s life. Arafura has 60in. rain-
fall, and that’s a swamp. Hussey Burgh Macartney
had a station on Florida, in the middle of Arafura, many
years ago, and abandoned it. The droving and the
general management w'ere farcical. Watty Marshall re-
ported on the concern, and Bergl Australia, Ltd., took
the management. A good many thousands of fats were

where the turkey got it at Christmas, and that’s in the

works erected—except in the prospectus. There’s a book
in the funny business of the float—l’ll do it one day.

FIRST DOSSER: “ They’re talkin’ av resuming this park fer the Greater Sydney scheme. What's
ter become uv us?”
SEOND DITTO: “ Well, ef you’re been in one place 20 years youse kin claim compensation.”
lines were built, water-courses were bridged over
and wide spaces were allowed for big bodies of
water to pass under. To save the expense of
keeping some of the bridges in repair,-from half
to two-thirds of the_*spaces have been filled in
with earth in later years/ Miles of line which
-were formerly bridges are now earth embank-
ments. For instance, the Wimmera River flats
at Glenorcby were spanned by bridges for-nearly
a mile ; but so much filling has been done that a
sort of dam has been formed. When an unusually
heavy fall of rain occurs, this dam is washed
away, leaving the rails >and sleepers hanging. It
is all of a piece with thejcheap and nasty .Victorian
railway management of modern times.
“Camel Pad”: Bourke, which frizzles
away out on the brink of the great nor’-west sand
patch (N.S.W.), must now pretty well hold the
Australian pub record. With a population re-
duced to 1800, including kids, but omitting goats,
it moistens its throat at 15 beer-pumps ; and evi-
dently it doesn't think 14 would be enough, for it
voted for “continuance” lately. Of course,
Coolibah Charley used to come in with a cheque
big enough to keep any modest pub going for a
month, and pass it over the bar ; but nowadays
Coolibah Charley is mostly married, or has joined
the Y.M.C. A. or something, and that makes the
marvel of Bourke’s 15 pubs still more marvellous.
“Zenda”: “Silas Snell” (B. 12/8/’O9)
suggests the possibilities of Highton, near Gee-
long, blowing away with the first prize in a windy
district competition. This amounts to an insult
to Wellington (Maoriland), where the atmosphere
is almost perpetually in a terrible hurry. The
other day a hatless man had his scalp blown off
in that city. Also a large dog and his kennel
were blown away. A fowlhouse and stable and a
horse blew away the same night. A painter who
was falling off a roof was blown through an open
window and saved. One of the reasons why we
are so pious here is that some years ago the wind
was so high that it blew away the Devil.
DEAR THINGS THAT I LOVE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
No one can steal the dear things that I love from me,
Nor can I throw them away like a glove from me ;
Gum-trees and brumbies, buckjumpers and sheep,
Roads winding in where the scrub belars weep ;
Quart-pot and billycan lunch in the bush ;
Soft tramp of the sheep and the cattle’s wild rush.
They are deep buried away in the heart of me,
Welded or rooted, and joined as a part of me ;
Brolgas that dance in the long reedy grass,
Kangaroos scudding away as we pass ;
Fruit of the krin-tree, sour to the alien,
Good to the taste of the bush-born Australian.
Though they be parted by miles upon miles from me,
Memory never shall withhold her smiles from me :
tight flooding 4 down in-the glory of day,
Warning the soul of the bushman ahvay ;
Wild flowers scenting the spring of the year,
When sheep are a-bustle and sheds are a-shear.
They color the heart, the life and the soul of me,
•Giving their best to me, gaining the whole of me :
A blue and white sky and swift, flashing flight
■Of birds, pink and grey, through the warm, gold light ;
titter of leaves on the sky and stems brown
•Of ironbarks that spread towards the sun’s going down.
Why are they good to see, why are they dear to me?
Only my country —home—that’s all is clear to me.
Bread for my body and food for my soul,
Drink for my thirst, though a pint be my bowl ;
[Filling the heart with joy of wide spaces,
There lies the charm of the old station places.
K. S.W.
R. A . HO[?]
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

OUR ATTACK OF AEROOFOBIA.
The Ordnance Branch of the Defence Department is being besieged by hordes of eager inventors who are anxious to establish a
claim on the prize of £5000 offered for an Australian aeroplane.


EMIGRATION AND THE BIRTH-RATE.
Should the Conference agreement be ratified by Parliament, each child born will be a gain of 25s. to the State ; and each person leaving will mean a loss of a similar am ount.-Daily Paper.
Victoria: “Strikes me, if these two items keep on increasin’ the way they are, I’ll find meself owing the Commonwealth a few thou!”

Where’er a man’s delight finds source,
One more delight keeps him aglow—
Best game of all —’tis sport to know
That Woods’ Great Peppermint Cure was sent
To be man’s best medicament.*

ADVICE TO RHEUMATICS.
Noted Physician Tells How to Prevent and Cure
Rheumatism, Kidney and Bladder Troubles.
If you would avoid rheumatism, kidney and bladder
troubles, be moderate in the consumption of heavy rich
foods, substitute as far as possible soups, broths, fresh
milk, and drink water—lots of water. Take plenty of
time to eat, and don’t eat after you have had enough,
even if it does taste good. If your work is confining, take
a moderate amount of exercise each day in the open air.
Of course, neither diet, rest, water nor exercise will
cure these afflictions. We advise them as preventives
only. For the benefit of readers of this article who are
now afflicted with rheumatism, kidney, bladder or
urinary troubles, and desire to be cured quickly, we give
below, complete in every detail, the
which has made a great authority on the subject so
successful in the treatment of these diseases. It is the
most certain cure which has been used. It is pleasant to
take, it is not expensive, it can be supplied by any
chemist, and we believe it to be the greatest prescription
for rheumatism, kidney and bladder troubles ever written.
It is also a valuable tonic and blood purifier. If you are
a,sufferer, take this prescription to any chemist and have
it prepared; or you can obtain the ingredients
separately, and mix them all at home.
Tincture Gentian Compound, one ounce; Syrup Rhu-
barb, one ounce ; Liquid Barkola Compound, one ounce ;
Syrup of Ginger, five ounces.
The dose for adults is one teaspoonful taken after
meals, and one at bedtime. Children should be given
one-fourth to one-half teaspoonful, after meals only.
After you are cured, follow the advice given as to diet,
water and exercise, and you will not need the services of
a physician again for these ailments.
Note.—Other papers are privileged to copy the above.*
PERSONAL
ITEMS
Motto of the modern one-man-dash ex-
plorer: “ Too many Cooks spoil the broth.”
Ned ” Simms, just dead in Adelaide at
4j, was the eighth or ninth of that famous brew-
ing family to die within as many years. He was
chairman of Tattersall’s, a member of the S.A. J.C.
and Stock Exchange committees, and possibly the
best amateur billiard-player in the State. His
'•"It Permian ran second in the S.A. Derby three
or four hours before lie passed out.
J. H. CANN. M.L.A.

Rosser, a tierv Welshman, is an enthusiastic,
whole-hog Socialist—such a whole-hogger that the

“D.”: Web Gilbert, the Melbourne
sculptor, going over some moulds that had come
into his possession, took a casting from one, and
found it to be a death-mask of Marcus Clarke. I
never knew before that such a cast was taken,
and everybody of whom I have inquired expressed
the same ignorance.
Died in Ceylon, aged 56, C. G. Warnford
Locke, the most indefatigable mining writer of
his day. He spent a few years in Australia as
superintendent of the once-famous mines at
Lucknow (N.S.W.); but he had serious trouble
first with the mines and then with the miners,
and therefore quitted the place. Probably no-
body else knew quite as much as he did about
mines and mining in “the East”—from China
down to Singapore—and he spent years roaming
thereabouts. A fine, vigorous, powerful fellow, if
he hadn’t taken to mining he might easil}' have
got two-inch letters on the bills as a strong man
in a circus.
David Ivirkcaldie, assistant Railway Com-
missioner of N.S.W., died on Sunday at a Sydney
private hospital, aged CO years. He was a native
of Kirkaldy (Scotland), and commenced as a cadet
on the Leven and East Fife Railway, a stumpy
little concern now owned by the North British
Co. In 1874 he got an appointment in India, but
for health reasons abandoned it and came to
Australia in 1876. Soon after arrival he got a
clerkship in the traffic manager’s office, and
worked his way upwards. He was the only one
of the three old commissioners who remained in
office after the conspiracy and disruption which
ended in Oliver’s retirement.
An obituary item :
Mrs. Bayly, widow of Nicholas Paget Bayly, formerly of
Havilah, near Mudgee (N. S. W.), died the other day. at Stan-
more. She was the daughter of one Blackman, who is said
to have been the first discoverer of the site of Mudgee,
whereby “Giraffe” Taylor and John Haynes were made
politically possible. For if there had been no Mudgee
there could have been no Mudgee Taylor and no Mudgee
Haynes. Bayly, one-time husband of the deceased, was
a son of Nicholas Bayly, of N.S.W.’sold Rum-selling Corps,
and was secretary or something to Colonel Johnston
during his usurped Governorship. On the death of Mr.
N. P. Bayly, Havilah was purchased by H. White. The
price was said to be £BO,OOO. Mrs. White, the widow, has
invested some of her coin in Beulah Flats, at North Shore,
a building that faintly suggests the Tower of London.
A traitor to his class principles has arisen
at Fitzroy (Vic.), and has protested in council
against the handing out of illuminated addresses
to ex-councillors. “Each of them costs 15
guineas, and what are they? A piece of paper in
a wooden frame. I had two, and I’d take 7s. Gd.
for them.” Truly, the life of a councillor is a vain
and trumpery thing. An election, aided in many
cases by beer and personal grovel to ratepayers ;
a thankless period as mayor; a few attendances
at council, full of windy repartee and wordy
insult; a punch on the nose, perhaps; a painted
bit of paper in a square of wood; and then —
oblivion !
A photo, of W. J. Lyne when he first
entered N.S.W. Parliament, 29 years ago, shows
him an agricultural-looking man with a clump
beard and dry-country cast of eye. Old hands
say there was doubt as to whether he was a sheep-
man or a wheatman, but he soon cleared the
matter up by advocating the remission of £250,000
owing by selectors. Some who sat in the House
wherein Lyne first appeared are still lively—
Harry Levien, for instance, and George Reid—
but most went for their wings. Parkes, who said
selectors were burglars, and Bob Wisdom the
pugilist, and Pat Jennings the musician, and
Dave Buchanan the freethinker, got measured for
their flying apparatus ; and Stuart, Squire Farnell
and John Davies applied for harps. A few
others, such as Tom Slattery, Pa Brunker and
Francis Abigail, are out of Parliament, but do not
yet fly.
Sky-pilotage has its perks, if one is to
believe the statement made to the Goulburn press
by C. Johnson, bush missioner and ex-Anglican
minister, lately brought up for soliciting alms and
discharged. Thus spake Johnson
If some of the young ladies of Goulburn whose house-
holds I visited had wished to do me an injustice they
could have come into court and done so; but they did
no such thing, because they knew perfectly well that
every act or word of mine towards them was done in love
or fondness of them. There are certain privileges
attached to the clerical profession besides preaching or
administering the Sacraments. A clergyman should be
master of the people. The Catholic Church call then-
clergymen fathers. That explains what the clergy really
are, or should be. We all know what a father of a family
should be, and also what he shout! do towards his family.
I have heard of a clergyman who when visiting his flock
before leaving them would kiss the children, some of
whom were young girls 16 or 17 years of age, and this
man did it in real love or fondness of them ; and I am
brave and bold enough to state that during my twoyeais
as an Anglican clergyman I have done similar actions
myself. We all know how the apostles and saints of old
have suffered because of the faith within them.
If these tilings be so, bow comes it that the
clerical business isn’t rushed? “Masters of the
people!” Licensed kissers of girls up to sweet
seventeen ! All the same, it’s likely that unre-
generate fathers and big brothers who caught
white-chokers at this pastoral game would view
the matter with some disfavor and heavy boots.
What the apostles and saints of old suffered be-
cause of the faith, would be a small circumstance
compared to what their lineal descendants would
Vie called on to endure in the attempt to exact
their pleasant little dues.
By the Oldest Inhabitant (more public-
houses) ; —-
Apropos the Liverpool Arms and Ben Palmer and his
copper tokens (B. 2/9/’O9). The bird on Ben’s apology for
a penny was a Liver, not a Lyrebird—Liver-bird, hence
Liver-pool. Another Sydney publican used tokens also,
Alfred Toogood, of the Rainbow Tavern, on the opposite
(western) corner to Ben Palmer. Toogood’s house in the
early days was the Bunch of Grapes, kept by Michael
Napthali, who had been a draper in Lower George-street,
and from whom the soldiers Sudds and Thompson stole
a shilling’s worth of calico—that calico which caused such
commotion in Governor Darling’s time. In time the
Rainbow came into the possession of Jim Punch, a popular
rowing man, who died in it. Frank Punch was the last to
hold a license for the premises. Munro’s Federal Bank
got it, and held it until its eminently disreputable
decease. On the third corner of the intersection was
The Rose and Crown Inn, kept bv William Toogood,
brother of Alfred. He also had Toogood’s Hotel on the
S.E. corner Market and Pitt streets, a house afterwards
kept by Detective McGlone, who arrested bushranger
Gardiner. Yet again William Toogood had another pub
property, The Golden Fleece, on the N.E. corner
King and George streets. When William was dead
enough, the widow married a St. Peter’s parson, Rev. C.
C. Kemp, and by his influence the pub license was aban-
doned, and the property let to the City Bank, when that
institution first commenced money-changing. Every de-
scription of trader minted (in Birmingham mostly) copper
tokens in the early days of Sydney. But the greed of the
minters caused the privilege to be abolished. They re-
duced the penny till it became hardly visible, and when
they tried at last to make a shilling’s worth of coppers
out of about a penn’orth of metal the Law grew angry.
John Jacob Cohen, who chairmans N.S.W -
Assembly, is not satisfactory to even his own
crowd. Varnished Parkes,sonof Vanished Parkes,
had a motion of censure tabled against Cohen, but
the Speaker ruled that he could not discuss it.
Chairman’s re-election was solidly opposed by the
Labor party, on the ground that he had displayed
partisanship, but the Wade whip was cracked.
Since then, John Jacob has done his best to be
impartial with difficult} - . He is a barrister and
qualified engineer, with a tendency to watch too
closely for points of order. This is all right before
the Bench or in front of a disorderly boiler, but it
is tactless in debate. The wise chairman shouldn’t
listen too attentively.

Dr. Cook, who tells the wondrous story
about reaching the North Pole, is alleged to be a
Jew named Koch. Credit Judceus / He travelled
practically SoZws—his two Eskimo don’t count.
A wise man, Cook. “ What is known to more
than one is no longer a secret.” And, as Napoleon
said, “ He goes furthest who goes alone.”
Thomas Main, who claimed to be the
oldest locomotive driver breathing, spoilt his
claim by dying completely at Richmond (Vic.) on
a recent date at the tottery age of 90 years and 10
months. Deceased, a native of Scotland, secured
the loudly-prized distinction of driving the pilot-
engine that preceded the late Victoria’s first visit
to Scotland. He had been in Australia since 1852.
The legal surprise of the week in Sydney has
been the appointment of the two Crown Prose-
cutors to fill the places of Merewetber, retired, and
Armstrong, deceased. Why Barrister White took
such a job is as hard for the ordinary Costs-
pursuer to resolve as is the reason why Barrister
Mason was singled out for it. White is well known
and esteemed among employers of junior bar-
risters. He is a sound lawyer, a useful man with
a jury, and popular. The fact that one of his
calibre is inclined to take a prosecutorship
suggests how extremely bad things must be at
the Bar. Mason is possibly a good man, but it
may be roughly estimated that most solicitors and
90 per cent, of the general public have never
heard of him. Still it is known that he has
appeared occasionally as junior to C. G. Wade,
that he has had Crown work from Wade, that he
-lives on the North Shore, and is on- Wade’s elec-
tion committee. Incidentally he once had cham-
bers with G. H. Reid.
S. Australia’s worst poet is Robert Cald-
well, yet as Robert was for many years a mem-
ber of the State Parliament he somehow gets the
papers to print his awful productions. Nobody is
ever reckoned quite dead thereabouts till Cald-
well has written something to jingle over his
grave ; and when the Waratah didn’t turn up to
time lots of people got up extra early to read
what their favorite-poet had to say about it. He
suspended judgment for three weeks or more,
and then this explosion happened (it is only part
of the explosion, but nobody deserves more who
has paid his sixpence):—
She crossed the Indian Main, no ill disturbing,
And, having berthed and coaled, pursued her way
Alonsr the billows from the Port of Durban—
Eight hundred miles, to Table Mountain’s Bay.
Far longer out have many vessels been,
Where greater dangers menaced, that have made
The hearts of thousands glad, whose eyes had seen
Their friends in life, whom they had wept as dead.
When Captain Moresby in the Talune
Found the Waikato on the Tasman Sea
She had been drifting months, o’er billows moony
And all on board were well as well could be.
The last verse deserves to be embalmed.
There is a bad time ahead of Johnson, the
awful M.H.R., who doesn’t want Australia to
have an army and navy, because the terrible
Labor party might some day turn them against
Great Britain. The Laborites'will, at next election,
run a young Presbyterian parson, T. S. Crawford,
for that seat ; and this Crawford seems to have a
way of getting votes —he won hands-down in the
Labor plebiscite. The electorate, by the way, is
Lang, which is appropriate, since it was named
after the first Presbyterian parson who battled his
way into N.S.W. politics. One or two other
Presbyterian meenisters have got into Parliament
since J. D. Lang’s day ; but—-like Ronald, the
last of them —they haven’t been stickers.

Croker, Melbourne’s solicitor and old-
established dandy, is known for something more
than the fastidious elegance of his waist. Mr.
Croker is also known for the studied magnificence
of his bills. Bent employed Croker to appear for
him before the recent Commission which inquired
re the Bent deals in soil, and Croker has pre-
sented his account to the Weary Government,
which weakly agreed to pay a reasonable propor-
tion of Bent’s expenses. Croker’s account is for
a backward sum of £525, and is just about £lO
short of the combined Costs of all the legal gentle-
men on the Government side of the inquiry. The
Government is believed to have settled up for an
amount between £350 and £4OO. Apparently,
therefore, the Government submitted the ex-
quisite’s bill to reasonable taxation. But Croker’s
bill ought to be taxed with a cannon.
“Montalex”: It does not seem to have
occurred to any of the recent industrious grubbers-
up of the late ex-Justice Foster’s pedigree that an
equally close investigation of the antecedents of
the conventionally “humblest ” in any community
would give astonishing results as regards being
“well-connected.” Nay, further, it has been
mathematically demonstrated (I have not the
figures by me, but doubtless some Bulletin
readers will be able to corroborate) that every
Briton or Britoness now alive possesses some share
of Plantagenet blood (and much good may it do
’em !). The demonstrator of this proposition was
an artillery officer, who had got tired of hearing
Fred Burnaby—the “Ride to Khiva” man—
pluming himself upon his “Planty” descent.
When the calculation had been duly formulated,
Burnaby at first snorted contemptuous disagree-
ment ; but he afterwards went carefully through
the figures himself, and, being no fool (many
people said that was why Queen Victoria never
liked him) admitted that it was correct.
THE CASH VALUE OF GOD’S OWN COUNTRY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mrs. Melba mused again on the richness of the country
she had travelled through. “ Yes, Australia is God’s own
country. There’s a man up at Orange who bought fifty
acres, and put down 25 acres in cherry trees. And now
from those cherry trees he is making £2OOO a year. At
Dubbo I saw boxes and boxes of enormous oranges, and
where they were grown is the most beautiful orchard you
have ever seen, and the owner makes more than £2OOO. ”
—Daily Paper,
Mrs. Melba mused again.
Memories of hill and plain,
Clad with blossom pink and white
Made her murmur in delight—
(“ Yes, Australia is God’s own country. There was a man
up at Orange who bought 50 acres and put down 25
acres in cherry trees. And now from those cherry
trees he is making £2OOO a year.”)—
Cash and blossom, understand,
Travel always hand-in-hand
In this Southern Eden land,
And the singer stills the song
As she moves herco. along,
Takes a pencil and a slate
Makes a hurried estimate
Of the value F.O. B. < '
Of the fruit that she can see
Blossoming on shrub and tree.
Mrs. Melba mused again.
Landscape glimpses from the train
Did once more a springtide dance,
And she murmured, in a trance :
(“ At Dubbo I saw boxes and boxes of enormous oranges,
and where they were grown is the most beautiful
orchard you have ever seen, and the owner makes
more than £2000.”)
Orchardists both far and near
Make two thousand pounds a year.
From this fact it would appear
As though God’s own country grew
More than blessings. What think you ?
Or perhaps it is the gold
And the oranges they’ve sold
Makes them call it God’s. Ah, me !
Where’s your springtime poetry,
With God’s Country F. 0.8. ?
N-S.w.
F. O. E.
