At the Lyceum Hall, Pitt-street, during the week, crowded
audiences have enjoyed the seri'es of biograph pictures illus-
trating the journey by motor from Coorna to the Hotel
Kosciusko., tha series of ski and toboggan races at the first
great Snow Carnival, and various scenes in the vicinity of the
Hotel. The pictures came as a revelation to the majority of
those who saw them. It is difficult even yet for most of our
people to realise that they have a stretch of hundreds of
square miles of snow-covered country —so like the highlands
of Switzerland or Norway. The Australian winter snowfields
may be reached in a comfortable journey of 16 hours from
Sydney, at little cost. On the snow will be found a palatial
hotel, quite up to date and with all the conveniences of a first-
class city hotel.
The winter sports, which have had a great vogue during the
last few months, will he indulged in only for about another
month before the novelty and sensation of the snowfields give
way to the summer delights of trout-fishing and mountaineer-
ing. Those of the Australians, and their name is legion, who
have never seen snow, should avail themselves of this oppor-
tunity. The English snowseape is beautiful, hut the Aus-
tralian bush, with its evergreen trees laden heavily with snow,
every leaf bearing its burden bravely, is an even more won-
derful sight. And the glory of the little furze bushes, with
their leafy tendrils loaded and crystallised, bending down
into the snow in graceful curves, is something to quicken the
pulse and fire the imagination.
You may play in the snow as babies play with the sand
of a sea-beach. More sedately, of course, and with a finer
appreciation of the beauties you move into being. In ski-ing
over Hills you will, enraptured, discover real snow eaves,
where an aggressive bubbling' creek has cut out a track for
jtself, getting a fleeting glimpse of earth and sky only to
disappear again in a few yards beneath twenty feet of snow.
Here you will see exposed a wall of snow ten, twelve or
twenty feet high, its white, powdery strata cleaner than an
ocean roller on the strand of an undiscovered shore, and you
gaze, amazed that such a structure should be at the mercy of
a rainstorm or a thaw. You may push your brake-stick four or
five feet into the snow and light a torch of a beautiful iride-
scent green, changing to blue. In the timber you may, if the
gods are kind and recent falls have been heavy, find scores of
trees linked up by the snow, their branches joining and inter-
twining, and bearing a load of snow forming a fair}' canopy
which seems but an audacity of the imagination.
A special “ Monaro Week ” has been arranged for the 11th
to the 18th September, and during this period special arrange-
ments have been made for the residents of the Monaro district
to visit the Hotel Kosciusko.

CORRECT TREATMENT
FOR ANAEMIA.
♦
One that Increases the Blood
Supply.
+
This Woman on the Verge of a
Decline, found a Valuable
Remedy.
Anaemia is simply a lack of blood. It follows
that the correct treatment for anaemia is one that
increases the blood supply. In fact, that is the on'y
treatment that can be successful. The symptoms
of anaemia are easily recognised. Paleness, list-
lessness, indigestion and failure of food to nourish,
headaches and often in women and girls back-
aches. The vitality being very low, renders
anaemic people liable to contract influenza, fevers
and other acute diseases. They have not the
power of resisting attack. To restore the blood
supply to its normal quantity, Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People can be recommended with
confidence. Firstly, because they are known to
have cured a host of anaemic people—that’s the
highest possible recommendation. Secondly, be-
cause they actually contain ingredients that
will combine with food and oxygen to make red
blood corpuscles. The case of Mrs. May Schiller-
man, Piesse Street, Montana, Coolgardie, W.A.,
well illustrates the advantage of treatment with
them in cases of bloodlessness.
Mrs. Schillerman stated to a reporter:—
“Anaemia set in gradually when I was a young
girl. My appetite faded away to nothing. For
days at a time even the lightest and daintiest
food wouldn’t tempt me. I was ghastly white,
under my eyes were dark rings. My gums and
lips had no blood. If I cut a finger it would only
bleed slightly, and
the blood had no body
in it. I remember
one day I had a tooth
pulled, and there was
not enough blood to
tinge the water. If
I walked any distance
my back ached
cruelly, and if I lay
down the pain wasn’t
much better. I
daren’t walk quickly
or climb a stair
rapidly. I’d just haxe
to stop and rest ftr
the awful thumping
ofmy heart. If I stayed in a hot room, I’d faint
off. I had no strength to do anything. My head
ached for hours, the pain pressing on my eyes, so
that I could hardly see. I 3lept very badly, and
woke up more tired than when I went to bed.
My feet were generally stone cold and my hands
damp and clammy, and I’d wake at night covered
with a cold sweat. My feet and ankles used to
puff up. I was dreadfully low spirited. At last
a lady friend advised me to take Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills. The first box did me good, and in a
little while they quite restored me to health. I
haven t a trace of anaemia, though I formerly had
it for years. Now I look after my home and
nothing tires me. I sleep well and there are no
more of those headaches and faint turns, as Mr.
Schillerman can bear witness.”
The price is 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d.,
and if you have trouble in getting them send a
postal note for the amount to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co. of Australasia Ltd., Sydney, and
they will be sent post free by return mail.*

From the Old Soldier :
Ex-Defence Minister Pearce the other day told the
Senate that the Japanese at Thursday Island could easily
rush the 60 artillerymen who form the burlesque of a
garrison, and take the whole place. He is probably right.
Thursday Island is practically a Jap colony, and the
alleged “fortifications” could not be defended for an
hour against a determined rush. There seems to be
some sort of mad idea of mounting more big guns at the
island, but what good big guns would do without men
behind them is not clear. Sixty men could not work
more than six big guns at one time, and if they were sub-
ject to a regular haminer-and-tongs bombardment which
would not only kill and wound some of them, but disable
some of their guns, the fort would be silenced in 24
hours. The alleged “garrisons” of Thursday Island and
Albany are really only sufficient to do fatigue duties
and fire occasional salutes ; for the defence of either
place they are ridiculously inadequate, and the provision
of more big guns will not make matters better.
The British voluntary army costs £lOO
per annum for each soldier, which is expen-
sive. In the United States, where wages
are nearer to the Australian standard, the
voluntary army costs £240 per annum for each
soldier, and that is probably about the sum
Australia would have to pay if it follow'ed
the advice of its Anglo-maniacs and stuck
to the voluntary principle. At that rate
100,000 soldiers—little enough for the de-
fence of a continent—would cost £24,000,000
per annum. Switzerland keeps its whole
capable male population armed and in fair
training for £8 per year per man, and the
difference seems worth considering. Also
even at £240 per head the U.S. finds it
impossible to recruit a force of 80,000 men.
Glorious Freetrade again! The Amalga- 1
mated Society of Carpenters and Joiners, one of
the three big British unions whieh have branches
in Australia and S’Africa, has actually 561 less
members now than it had in 1899. Last year so
shocking was the state of trade in Britain that
£112,253 was paid away in “unemployed bene-
fits,” the average in that country being 375. 3d.
per head. On account of the mining slump, things
were supposed to have about reached the rock-
bottom of wretchedness and misery in S’Africa ;
yet more unemployed benefits were actually paid
per member in Britain than in S’Africa—37s. 3d.
as against 31s. 2d. In the way of creating unem-
ployment and distress, Freetrade can obviously
beat even the worst of mining slumps any time.
The F used Government keeps on apolo-
gising and sliding away. The latest news is that
it won’t buy up the private lands in the Federal
territory, so that the increased land values created
by the establishment of the Australian capital at
Australia’s expense may be the property of the
Australian people. Private landholders are to
remain in possession and annex the profits. Why
the Australian people should make a present of a
few hundreds of thousands or a few millions of
pounds to certain big N.S.W. landholders the
Lord only knows, unless it is the price at which
Cook and Fuller, of N.S.W., will keep Deakin in
his billet.
There is a vague impression that the
only industries in the hands of the Chinese
in and around Sydney are furniture,
laundrying and vegetable growing. But
recent explorations by Labor members
have revealed the fact that the Mongols
are by far the largest producers of “ white
work ” for the drapery establishments. The
work is let out by the contractors to other
Chinese and white people ; but a very large
proportion of it is done in Chinese dens
where the heathen eat, sleep and have their
Joss. The stand-by of the fallen woman
is the Chow white-work shop; and when
business is pressing a Chow goes forth and
sweeps up the pavement. The nymphs
are brought in and set to work ; and in
due time the underclothing of the Chow
and the oldest profession on earth finds
its way to the wardrobes of the Very Best
People of Darling Point. And vet, for
some inscrutable reason, Premier Wade
refuses to compel Chinese goods to he
branded, so that his friends may be able
to identify this drapery, and refuse it if
they don’t want it.
Thus the deceased George Meredith
The (ear of death is the real cause of the English
objection to conscription. Men come to me and say "that
their trade would suffer, or they could not spare two
years from their apprenticeship. Their real meaning is
that they are afraid of being- called out and getting shot
at. So they pay others to do the killing and dying for
them. Every manly nation submits to general service
In the present state of the world it counts among the
necessities for safety. But nothing short of an invasion
will induce England to think of it seriously. As it is
80,000 Germans could march through England from end
to end.
The Fused Government retains its hold on
the Wakefield (S. A.)seat, rendered vacant by the
death of Frederick Holder, but the victory “isn’t
much to brag about. In 1901 S. A. was polled as
one constituency for the election of its seven
Representatives, and Holder was successful. He
came fourth among 17 candidates. In 1903 S. A.
was divided into constituencies, and Holder was
returned for Wakefield unopposed. In 1906 he
beat J. H. Vaughan, Labor candidate, by 3017
votes. This time the Fused Government put up
Foster, an old and respected S. A. State politician
who had held a portfolio in three Ministries, and
the same Vaughan his majority was only
On June 30 this year the public debt of
N.S.W. reached £90,307,419, an increase of
£2,671,593 during the year, despite Wade’s
alleged “surplus.” It is an appalling record
when one considers the taxes that the Deform
Government took off and the railway rates that it
reduced on the pretence that the State has more
money than it knows what to do with. Just
about four years more at this rate of folly and
N.S.W. will have a centenary—it will celebrate
its hundredth million of debt. And the people
seem to have quite forgotten that the infamous
Deform Government attained office on the promise
of Carruthers to reduce borrowing to £1,500,000 a
year, and possibly to cut it much lower or even
to abolish it altogether.
The P. and O. Marmora left Sydney on
August 28. Says the daily paper :
The principal lines of cargo shipped were 3232 bales
wool, . . . 120 bales leather, . . . 4000 crates rabbits.
One hundred and twenty bales leather ! And in
a year or so N. S. Wales will be buying back some
of that leather in the shape of boots ; and as the
poor Australian cheerfully pays about 25 per cent,
more than he need, he will remark—“We can’t
make boots like that in Australia; you see, we
can’t tan the leather out here.”

barred is a question. But, as for the verandah-post, it is
a nuisance and an obstruction, which progress should
have lopped off long before this. —Sydney Paper.

Melbourne Argus lias now discovered that
the reduction of the Victorian birth-rate is due to
the working of Wages Boards. Formerly the AlifiUS
declared that all such evils were due to Protection,
but since the Fusion it isn’t safe to say too much
against Protection, so the baby of responsibility
is left on the Wages Board’s doorstep now.
The Owe’Sullivan has joined the N.S.W.
Parliamentary Labor Party. Now G. R.
McLaurin, of Bill Lyns’s Albury district, is sus-
pected to be making for the same camp ; and
R. D. Meagher is already boiling his billy in it.
The Sydney D.T., Wade, and all the rest
of the Tories who have been objecting to a third
party on “high Constitutional grounds,” are of
course delighted at this fading away of N. S.
Wales’s third party; and they shy bricks, car-
toons and other mud at every man who crosses
over, just to show how delighted they really are.
De Largie, Henderson and Croft, of West-
ralia, quit the Senate next year ; and the Laborites
on that side have been choosing three men to
battle for them. De Largie and Henderson—
both of them old N.S.W. coal-miners’ union
officers—won’t be disturbed ; and along with
them the party will run Buzacott. The third
choice isn’t much of a speaker, but he is a sticker,
and he and Mines Minister Gregory have been
fighting one another for a State seat for years
past. Last time Buzacott was unseated on
petition; and over that business he got enough
sympathy and advt. to land him just ahead of
.T. B. Holman in the big Labor plebiscite.
A Victorian Land Board lately discovered
a new gang of bushrangers. Out past Morwell,
in Gippsland, the State’s surveyors laid out a
three-chain road. It is not much used at present,
but it is liable to be a busy track bv-and-bye.
Some of the greedy soil-snappers, however, con-
ceived the idea that if they planted a few shrubs
along their frontages, the'local council might be
persuaded to reduce the track to a width of one
chain, and allow them to fence in the two-chain
strip as “plantations.” The council was quite
agreeable, whereupon one owner promptly fenced
in 26 chains of the road—a trifle over five acres—on
the strength of having planted some 20 trees im-
mediately in front of his house ; and others seem
to have done just about the same. The Land
Board, having discovered this remarkable grab,
announced that the whole business was utterly
illegal, the council having no more right to give
away a slice of road than It had to sell or swap a
slice of Bourke-street; but so far nothing seems
to have happened to that council or to the bush-
rangers either.
“ Battler ” writes :
I can safely say that at no period in the long struggle
of the workers of Victoria for some measure of power in
the control of the industries by which they live has a bio-
employing concern assumed so tyrannous a tone towards
its employees, or held them by so tight a hand, as the
Tram Co. does now. It is more than a gripman’s billet
is worth for his wife to be seen talking to a democratic
politician, and it would go hard with him if his aunt were
known to be on speaking terms with Fred Anstey, or any
other Labor M.L.A. To be suspected of desiring to help
the formation of a union is sufficient to cause any man to
be promptly spilt out, and it is as much as his job
is worth for conductor, gripman, cleaner, battler, or
other grafter in the service to dream of furthering
the agitation for Wages Boards. Officials have even
moved among the men, stirring them to get up tearful
and eloquent petitions against the infamous Socialistic
proposal of a Wages Board to determine what is decent
pay and what are reasonable hours for the Tram Co.’s
workmen. In one shed a notice was posted, informing
the hands that a sympathetic attitude towards the Wages
Board scheme would mean the long shift off for the
guilty wretch. The system of espionage is so far-reaching
that an employee simply never knows his luck.
Neil Lewis, Premier of Tasmania, wa s
probably the saddest man who went home from
the Premiers’ Conference. The conference broke its
front teeth over the fact that the only possible way
to prevent the arrival of Federal industrial legis-
lation is to establish a comprehensive system of
State industrial legislation. Tasmania hasn’t
gone in much for that sort of thing, and the news
was divulged to Lewis that he had better go back
and legislate industrially at once, otherwise his
neglect to do it might furnish the Commonwealth
with a reason for doing it, not only as regards his
State, but as regards all the States. And Lewis,
who was made Premier because he was considered
to be a solid Tory and wouldn’t even dream of
industrial legislation, returned to Tasmania weep-
ing bitterly into his top hat.
If a suggestion which is now casually
hanging around takes definite shape, the Fathers
of Federation will have their portraits painted
and hung up in a national portrait gallery that
future generations may admire them. A strange
lot will be these probable Fathers. Among the
builders of the Constitution who were elected to
the Federal Convention there were some earnest
Federationists—Barton, Carruthers (he was a
vigorous Federationist then), O’Connor, Wise,
Quick, Deakin, Isaacs, Kingston, and one or
two more. But the majority stood for election
because they felt so important in a local way that
it was incumbent on them to be there. It would
be a wild joke to paint the portraits of Brunker,
McMillan, Reid, Simian Phraser, Solomon, Dobson
and their kind as Fathers, or even Uncles. They
were present to assert the fact that no national
convention was complete without them ; but to
depict them as infuriated and whole-souled battlers
in the Federal cause would be to turn history
upside down.
THE CALL OF LOCAL INTERSETS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is of importance to women to maintain the stability
of the States, because they are the guardians of purely
local interests, and women’s ctiief interests are local.—
Melbourne Argus.
The little local female lives her little local day
Absorbed in little things, in her own little local way ;
She does not lend an ear to hear the loud Australian call,
Nor bothers with the silly stupid Commonwealth at all;
The little local male, to whom the Greater Things are rot,
To him she looks admiringly—this little local lot!
The women West and North may be Australians. All the
same,
Australia doesn’t matter to the little local dame.
What odds if women West be gay, or women North be
grave ?
That only matters West and North ! Let East and South
behave
In their own little local way ; let things Australian rip
So long as of the local things we have a solid grip.
The little local church bazaar is good for trade and that ;
The charity committee’s mostly little local cat;
The little local politics of little shilling leagues,
They are the thing, with all their little penny-bun
intrigues ;
And woman’s work is in the Home—remember that, my
dear !
So long as maids are plentiful, and know their place
[Hear, hear !].
Yes, this is life —to seize the joy each [local moment
brings,
Despising not the petty day of little local things ;
Why, when State guv’nors beam on little local bowing
dames,
What pleasure can be found in playing larger Federal
games ?
Pray how, to social leaders, can race-problems seem
immense,
Or friends of fancy-work take up such fads as—say,
defence ?
This matter of our foes is not for local womenfolk !
Why, people say Australia’s foes are just a stupid joke !
What though some larger greater minds look anxiously
ahead?
A hundred years and, small and great, we’ll all of us be
dead !
Though things Australian boom or burst, and nations
soar or slump, j
The little local feminine, why should she care a dump ? ]
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

Does Yoisr Hair Worry You ?
It is no easy matter to find a specialist who success-
fully treats all scalp troubles, and in Mrs. Cowling’,
Manageress of the Hvgeia Institute, we have a specialist
of no mean order —one who has demonstrated by
practical results that premature greyness and baldness
are both preventable and curable. She is an earnest
worker, giving each patient individual treatment, and her
fees are really most moderate. She gives free consultations
every afternoon but Saturday between 2.15 and. 4.45,.45,
and, by appointment only, on Wednesday evenings
between 6 and 7.
Those who are in any way troubled about their
hair should call in and see Mrs. Cowling, or write for
her booklet. Some of her most remarkable cures have
been treated entirely by correspondence.
Her new address is HYGEI.A INSTITUTE, Ocean
House, opposite Government Savings Bank, Moore
Street, Sydney. <

Cookin’s not half bad if you
have nice people to cook for —
my idea is a cook ought to be
boss of the kitchen, and nobody
should interfere with her the
sort of place 1 like is where
the missus leaves everythin’ to
me —l knows what’s wanted
how much to order, and the
best sort to buy—much better
than any missus does.
The thing that most people find
hardest to buy, with satisfaction
to themselves, is tea —it isn’t
everybody as knows when tea
is good, do’ye see ?—now, I’ve
tried ’em all, and the only
tea 1 ever bother about gettin’
is Robur 1 like the No. 1
Grade, and 1 believe it’s as
good tea as can be got.
Tea travellers don’t waste their
time about my kitchen 1 tell
’em straight out l won’t be
bothered with ’em—l know what
Robur is—it’s good wholesome
tea it’s always the same, and
the flavour’s just as nice as
nice can be, and what more
can anybody get or want.
The Cook.
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 7, 1909.
My Dear Arint,—
Addressing my mind this week, in common
with the rest of the newspaper-reading-
section of humanity, to the freezing question
of Arctic exploration, I arrived speedily at
the conclusion that the explanation of Dr.
Cook’s triumph is to be found in the fact that he
was the first of his kind who had the hardihood
to make the trip without a Bridge party. If there
are enough explorers to make up a Bridge party,
and perhaps some menials as well, the run on the
commissariat is too great. Dr. Cook’s companions
were two Eskimo, neither of whom, it is safe to
assume, was a Bridgeite, the game not having
penetrated to their country. It is obviously easy
enough to get to the Pole if one has ample
leisure and is not hampered by the presence
of a horde of hungry Sybaritic acquaintances who,
having eaten all the blubber and so forth, turn to
ultimately and devour the motive power of the
sledge. The attainment of the South Pole should
now be a simple matter. Taking a line by Dr.
Cook, the next to adventure there will doubtless
take with him, not two colleagues and 30 dogs,
but 700 dogs and only himself. It is hard to see
how any Polar explorer could go wrong in those
circumstances, since at the gastronomic worst he
would always have dog to fall back on for dinner.
A reliable compass, a fur coat and a foot-warmer
would be about the only other things needed.
Cranbrook, now inhabited by State Governor
Chelmsford and family, is undergoing alterations
and repairs, as the builders’ notices have it. Exten-
sive disarrangements for the better are in progress,
to the value, report says, of several thousand
pounds. The vice-regal place of Australian exile
ought to be made pleasant for its occupants ; but
the ring of the woodman’s axe is so loud and long
in the land of Rose Bay that extensive tree-
felling must be part of the improvements. The
tree lover hates to know that the objects of his
affection are being slain. O, that someone would
invent an infallible tree-restorer in a bottle !
The Lord de Clifford, who upset his motof-
car last week and was killed immediately as a
result, is a nobleman who a while back married
Miss Eva Carrington, a London stage beauty. He
had most of this world’s desirable things, youth (he
was but 24), a son, an attractive wife and money.
What madness to risk all that motor-scorching.
Preferably would I, in such circumstances,
habitually wear flannel next the skin, and travel
in some perfectly safe conveyance, such as a bath-
chair or a wheelbarrow.
Peeress Dudley and daughters will be present
at the Amy Castles opening concert on the 18th
inst. at the Town Hall. At the following concert
the Chelmsford vice-regal brace will probably sit
in the seats of the mighty.
At a recent Sydney suburban wedding break-
fast the bride received a hint that the covering of
something deposited in front of her was not to be
removed till the feast was well advanced. This
at the request of the giver. Curiosity as to the
hidden treasure extended round the board, and
suggestions, light and airy, heavy and tragical,
helped the conversation. At last the bride
removed the draping, and disclosed a heart-shaped
cake with a dagger buried in it to the handle.
She fainted, and shrieks, hysterics and general
commotion ensued. Investigation proved the
donor to be the old-enough-to-know-better woman
friend of the other girl. She took this means of
showing her disapproval of the broken engage-
ment. The cake and dagger dame has the repu-
tation of being the kindest soul on earth, which
only goes to show that woman is a mystery at
whatever age you mistake her.
Two American ladies, Mrs. Park and Miss
Willard, have been around in Sydney for a few
days. They are from Boston, and have been
travelling in various parts of the world for
two years. Their inquiries about our
Parliamentary institutions, womanhood suff-
rage, municipal matters and the like have
been eager and close. All information obtained
will be sent to institutions or organisations in
Boston for use in controversies there. The visitors
were shown over Parliament House by Mr. E. W.
O’Sullivan, and they had interviews with Mr.
Opposition-leader McGowen and Mr. Holman,
and were afterwards entertained by the Sergeant-
at-Arms.
By the Mooltan will travel to England a
strangely re-united pair. They could not agree
like the birds in their little nests, and many years
ago she left Melbourne for Sydney, and lived most
of the time with her mother. A few weeks ago her
grass-widower, jumping off a tram in the gloaming
knocked down a lady. He picked her up, and
whilst dusting her and apologising, saw that she
was his wife. That was how the reconciliation
began.
Mention of the Mooltan reminds that the vessel
brought back from foreign parts Sydney-siders
Mr. Henry Dangar, Major Horace Dangar, and
Miss Essie Davies.
Mr. A. Vincent Cape was married last week to
Miss Ruth Smith, of the Lance Smith family of
squatters. Father Doran, of Cowra, did the deed,
the Capes being, to a man, woman and child,
staunch Romanists. Scores of Smiths—.Tagos,
Lances, Wallaces, Claudes, et <il— watched the, to
them, unfamiliar ceremony which took place-on a
verandah of the residence at the bride’s ancestral
sheepery. The original Cape was one of Sydney’s
earliest and best schoolmasters. The Smiths are
mostly on the land in one capacity or another.
’An Australienne who has been studying
‘‘Fashion, the finger-post to fools” (it was a
sbured and badly-dressed man who said that) in
London, Paris and at restaurants like Maxim’s for
A: year past, is back here, and will probably use
her acquirements in Sydney. I saw her just now.
She was the latest thing in demmed outlines, or
should it be “no outline at all?” Foreign cor-
setieres had altered her figure to fit into “model”
rpbes that might be sheaths for a bamboo
wand. Her hemp-straw hat was so up-to-date
that I had to look under it to see her
face, and the rim of the brim of half of it
touched her shoulder. A brush or a broom
stood business end upright to the sky, as if wait-
ing to brush the cobwebs from the blue roof, while
something expensive-looking and limp in the way
of bird’s plumage trailed other-shoulderward.
Her gloves were white, with thick sewings of
green silk on the hacks, her patent-leathers were
topped with antelope, her coat and skirt showed
that an artist’s eye had guided the hand that held
the cutlery.
To be white-faced and crimson-lipped is the
correct thing in complexions in Paris, said
the returned native. But so it has been
for some years. The aristos of the Fau-
bourg St. Germain, and the demi-mondaines
of the public bals, are alike snow-white and
rose-red in these particulars. But it is sur-
prising the number of men who “make up”
their faces. They use rouge—the rouge their
women have discarded—on their cheeks. One
night, at Maxim's, the loveliest creature the
Australienne had ever seen strolled in. She was
naturally young and beautiful and the rest, and
wore elegant clothes and jewellery. Three men
followed. Anyone could see she was a daisy—a
regular marguerite. Why? Because she was
there in so remarkable a get-up. A coat and
skirt would have been the tiling if she were not
out to attract attention. The three men were
obviously saying the complimentary thing in
chorus. The dazzler glanced at the stranger,
and. noting her look of wide-eyed interest,
smiled amicably, drank to her health, and then
broke the glass, remarking to her escorts in her
own Gallic that “Mademoiselle’s admiration was
worth all theirs.” It was very good-natured of
the “creature” not to mistake the look of the other
girl for one of envy.
I went to “Cinderella” the other night and
started thinking of the altitudinous (you must be
weary of the “ more than common tall” of Rosa-
lind) young actresses who have played their way
hither from the land which has just scooped the
North Pole. One sky-scraper was Nance O'Neil.
Then there were Maud Jeffries, Ola Humphrey,
Margaret Anglin, and that Widow Something who
mummered so horribly with Julius Knight for a
season. She was handsome, and she didn’t need
to stand on an envelope to look over the roof of
twopence, but her acting was lamentable. Now
We have Meredith Meredro, who is also American
and good to gaze upon by reason of her face
and her stately proportions—the sort of girl
who can drop mullock down the kitchen chim-
ney of the gods without straining herself in
the upward reach. She would be a good
height for a crown prince, let alone for the
fairy potentate she illustrates. When she has
discarded the close-fitting silken wear of the
future Mr. Cinderella, and gotten herself (the
Americans use that veirb) into a black frock that
appears to button tightly from under her left ear
to her corresponding foot, she mentions this
running-to-palm-tree-stature habit of her country-
women. A black hat with wide velvet of a vivid
shade of red and an ermine muff—big enough to
carry a poodle as well as a pair of hands in—are
cheerful accessories after the fact of the black
frock. “I’m only the average height in my
country,” she says. “Five feet six or
seven is quite a usual stature.*' Per-
haps it’s the instinct of reaching out and
up—-to the higher life—that makes for growth.
The alliteratively named artiste is cross-examined
as to methods of skin-feeding and massage in New
York. She has the magnolia-petal quality of
face-epidermis that seems peculiar to the young-
womanhood of the U.S. There is no colorin the
cheeks, yet this clear pallor, as the American
novelists (who always use it in the young heroine’s
outfit) are careful to explain, is the hue of perfect
health ; and skin-feeding and massage have
nothing to do with the case.
This healthy, cheerful fairy is excusably proud
of the fact that the “ Cinderella ” panto, has
broken the record of runs by its seventeen weeks’
presence in Melbourne. One enthusiast of an
auditor was amongst those present on over 120
occasions. Another tore himself from all that
was dismal and witnessed the piece unto 70 times.
Also the lady frankly owns up to better treat-
ment here in the sense of being given a good time
than she experienced in England or America.
“All the mimes who come here and play for us
go back and pray for us—so to speak.” She
smiles, showing her very own teeth that ought
to be taken into account when she is making
terms with a manager for joyous rdles. Miss Mere-
dith fluffs informatively on the subject of bargain-
hunting in New York, and asserts that it is not
a more expensive place to live in than London or
Sydney. Her hotel-bills in the Flat-iron City
and here are about the same, but here she misses
the facilities for getting “shirt-waists.” Don’t the
American magazines show the shirt-waisted girl
with her unfuzzyand central parted hair to advan-
tage? Yes, they do. Which reminds her of an
incident of her first week in Sydney. A woman
wrote to her a rather pathetic note, explain-
ing that she was a fellow-countrywoman
in poor circumstances, and would be glad to do
some frockmaking or the like. As the Fairy
Prince party was just then yearning to have some
airy fairy silken fabric in her possession made into
shirt-waists, she gave the material to her visitor,
who, like the Waratah, has not since been sighted
or heard of. It is presumed that she capsized and
sank.
If I were a moneyed person, and of the class
that gives freak meals to freaks, I believe I would
enjoy giving a fish supper to the educated seals
at the Tivoli. I didn’t believe in love at first
sight until I saw those seals join the audience in
applauding one of their number for a “turn.”
The audience clapped its collective hands, and
the seals clapped their front flippers. Then one
of them flopped along—the seal doesn’t crawl or
walk, he flops—on a tight rope, with nose point-
ing ceilingward, and balancing an open umbrella
on that pointed organ. The balancing of goods
on the nose is their favorite amusement, and they
appear to enjoy their jobs as much as does the audi-
ence. A seal is a squab creature that none would
suspectof acapacitv for playing variousinstruments
in a band, going to bed at word of command and
snoring loudly, or playing cricket, or carrying fire-
arms. And his seal-skin coat fits his blubber so
oreaselesslv you’d think he was sheathed in a
directoire gown. Those Tivoli seals have a
personality of a' lovable When I Came
away I found myself absent-mindedly addressing
a girl with me as “mv dear seal.” I shall get a
seal at once, and keep him in the bathroom. The
rest of the household may go to Balmoral Beach
for its plunge.
Talking of freak parties: A young American
widow, with many millions of dollars and more
looks than were good for her, gave a sliding-down-
the-banisters-party on her coming-out (out of
mourning, of course). The widow is merry else-
where than in the musical comedy.
I have just been gazing at Amy Castles’latest
portrait, with its seraphically rapt expression.
She sits and gazes upward and holds a book on
her knee. “No literature,” I mused, “but that
of Thomas A’Kempis’ famous work could give
that expression of inward peace.” Then I turned
the portrait upside down, got a lens and read the
identity of the book. It was an obscure American
magazine. If it had been The Lone Hand,
now
Maggie Papakura, the well-known Maori guide,
who has been a sojourner here for some time,
returned to her native land last week to gather a
Maori company for displays at Clontarf. The
Balmain Ferry Co. will run the scheme, and the
.Papakura will run the Maori co. The pah that
was used at the M.L. Exhibition of some years ago
will be put up at Clontarf, and the visitors will
Jive there. Hakas, poi dances and other properties
peculiar to aboriginal Maoriland will enliven the
coming Sydney summer.
The “Jack and Jill” company, which intended,
by order of the Czar Ramaciotti, to side-step a
return to Sydney, is to be back here soon, by the
Same order.
A warbling girl who has the soprano gift has
(sent out white and gold requests “for the plea-
sure of your company at a Private Vocal Recital,
ht the Y.M.C.A. Hall in Bathurst-street, on
Friday, Sep. 24, at 8 p.m.” The new song-bird,
who has made a name for herself in West-
ralia. has been studying with Steffani
in Sydney for over a year. Her pretty
designation is Lucille Stuart Benstead, and she
was born at Alice Springs, in the middle of Aus-
tralia. Clara Butt, who gave her a trial stance,
is quoted on the white-and-gold leaflet in mauve
Ink: “ This young lady should bring honor to
herself and her native lahd.”
Nordica, the once famous singer, was married
five or six weeks ago for the third time. No. 1
spouse went up in a balloon and descended in the
wrong way. No. 2 the lady divorced for good and
Sufficient reasons. Her third is a New York
banker named Young.
, An acquaintance arises to say that the young
basso, Percy Graham, told me incorrectly wffien
he mentioned that Melba was married at Mackay,
in Queensland. She was married in Brisbane by
a Presbyterian parson at the Bellevue Hotel. “1
was staying there at the time,” proceeds the cor-
rector, “and saw the wedding alarms and ex-
cursions. Melba’s father and her aunt and a
sister were present for the occasion, and looked as
if they didn’t like it. That’s a bit over 26 years
ago, and Melba had been a governess at a station*
in Mackay. On that same station her bridegroom,
as is well known, was a jackerooi "’They made
Iheir home for a while at Mackay ; that’s how the
mistake occurred, no doubt. Melba wrote her
name opposite her birthday, the 19th of May, in
my birthday book.”
I have received a copy of the Knight Errant,
which is the organ of the Julius Knight Dramatic
Co., and is obtainable from that combination for
the price of four afternoon teas. It is edited by that
sound player, Aubrey Mallalieu. It contains verse,
jests, epigrams and other matter, grave and gay ;
also a number of palpably arch and telling allusions
understandable only by chronic grease-painters.
All, however, are put in the shade and rendered
negligible and of scant human interest by the
tremendous announcement which occurs in the
leading article. Julius, the Incomparable, pro-
poses shortly to essay the rdle of the melancholy
Dane. He intends to play Hamlet. He writes so
himself in the organ of his own dramatic co.
Nothing will stop him. He is resolved. It is as
momentous a bit of news as has burst on Poverty
Point for a decade, and should drive 1724
matinee girls wild with delighted anticipation.
The Norman Lindsay picture Exhibition opens
at Angus and Robertson’s library in Castlereagh-
street, on the day (Thursday) on which this
screed appears in public. I was accorded a
private view of the works on Monday, and
they are exquisite. Most cduntries dis-
cover their geniuses when they have gone
to Heaven ; Australia mostly does it when
they have gone to England. This country
is by no means the grave of the extra-
ordinary artistic talent which it produces—
merely the clearing-house, as it were. The
list of artists w*ho have fled these pastures
and settled elsewhere is indeed amazing,
and now there is danger that Lindsay, whom
overseas critics acclaim as one of the world’s
masters in black and white, may join their num-
ber. However, he stoutly maintains that he means
to return to Australia, where all his interests are,
and to remain away from it no longer than
is needful to place his Casanova illustra-
tions on the London and Paris markets.
His present show contains few of the Casa-
nova drawings, which are, of course, his best.
There are numberless small black-and-white
sketches of figures, animals and so forth, priced
at a guinea and upwards (this is the first Norman
Lindsay show at which the public has had a
chance to secure cheap examples of his work),
some passing fine water-color sketches, a number
of portraits of Sydney’s good-looking society
women, and the inevitable and always alluring
problem picture. The show will continue for a
week and then proceed to Melbourne.
By the way, Hugh McCrae’s “Silvarum Libri,”
which contains eight Norman Lindsay auto-litho-
graphs done on stone, is on the market. The
volume may be had from J. Lane Mullen, at
Challis House, Sydney, for a guinea, and it is
worth many such. The Dudley family, Yic.
Governor Carmichael, and other art-lovers are
subscribers to the edition, which is limited to 100
signed and numbered copies.
A Sydney publican is full of w onderment at the
astuteness of an imported serving damsel. She
had an inexplicable love of scrubbing, and used to be
in the bar with bucket and brush every morn-
ing by daylight. Her scrubbing-fury seemed
unnatural, and an occasional incoherence of speech
she ascribed to housemaid’s knee. One Sunday,
when she was out. her mistress inspected her
room and found 28 empty liquor bottles. Her
way was to lift a flask every morning and
drop it into the water in the scrubbing bucket.
Many of the imported girls seem to suffer from a
fixed delusion. They say they were told that-the
excess of the masculine element over the feminine
is something immense in Australia, and that they
would be married within three months of their
arrival. When asked who told them this they
reply “Everybody,” which is vague. It wasn't
the official immigration agents —it was just
“Everybody.” A male friend tells me a weird
story, by the way, of a still more disillusioned
Englishman who was wharf-lumping in Melbourne
some 25 years ago. “This is all the work I can
get here, ”he said. “ And my friends told me that
as soon as a man like me, that had been in the
Town Council in my own place in England,
reached the colony, he would be eagerly sought
after and made Premier.” Inquiry proved that
he came from some quiet little speck in the north
of England—Crockermouth or Thirsk or Tad-
caster.
Maoriland, among other abnormal growths, has
now produced a regiment of women warriors.
The Waikato Mounted Rifles (Corps No. 2) is the
institution’s baptismal designation. They paraded
the other day and were photographed. Some are
young and pretty ; others are decidedly of the
battle-axe order. The information is offered that
the force is “attached” to Corps No. 1, but it
isn’t the sort of attachment which ends in
marriage—only some military arrangement or
other. The corps is not taken very seriously in
war circles, and no one really depicts it as dying
in the last ditch with its gun in its hand and its
best hat trodden underfoot by furious Frank and
fiery Hun and such-like dubious characters.
One of the newest of our nobility materialised
by the side of her head gardener lately holding a
copy of the Amateur Gardener, as if it were
Holy Writ. “Paxton,” she said (or some such
name), “I wish you to read the 4 Monthly Notes ’
in this paper; you will find them most informa-
tive.” “Yes, madam,” quoth Paxton, “they
are good. I write them myself.”
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 6, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik,—
The City Newsboys’ Ball, an annual charity ’op
of much moment, “featured ” itself conspicuously
this season. Society dames had many sets on
hand—so many that it turned the affair practically
into a fancy dress carnival (on strictly important
lines, of course). Military men of scarlet pomp
added to the gaiety of colors, and hunters in pink
coats shocked correct folk for a moment, until the
ameliorating fact that the huntsmen belonged to
a set brought their red-togged presence into
polite focus again. There were two newspaper
sets—of such rivalry that they insisted on lancer-
ing at extreme ends of the ballroom. Mrs. Fred
Payne engineered one, and had used a general’s
eye for picking out dainty girls. Nan O’Hara
had the weight of the Age on her shoulders,
and the Tait chicken cheekily carried an Argus
in her tricorner hat. They were both in black
and white and silver. The Bulletin, a dainty
brunette, had black pompoms in her pink paper
three-cornered hat, and the gay pink of the paper
cover in a cute cape over her black and white
frock. The Herald and Table Talk helped fill
the set, and the men folk wandered about in an
embarrassed way with printer’s ink staining their
white gloves and pleated shirt-fronts. Miss
Incely, in a Punch dress, all pointed cap and
rosettes, made a crusade with various other
journals. Unie Russell flaunted a Royal Show
advt. on her Australasian pink frock.
A military set of vivandiere scarlet and white
had a veritable amazon in the lead. A man near-
ing 7ft. up into the ether led her out, and he gave
her little start in the race skyward. The Forrest
dame begot herself into powder about the hair and
daffodils about the staff—and all her set did like-
wise except the crow-like men, who insisted upon
being 1909 male beings in short unpowdered hair
and ugly tubular garments. A Harvest set,
which may have been intended as a com-
pliment to rural visitors, got in the way
with its unsophisticated poppies. Sheaf hats
and the Two Step don’t mingle worth a cent.
City newsboys, small atoms in the dress of the
British navy, were seriously drilled by Gunner
Cairns, of the Cerberus, and received the guber-
natorial party in solemn state. The Carmichael
lady swept up this warlike avenue in a marvellous
black gown ; it was all waves of shining jet that
broke at last into white tulle surf on the lady’s
shoulders. She trailed an orange scarf. To trail
a scarf is It—the ittest It of the present.
The affair was very military. Hoad, Campbell,
Stanley, Reay and other big guns of our army
sported scarlet and gold. The sporting Gov.
quite enjoyed the reception. The sets paraded
round the room (just like the cattle did t’other day
at the Show), and all the pretty girls bobbed a
curtsey to his beaming Ex., though a severe and
classical chaperone, skied in the balcony, vented her
jealousy by remarking thatit was notcorrect togive
a mere State Governor a curtsey. The matter
becomes appalling when Governors become so
numerous that their rank is thus criticised.
Premier Murray and his wife came along to dance in
the important lancers; so did Senator Best and his
lady. The Asches arrived after their show (they
are insatiable in social matters)—Mrs. Asche a
veritable Cleopatra in vivid scarlet, with gold
wheels weighing down her ears and gold
shoes on her feet. Manager Meyer brought his
wife, Miss Dorothy Grimston, in white, with
blue ingenue ribbons threaded in her hair. A
Madden maiden came early with the gubernatorial
push, and left early. Mrs. Geoff. Syme made a
streak of color with a peacock-colored sheath, and
sister Miss Garnett, in blue and white, sported
grown-up hair for the first time.
I saw the returned Stockfis there—that bachelor
of Holland who has been from these shores for
many moons. He created a stir in the simple,
unmercenary hearts of the society maidens, for
the gentleman has buckets of money. Mrs.
Artist M'Cubbin trailed faint blue toggery. Mrs.
Flinders-lane Felstead wore expensive green.
Last year this particular dance was over-run with
Fleet officers, and our men could only cadge what
dances the favored buttoned beauties didn’t use.
But these times are past, and this year the
girls rolled languishing eyes to the men
of this city. Miss Onians, who secretaries
the arduous work of the Street Boys’ Club, helped
considerably with the ball. The Naval Newsboys
(all about the size of three-ha’pence) were put on
guard to separate the most important fancy sets.
There was something of a boy-Casabianca-
burning-deok pose about these youngsters.
In a square they were placed—in a square
they would stand whatever happened. They
were glued to the floor like toy soldiers
—weighted down with serviceable big boots.
In the rush of the lancers they were almost
overwhelmed by fancy-dress, ornate drapery and
swishing trains. They scarcely moved—hut most
of the dancers went outside after and picked small
boys from the lace of their trains—like burrs.
All the week Show —“shee-o-w, show”—(to
misquote Mrs. Sans Gene) has been the keynote.
The Backbone of the country has mixed itself in a
most aggressive way with the rest of its anatomy.
The slow deliberation of the Backbone has
congested the traffic, stopped trams, and
even bailed up motor-cars. The post office
wealth of steps has proved a godsend for pave-
ment-weary sons of leisure, and the honeymooners
are in such battalions as to make glad the heart of
the man of statistics. The newly-weds are mak-
ing their town visit to get a real town photograph.
Upper Bourke-street has quite a French pro-
vincial flavor by reason of brides walking to the
photographers guileless of such extravagance as a
cab, but in full bridal gear—white shoes, veil, etc.
—and the accenting background of hubby s pink
ecstatic face framed in a new hard hat and a pale
blue tie.
The Show day (Thursday), when Governors and
such swell the crowd and make the entrance fee
double, was spent by that species mostly in
lunching and speeching. Carmichael spoke faceti-
ously, and strung together phrases that he had
culled from last year’s Show orations. He re-
marked especially on a phrase from Deakin,
that the future of the country was “wrapped
in its rural industries.” The thing was
so apt that the Excellent one chortled
“I’ve kept it for agricultural show's ever since.”
Prime Minister Deakin and Premier Murray
tried their durnedest to keep to peaceable rural
topics, but were just held back by force from
snapping and snarling in political jargon. Allan
Currie was getting on to a good wicket
when he lost his notes, floundered in
the centre of a sentence, and dropped
out in a hopeless funk. Tom Cumming
could not be found to speak for the Judges, so
Johnstone, of military methods and horsey lore,
spoke instead. This stretched into something
like two hours, while the wives of the spouters
tried not to look bored. The Carmichael lady
was in black, and the Forrest dame sported a
shriek of purple feather such as no self-respecting
ostrich would countenance on its own. The Gaud
Mayoress, in blue, and a few more befeathered
lady mortals made up the imposing party which
went on to watch the more congenial jumping—
especially the cute little polo ponies being put to
the high jump.
I suppose the most modern thing on exhibition
was a model (quarter size) of the W ilbur Wright air-
ship. It was put in by the Continental Tyre people.
It worked hard, and would have flown away only
that it was tethered. It was a fascinating thing
and stood for the simplicity of genius.
Mrs. L. Daniel left by the Marmora for Eng-
land on August 31. The bronze-haired lady was
one of the beautiful Morphy sisters, and she goes
to stay with another of them—Florence, now
Countess of Darnley, who lives in baronial halls,
with moats and armorial bearings and ghostly
ancestors all over the place.
That important paper baronet, Albert Spicer,
with Mrs. Spicer and the Spicer family, have
made pompous progress through the city. Gates,
the Spicer paper-man-in-chief for Australasia,
looks as if one more banquet would kill him. The
people with the fragrant name start for Banana-
land in their own special hired railway car. It’s
a weighty thing to have so many tons of paper on
one’s mind.
An event of interest to happen shortly is the
executors’ sale of dead Surgeon Fitzgerald’s col-
lection of paintings. The Gemmell-Tuckett
people conduct the business. Fitzgerald owned
Lefebre’s “Chloe” (original), supposed to be the
most beautiful study of the nude among
moderns. Once during his lifetime old Fitz
loaned the precious thing to our National Gallery.
There was an outburst from the mouth that is
in the head which is covered by the poke bonnet
of Mrs. Grundy. To that lady beauty of any kind
is immoral, and the glory of the nude is a thing to
shudder at. The generous surgeon took his trea-
sure back tenderly and with much indignation,
and, so far as is known, it has been in the big,
grey house in Lonsdale-street ever since. Chloe
(a painting that will probably be hunted for by
some of the famous galleries of the world) was only
one of the treasures in Fitzgerald’s collection, and
moneyed art lovers are keen on the scent of the
auction.
An enterprising young lady, Miss Ethel God-
dard, who is hugged closely to the capacious bosom
of the Austral Salon, threatens a dramatic recital
in the Masonic Hall next Saturday. The dramatic
speechifying will be done in partnership with
Mr. George Morgan, and the Strolling Flayers’
orchestra will form a musical background, so to
speak.
Amy Castles is working for her living. Her
recent visible means of support have been seven
concerts in little more than a week, and Amy’s
voice has constituted a large section of the pro-
gramme in each case. Also she has given a gener-
ous measure of encore which was not on the pro-
gramme. She is young to carry such a strain.
Belated sunshine has brought out a new force
in her voice which is full of surprises.
Her houses have been phenomenal. No
foreign artist of whom I have heard has had such
a whole-hearted rush, and not even Melba or
Crossley had more lavish support. Last week the
poor little lady was singing and smiling over the
hideous irritation of toothache with abscess
attached. That was how things were in public ;
in the dressing-room an irate damsel was striding
up and down with a vicious stamp of silver shoes,
and clamoring at an unheeding Fate with the
question : “ Why must I work for my living?”
With a long programme before her and a dentist
waiting to attack her after the performance,
things looked gloomy. Yet the House did not see
a cloud —nothing but the most sweetly good-
tempered face ever moulded on a maiden. The
week included a Ballarat concert. There was a
wild dash there, with the tooth as part of the
luggage ; a reception and other troublesome frill,
with the tooth for company; then the concert,
with the tooth on the platform j and a scurry back
to Melbourne in a train that was too slow to
leave the tooth behind. And in Melbourne there
were more dentist, more concert, and more tooth.
It looks like overworking the flesh. An unwise
management almost killed Amy Castles in her
most youthful singing days by breaking the Act to
Prevent the Sweating of Nightingales, and rush-
ing her over miles of continent, on which jour-
neys she sang at every crossroad and bush hamlet.
“The Village Priest,” at the Princess’s, with
Titheradge back in his old part, made a break in
the long, whirling string of opera-bouffe and pan-
tomime. The play was put in a new-old guise by
being dressed beautifully and accurately in 17th
century clothing and backgrounding. Dorothy
Grimston’s Comtesse lies upon a regal gold and
brocade couch, built in the time of the Louis the
Blankth, and her clinging green satin gown
has a room hung with peach-colored silk for
background, and Armand (Cyril McKay), in
plum-colored piecings, as a contrast. Out-
doors the dame wears Empire gowns all
clinging and hung with filmy scarves, and a dainty
lace cap snoods her hair under a rose-color hat.
Black ninon scarves, subduing her rose-color
gown, supply a tenderly-sad frocking for the
gloomy last act. Mrs. Waller’s Jeanne plays
throughout in a Puritan grey-blue frock and big
brown eyes.
Mrs. Brough was in the house with her new
husband. Her dress was grey and magnificent,
and trained with unnecessary silken yards.
Mrs. Brough is saying farewells—not stagey
ones—to Melbourne. She departs within a week
—she and her new husband —for Engleland, as
Wilson Barrett used to call it—the place Alfred
came from.
It looks as if Titheradge intended spending the
rest of his days in Australia. He is making a
garden on North Head. Tith says there
never was land where so many natural
specimens of botanical valuables grew to
the acre. He is keeping a space in its
natural glory just to grub round and
worship and explore for many years.
Another world-wide botanist —Carl Bogue Luff-
mann—was wont to rave about North Head. He
would tramp that wild country for hours, and
come back to the city gloating over an armful of
weeds. Titheradge says he has made four gardens
in his life —made them with years of toil and
whole-heartedness —only to leave the perfect tilings
to strangers. This will be his fifth, and he hopes
to possess it in its fulness. Australia echoes the
hope.
The Women’s Hospital, where so many unwel-
come little strange;s come from, is to be substan-
tially benefited by the opening of the new West’s
Pictures building next week. The management
of West’s has presented the W.H. committee
with 1000 tickets for the “house-warming” per-
formance, and some of Melbourne’s most expen-
sive people are arranging flickergraph parties for
the occasion. A long evening with pictures will
be a novel experience for many Toorakians, who
have hitherto restricted themselves to homce-
pathic doses of the entertainment that happens
in the gloom. After keeping their eyes fixed on
the screen for an hour at a stretch, they will feel
the Flicker germ stirring in tlieir blood, and get
a notion of why so many thousands of their fellow
mortals have acquired a craving for films. The
prevailing darkness is said to have a lot to do
with it. Couples who would merely like to hold
hands at the theatre are able to grasp the oppor-
tunity at a picture show. The silent eloquence
of beating hearts fills the air. Waves of sym-
pathy flow from Jim and Cis in the sixpenny
seats to Algy and Gwen in the reserved stalls,
and one touch of nature makes the whole audience
kin.
Claviola recitals —Allan’s gave another of them
at the Masonic last Friday afternoon —set one
wondering why the musical’phones are not of tener
exploited at invitation concerts in the big halls.
The Claviola matinees must be intended to adver-
tise the latest “ music rolls” rather than to push
the sale of the machine that plays the piano.
People possessed of ’olas go to hear the new
records. A ’phone recital attracts probable
buyers of the instrument as well as numbers
who have already got it but are selecting some
fresh pieces for it to play. And for such as don’t
think of buying anything it is a pleasant means
of passing half an hour before tea time. One can
suddenly get up and walk from a ’phone perform-
ance without any compunction. The machine
takes no notice and the whirling disc careth not.
MissFrederika Meyer, who gave an elocutionary
recital at the Austral Salon way back in the time
before the wet weather set in, is going to be “ pro-
duced” on a more elaborate scale at one of the
Collins-street halls early in October.
From “ Rosna,” of Adelaide :
The Derby had to be postponed from Saturday till
Wednesday, because it wasn’t thought advisable to turn
it into a swimming race. Morphettville, which is along-
side the Sturt, was a flowing river, and the totes and
stands were islands in a watery waste. The course was
hung out to dry, however, and portions of it were visible
by YVednesday. The public then came along in modera-
tion, furred, coated and umbrella-ed; for the water-
pot gentleman still had designs on our millinery. The
one note of joy was struck over the brand-new edition of
the woman’s tote. It is three times its former size, and
the bloodshed at the windows will now be considerably
less. There were three dividends during the day to make
your hair curl; but the Government House people
weren’t there to collect any of them, as her Ex., a
daughter and an aide had previously booked themselves
to go and buy a few things they didn’t want at a f§te at
Magill.
The “ Appendreena” Amateurs gave a performance of
Haddon Chambers’ “ The Idler,” at the YValkerville Hall
the other eve, and 40 or so electric cars, crammed to the
footrails with frilly guests, shot out to it. The girly lead
was taken this time by Kathleen Sutherland, who wore
five or six different and decorative chiffons, and looked
very charming. She was a rather too calm and collected
heroine, however. Muriel Craigie made a delightful old
lady, but her talents were concealed beneath a small part.
Sundry clever men shed light on the performance, includ-
ing Wilfred Neill, whose Idler was an artistic study, and
A. S. Lewis, who was the American gentleman.
The YVillie Horns’ eldest daughter, Penelope, was mar-
ried off on August 28, at Wimbledon, that portion of the
British speck where the family has bought an ancestral
acre. The bridegroom’s full name is Joseph Anthony
Douglas Bell, and he is a portion of the 96th Berar In-
fantry of the Indian Army. And owing to the condensed
habits of the cable, that is nearly all we know about the
matter at present.
Brewster Jones, who took an Elder Scholarship to the
Bleak Country some years ago, and has since been work-
ing hard on the pianofortes of Britain, is coming back
hurriedly to South Aus. by the Suevic. His unexpected
return is based on a solemn medical warning to get away
home out of the fogs. Meantime a French firm of piano-
builders has obligingly thrust a fine instrument upon
him, and later oil he is to play on it to the Kangaroo,
partly in the hope that the Kangaroo will change his
popular brand of piano.
As we feel it our solemn duty to export something in
the musical line every year, fiddler Willie Cade is being
got ready for a passage to Europe. He is a gifted lad
with a soulful fiddle and musical hair, and the usual
complimentary concert will happen during the month.
The Tourist Bureau is feeling happy. Foster Fraser
not only visited it, but wrote in its autograph book—
Adelaide is one of the most beautiful cities I have ever
seen.” And so it is. Of course we haven’t got a Har-
bor ; but we’ve got a lovely background of wooded hills,
and we’ve got the floweriest, shrubbiest suburbs from
here to the place where Dr. Cook has been.
One of our country papers, the Bordkr Watch, has
just fixed up journalist Beaumont Smith for life. You
know it was cabled the other day that, while in London
with Bilanderson, he had engaged Muriel Matters, elocu-
tionist and suffragette, to come to Australia and lecture.
Evidently the Watch looked hurriedly at the printed re-
mark, saw something with a man and a girl and an en-
gagement in it, and promptly published the couple under
the heading “Matrimonial.”
The Lund liner Geelong returned several citizens to us.
One was Dr. Herbert Shorney, who has been repairing
John Bull and his family for five years, part of the time
as house surgeon of Middlesex Hospital, and other parts
at the Throat Hospital and the Royal Ophthalmic Hospital,
Westminster. He is staying at Medindie until he finally
decides whether he will go over and patch up Sydney, or
stay here and mend Adelaide.
Albert Spicer, President of London Chamber of Com-
merce, and his wife and damsel were here for a day or
two last week, getting welcomed. Our Chamber of Com-
merce clasped them by the hand, and a branch of a mis-
sionary society, of which Mr. Spicer holds the purse
strings, told them it was rejoiced to see them. Their
Excellencies went one better, and asked them to dinner ;
and the Chief Justice Ways duplicated this attention.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.
The low estate to which the British Free-
trade working man, once the aristocrat of the
industrial world, has fallen was vividly revealed
the other day at the International Labor Con-
ference at Paris. There an English delegate pro-
mised that the British Labor party would endeavor
to secure an enlargement of the Foreign Enlist-
ment Act so that it could be used to prevent the
enlistment of the British workman for service as a
cheap blackleg and strike-breaker on the Continent
of Europe.

Pure Herbal Skin Soap.
Important Analyses,
F. H. MOLESWORTH, F.C.S., F.G.S., Mem. Soc. Chem. Ind. London; Public Analyst and Assayer;
Late Lecturer in Chemistry S.A. Government and Technical College, Sydney.
Central Analytical and Assay Office, Vickery’s Chambers, 82 Pitt Street, Sydney, 21st September, 1908.
Messrs. H. E. KUGELMANN & CO., 199 Clarence Street, Sydney. Gentlemen,—l have made a careful analysis of a sample of “ Kugelmann’s Pure Herbal Skin Soap,” which I find to be composed of
vegetable oils and fats, saponified with pure alkali. It contains no free alkali, nor any substance deleterious to the most delicate skin. The perfume and color are both of natural and harmless plant origin,
neither having any trace of animal or poisonous materials. Having powerful but non-poisonous antiseptics thoroughly Incorporated in the process of manufacture, this soap possesses healing. properties
which will remove diseases of the skin and restore it to a healthy condition. It is a beautifully finished article, comparing favorably with similar imported soaps, and if used regularly as an adjunct of the
toilet, it will assist in keeping the body in health, and will have a pleasing, soothing effect on the skin. ‘‘Kugelmann’s Herbal Skin Soap” will NOT promote the growth of hair upon the face, or
anywhere else upon the human body where hair does not grow naturally Yours faithfully, F. H. MOLESWORTH, F.C.S., F.G.S., M.S.C.I. London, Public Analyst and Assayer.
Chemical and Assay Laboratory, 97 Pitt Street, Sydney, 7th July, 1908.
Messrs. H. E. KUGELMANN & CO., Sydney. Gentlemen,—We have analysed a sample of “ Kugelmann’s Herbal Skin Soap,” and find it to be a well manufactured article, containing no free alkali, or
any injurious substance. It is made from pure vegetable oils, and contains a non-poisonous but powerful antiseptic. This soap has nothing in it that can possibly injure the skin, it has strong curative
properties, and will be also suitable as a dentifrice. There is nothing in “ Kugelmann’s Herbal Skin Soap ” to Injure the hair in any way, or to cause hair to grow on the face or on any part of the body
where hair does not normally grow. Yours faithfully, DIXON & BYRN, F.1.C., F.C.S., Analyists under the Public Health Act, and Assayers.
Analytical Laboratory, Lincoln Inn Chambers, 38 Elizabeth Street, Sydney, 16/4/’O9.
Messrs. H. E. KUGELMANN & CO., 199 Clarence Street, Sydney. Gentlemen,—l have made an analysis of a sample of “Kugelmann’s Herbal Skin Soap,” with the result that 1 find no excess of
moisture, and it does not contain any free alkali, or any injurious substance. It has In my opinion been prepared from carefully selected and pure ingredients. The soap does not contain any substance
which can possibly injure the skin, and it is suitable for use as a dentifrice; it has a very delicate and fragrant aroma, and contains a non-poisonous antiseptic. It should, therefore, prove eminently
useful for toilet and curative purposes. Yours faithfully, (Signed) JAMES MAYNE, Public Analyst under Health Act; Late Analyst City Council, Sydney ; Late Examiner in Chemistry, Pharmacy Board.

AS a SKIN FOOD. SHAVING SOAP and
TOILET PREPARATION, KUGEL-
MANN’S PURE HERBAL SKIN
SOAP is fast achieving a position before
all others as the Soap for special treatment for the
complexion and skin, as well as for medical,
hygienic, shaving and general toilet purposes.
The main reason for this unique assumption of
public favor is that KUGELMANN’S HERBAL
SKIN SOAP is not merely an article of ordinary
commercial manufacture, but is carefully pre-
pared under the direction and from the prescrip-
tion of Australia’s Leading Consulting Herbal
Practitioner, so as to really constitute a part of
his treatment. In short, two great principles
form the solid base of this Herbal Soap :
1. That ABSOLUTE PURITY is essential in
any article of constant application to that delicate,
vital organ, the Skin.
2. That soap affords one of the readiest mediums
for the medical treatment of the human body.
The HERBAL SKIN SOAP in its present
form represents the fruit of nearly forty years’
experience in the use and prescription of beneficent
herbs for the cure of Australian maladies, and,
therefore, is, better adapted to the climatic and
other conditions of Australian life than any
imported soap.
For the Prevention qf Redness and Skin Irrita-
tion of all kinds, the cure of Eczema, Tetter,
Ringworm, Shingles, Blotches, Pimples and
Freckles; the prevention and cure of Dandruff,
fostering a stimulating effect upon the growth of
the. Hair, stopping falling-out, inducing growth,
and making it soft and glossy. As a dentifrice, it
arrests decay and produces a dazzling brilliance.
For Shaving, is of Unique Benefit, owing to its
production of a rich, lasting and creamy lather, and
the exercise of soothing and hygienic, functions
upon the skin.
In the nursery its beneficent effects upon the
tender skin of infancy obviates the use of powder,
etc., the child’s skin being always kept cool and
smooth by its use.
“ Beauty’s Aid ” is the well-earned title of this
Herbal Skin Soap, because, in a healthy and per-
manent manner, it enhances natural beauties as
no mere nostrum can ever hope to do. In every
department of the toilet, and for every medicinal
or hygienic purpose for which soap can be used,
this soap has proved superior to any other soap.
The Greatness of the Herbal Skin Soap is the
greatness of Nature. It is a rare combination of
natural organic remedies culled from the grand
and simple provision of Mother Earth, whose
vegetable kingdom contains in abundance the only
medicines suited for assimilation by mankind.

Herbal Skin Soap is indicated by the following
selection of testimonials, which are portion of those

A LITTLE COMMON-SENSE.
In using any kind of medicine a little common-
sense is always necessary. Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Rills are compounded to meet the general
requirements of man, woman and child, both
weak and strong, and in prescribing the doses of
from one to four pills, the actual number necessary
in each case must be determined according to the
age and condition of the patient. A dose, how-
ever, should be sufficient to move the bowels at
least twice, and although relief may be obtained
at once, it is always advisable to take a pill or two
on the following night.
In some instances the first dose may cause a
little pain and sickness, which will not recur sub-
sequently. The cause is due to the large flow of
bile into the intestines, which the Pills have let
loose by opening the congested ducts which have
been clogged up, perhaps, for months. Do not
blame the Pills if under these conditions you feel
sick. You will feel better afterwards, and if you
adopt Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills as your
regular medicine, there is no likelihood of your
liver getting in the same state again.*
INTIMATION IS HEREBY GIVEN that a
Writ has been presented under “The Pre-
sumption of Life Limitation (Scotland) Act, 1891”
to the Sheriff Court of Forfarshire at Forfar,
Scotland, at the instance of James Cargill, Senior,
Retired Police Constable, 43 King Street, Mont-
rose, and others, craving the Court to find that
Alexander Cargill, Shipmaster, who went to
Australia about 1885, and subsequently shipped
on various vessels from Australian ports, and who
was a son of the said James Cargill, Senior, has
disappeared, and that he shall be presumed to
have died on 31st January, 1896; in which Writ
a deliverance has been pronounced inter alia
appointing intimation of said Writ and the pur-
port of said deliverance to be made forthwith by
advertisement once in each of the People’s
Journal, Forfarshire Edition, and the Sydney
Bulletin, Newspapers, and ordaining any parties
interested to lodge objections, if so advised, in the
hands of the Clerk of Court at Forfar on or before
the 17th. day of November, 1909, of all which
notice is hereby given.
JAMES CUM MING. Law Agent,
49 High Street, Montrose,
Agent for Pursuers.
