SPORTING
NOTIONS
Football’s brief wintry day, is sinking to a close
in Sydney, and on Saturday the Rugby Union brought
its First Grade competition to a close. The Soccer people
also finished their struggle for the premiership and
Rawson Cup; while all other divisions are in their last
stages.
* * * +
The overthrow of Newtown by South Sydney
(Union) the previous week was responsible for much of
the elation with which the South barrackers greeted their
team. But cooler heads remembered that Glebe had, on
points, much the best of South Sydney, having scored
213 for the season as against South’s 139, and
that fact meant a lot. All the same, the game
opened badly for Glebe. South came away with
splendid rushes, and worked the line for every
ounce it was worth, and drew first blood in the shape of
an unconverted try. Glebe settled down to a bullocking
game—a sort of infantry attack on long-range artillery
and light cavalry. Later on it changed the tactics and
converted the infantry into heavy cavalry, which rolled
right through the struggling South men and bore them
down by sheer weight and brute force. The score was 17
points to six. An extra spice of excitement was added
by the defection of the Wallabies, and South was so hard
put to it that mercenaries had to be rung in under
assumed names to make up the cohort. Glebe had better
luck in that respect; so, taking all things into considera-
tion, South put up a magnificent scrap.
The rebellion of the Wallabies is threatening to
make history, and the matter will, in all probability, be
finally fought out in the law-courts. It seems that the
dragging-in of the South Sydney Hospital as a stalking-
horse was a somewhat more flagrant affair than reported
in last iosue. The agreement actually fixed up by the
League committee, in spite of the protests of Knox, pro-
vided for four charges on the gate of the three
matches. The first charge was for an indefinite
amount, to be taken by Mr. J. Joynton Smith
to reimburse his guarantee to the Wallabies. At the
time it was not stated what the Wallabies were to
get, but it is now generally understood that it was £l5OO
—£loo each to 13 men, £l5O to another and £5O to one.
The second charge was to pay ads. and ground expenses.
The third was to pay an indefinite, unstated “bonus” to
the Kangaroos. The fourth charge was an equal division
of what remained over between the League and the South
Sydney Hospital. Probably both the League and South
Sydney Hospital will sell their shares dirt cheap.
What the League requires most just now is
some public-spirited member to bring the matter before
the courts and biff out the committee that has done this
thing. The situation is humorous, and must cause
Messrs. Giltinan, Trumper and Hoyle pain in their sides.
When they ran the League they banked sums of money
to a Trust Account, a sort of secret campaign fund,
which was to be used to attract good players and gener-
ally to foster the interests of the League by secret but
not dishonorable means. How they administered that
fund does not matter just now. But the League eventu-
ally rose and kicked them out of office for creating it.
And it was this very committee that did most of the
kicking. Now it turns round and boots Knox and
O’Sullivan out because they will not agree to do what
Giltinan, Trumper and Hoyle were sacked for doing.
Briefly, the position is this: A match was
arranged between the Wallabies and the Kangaroos by
Mr. J. J. Smith, who guaranteed the Wallabies a certain
sum, now said to be £l5OO. Mr. Smith is not an office-
holder in the League. He is said to have had his owm
men on the gates, collected the cash, deducted his per-
centage, and banked it in his own bank. All the League
committee did was to give a sort of open order to this
gentleman, who was, after all, an outsider. Yet by-law
1, clause A, declares that the League is to control
Rugby League football throughout the State of N.S.W.,
and to have entire management thereof. In this case
Mr. Smith seems to have managed. The general com-
mittee which sanctioned this affair has apparently ex-
ceeded its powers, as it can only administer the League
according to the Constitution, and any changes in the
Constitution have to be passed by a referendum. The
rules also provide that the net proceeds of matches shall,
after the deduction of ground per centages and adver-
tising exes., wages of gatemen and so forth, be placed to
the credit of the League. Yet it is reported that they have
been placed to the credit of someone else. The rules limit
the remuneration of players to compensation for actual
loss of time while playing, and still further limit it by the
statement that the sum must not exceed 10s. per day.
Most of the Wallabies are said to have exceeded that
amount, and if the Wallabies do not count as members of
the League the committee is still in the soup, as it is pro-
posed to give the Kangaroos a bonus, which is to be a
third cut at the gate money.
League Rugby, as played under the League
Constitution, is not professional football ; but as played
between the Wallabies and Kangaroos it is professionalism
out and out. Few of the footballers earn more than
£2 per week; so they were guaranteed practically
a year’s salary each for a series of three matches. It is re-
ducing football to private enterprise, and making it a wild
scramble for cash. It is making the League merely a bogus
sporting body, whose premises will practically be an office
where private syndicators will recruit footballers to play
for fixed sums and portions of the gate ; and the end will
be the ruin of the League and its disappearance.
Also, it may result in a lot of legal expense to some
estimable gentlemen ; for if the matter goes to the courts
there seems to be no escape from the fact that the rules
have been blown to smithereens, and that the committee
has usurped the functions and authority of a general
meeting of the members.
Just here some remarks about the actual match
might be of interest. Twenty thousand people looked on
and expected to see something that would knock corners
off the displays against the Maoris. But they didn’t.
The Wallabies played a constrained sort of game. The
Kangaroos seemed listless, as though it was bad busi-
ness to romp over the newcomers even when they got the
chance, which they did often. They scored turn about
in a way that was monotonous, and at half time the
Wallabies led by 18 to 16. Then the Kangaroos
went ahead, and the Wallabies lagged behind. But, later
on, the pendulum swung back, and the Kangaroos lagged
till the Wallabies came alongside. And still later, with a
final spurt, the Kangs, scored another try, and the whistle
blew', leaving the new converts in a minority of three.
* « *■ «
The last match of the Soccer competition was
that between the terrible Pyrmont and Northern
Suburbs, and went to the terrible ones by eight goals to
nil. Pyrmont’s score for the season stands at 14 victories,
one defeat and one draw. Of 32 possible points it scored
29, and during the season it kicked 52 goals, with only 16
kicked into its own net. Rozelle, second on the list, won
11 matches, lost three and drew two, scoring only 24
points out of the possible 32, with 38 goals kicked for 14
made against it. Granville is the third on the list, and
after that Sydney, Balmain and Ultimo.
, . - * m
The Gardiner Cup trouble (Soccer) will come to
a finish on Saturday next, when Pyrmont, with its cham-
pionship fresh upon it, will meet in battle array the men
of Newcastle, the tyrants of the North, at Sydney Cricket
Ground. And great will be the struggle, for the Gardiner
Cup is the historic trophy of Sydney Soccerdom, and
dates back to the misty past of 1885.
Sydney and Paddington, which were at the
bottom of the Australian Rules list last year, are this time
at the top ; so the Scripture which said the last shall, be
first and so forth is fulfilled. And they got there by
clean playing. It will be a case of virtue rewarded if
either of them wins the Rawson Cup, and the chances
are that North Sydney will accomplish that feat, with
Paddington as runner-up, Y.M.C.A. third, and “the
dirty sloggers” (Newtown) fourth. Fourth is too
good for Newtown ; it ought to be in the
duck-pond. On Saturday, Newtown managed to
down the Good Young Men by just one little point. The
G.Y.M. had all the best of the actual playing, but their
kicking was deplorable. Had they but kicked as many
goals as behinds, the Lord would not have looked side-
ways while Newtown won. On Saturday next Newtown
meets North Shore, and it is to be hoped the authorities
will deal roughly with the Newtown hoodlums who
usually play the man and not the ball. One cheerful
feature of the game is that Bob Errol, a mighty pug.
before the Lord, will be playing with the Y.M.C.A., and
the fact may have a restraining influence on the dirty
sloggers.
* * * *
Maoriland is a place of mighty Rugby foot-
ball records. But recently a mammoth score of 91 points
was put up bv a winning team. Now there is a great
searching of records to find a better waste of boot.
* * * *
When the church takes to football, the names
are fearsome. St. Simon and St. Jude recently won its
competition with 329 points scored against three, every
game won, the last being finished with eight to three. So
for the whole season right up till the very last match, St.
Simon and St. Jude had not had a solitary point scored
against it.
* * * *
In Melbourne’s League ’oofball on Saturday
there were only two games that counted; but they
mattered a lot to Essendon and Melbourne, which col-
lided with Carlton and’Wood respectively, inasmuch as
Melbourne was but half a game behind the ’Dons. The
authorities thoughtfully studied the niceties of the posi-
tion by posting the scores of each game on the other
ground, so that the contestants could see how things
stood with them. Both events were a complete answer to
those know-alls who asserted that Carlton and ’Wood,
having nothing whatever to gain by winning, w'ould
not unduly distress themselves. At East Mel-
bourne the high wind blew across the ground
over a packed “house,” and a joyous, swift and
determined encounter ensued. The “Same Old” had
a trifle the better of the scuffle through the first half,
Carlton’s shooting being painfully erratic. After the spell
the Old Blues of Carlton set out to win, and had put on
an eight-point advantage by three-quarter time. Then
the information was published that Melbourne was but
three goals behind ’Wood, and had the wind for the finish,
and the ’Dons saw their semi-final hopes fading ; so they
bogged in something tremendous. For 15 of the last 25
minutes they put up a sustained, determined attack on
Carlton’s goal, and had reduced the lead to one point,
when the Old Blues decided to stand no more nonsense.
They didn’t. Before the ’Dons had time to realise it,
Carlton had added another 14 points, and ran out winners
by 49 to 34. And, after all, the tremendous struggle
went for nothing, for Collingwood had put up
a finish that knocked Melbourne silly. Starting with the
gale right behind them, the Magpies methodically placed
46 points to seven on the board. Changing over, the Red
Legs had their little say, the figures at the interval being
’Wood 57, Melb. 48. At three-quarter time, ’Wood had
increased its lead by only nine points—Bo to 62—and the
old club, with the knowledge that Essendon was losing,
took the wind with high hopes. Then came the shock —a
repetition of that marvellous game between the same
teams, when the Magpies, in 20 minutes, turned a lost
event into a draw. Playing the old systematic
game, with short, low, skimming kicks from man
to man, they added 28 points, while Melbourne
could only scratch up a paltry four—loB points
to 66, and Melbourne passed out of public life till May 10.
Of the other scuffles South won, with little credit, from
Saints by 57 to 35 ; Geelong scrambled on to the penulti-
mate rung of the ladder by knocking up 92 points to
Richmond’s 31 ; and ’Varsity and ’Roy indulged in an
elegant dissertation which gave neither party the advan-
tage—6o points both being the record. As the Students
now retire from public view for the season, this seems to
be the place to point out that their lamentable habit of
kicking behinds when goals are wanted has lost them four
distinct games this season—-and four more wins would
have put them among the elect.
While these episodes were transpiring, an even
more strenuous afternoon was being spent on the Mel-
bourne ground, where Brunswick was essaying to get into
the Ass. final, and P’ran was trying to keep it out. P’ran,
being on top at the end of the first round, has a second
chance—a sort of “ challenge ” final—but it has been
walloped twice by Essendon this season, and it wanted to
keep that “ saver ” in case it was bumped out in the
“first” final. But Brunswick willed otherwise. The
Ass. has lately been handing out suspensions with
a lavish hand—last week it retired four ’bailers
until June TO—wherefore the game was of a
distinctly genteel character, though the barracker
misbehaved himself as usual. An unusually com-
petent umpire early impressed on the players the dis-
advantage of playing anything but the ball, and as things
settled down the discussion developed into one of the
prettiest games seen this year—fast, clean, open and
hotly-contested right through. Just as at Collingwood,
the Ass. Magpies (’Wick) started with the wind, and led
in the first and third quarters. At the last change the
Two Blues were but three points to the bad, and looked
as if going to win handiß', but that last quarter was a
replica of the other Magpie uprising. ’Wick piled on 21
more points to P’ran’s six, and got into the final by 54
points to 36. Whereat there was much “ Blime,” also
“ What price the two Magpies?” for premiers.
9 ft * *
One of Barry’s reasons for not going to Maori-
land to row Dick Arnst was that by doing so he would
miss the £5OO Sculling Handicap on the Thames. Well,
he might as well have missed it for all the good he did. as
he was beaten and appears to have been unplaced. The
winner was a youthful phenomenon named G. R. Luck,
and he came out of the race worth £250 more than he
went into it. The same youth subsequently won Dog-
gett’s coat and badge, which carries with it ten quid to
make the coat feel comfortable. Altogether, Luck is in.
* * * *
Notwithstanding the fatuousness of the Bench,
the police are still struggling to make Centennial Park
(Sydney) a place of reasonable safety for children ; and
last week a batch of horse trainers were roped in. These
undesirable gentry have for years been using the park as
a cheap training ground, and in defence pleaded custom.
The S.M. let them down lightly with £2 fines ; and said
something about more next time. But £2 per time will
scare the trainers off, and when the motor hog and the
cycling fiends have been wiped out, the glorious park will
once more be a place of safety, in which the children can
romp and breathe fresh air without gambling on their
lives.
* * 9 9
The Dunlop Rubber 00. has been overwhelmed
with nominations for the Warrnambool-Melbourne road
race, no less than 550 gentlemen having sent in their
papers. The popularity of this ride is amazing, and the
men who win the little bronze and enamel medallions
issued by the Dunlop people are as proud of them as old
campaigners are of the V.C.
* * * *
The Bathurst-to-Sydney cycle race was run
under ideal weather conditions on Saturday. No less
than 110 acceptors faced the starter at the Royal Hotel,
Bathurst, and 70 competed. Fortunately for the riders,
people along the line of riding took sufficient interest in
the affair to provide refreshments. The winner turned
up in H. Lundie, of Merrylands (N.S.W.), and F. D. Wal-
cott scored the fastest time. He covered the distance in
7hr. 24mjn. lOsee., and secured third prize.
* * * *
“Vulcan” : The “very latest ” in correct Eng-
lish country invitations, according to Henry Lucy, is to
print along with “R.S.V.P.” a carefully detailed list of
the police motor traps between the guest’s point of de-
parture and his prospective dinner.
9 * * *
Victorian Cricket Association has been much
exercised in mind re the recent victory of the N.S.W.
body over the Cricket Ground Trust, and proposes to do
likewise. The Melbourne Cricket Ground is in a much
weaker position than the Sydney Cricket Ground, as it is
said that there is no title to the territory, and that it was
merely grabbed in the good old-fashioned manner by
squatting. However, the Cricket Ass. will proceed cau-
tiously, and will ask for applications from bodies control-
ling grounds to supply playing space for inter-State and
international matches. The projected visits of S. African
and Englishman teams in 1910 and 1912 have stirred it to
action to secure a larger share of the gate money.
• * * *
Pearce and Day, who are to row for the cham-
pionship of Australia on the 25th inst., have not yet de-
cided whether they will row for £lOO or £2OO a side. Pre-
sumably one or the other has doubts about his ability to
raise the second century ; but if the money can be raised
it will be gambled for cheerfully.
» * * *
Finding that Barry of England and Durnan of
Canada are not wildly profligate with their offers for
expenses, Arnst has decided to stay in southern climes,
and give Australasians a show to win his title. There
doesn’t seem to be much money in sculling in furrin parts
these days.
■*■*•-»*
The Australian XI. got home on the M.C.C.
last week. Last time they met the M.C.C. won by
three wickets. This time Australia piled up 434 against
188 and 187 for one wicket, when down came the rain so
heavily that the players swam for shelter and safety.
Ransford was the bright particular star of the match,
and smote up 190, while the only Trumper retired with
a modest 80 to his score.
* * * *
Leaving out the picnic game against Scotland,
Australia has scored 19 centuries this trip against
England’s nine. Also, after losing three matches in the
first seven, the Australians have staved off defeat in the
remaining 28.
* • « *
The N.S.W. lacrosse team in Queensland was
utterly routed. The first match was lost by two to 22,
and then the Queensland second XI. came along and
rubbed it in by seven to five, and finally the State team
again downed the invaders by nine to two. The sooner
Tahmmy gives the N.S.W. team some lessons, as he
offered to do, the better.
* • * *
Affairs in Vic. trotting circles have been very
unhappy of late. The unpleasantness culminated re-
cently when the Melbourne Trotting Club, which is
mostly Citizen Wren, seceded from the Vic. Trotting
Association and threatened to go a-galloping on its lone-
some. Of course, Wren controls Richmond, Ascot and
Fitzroy courses, and as he gives big prize money the
owners couldn’t do without him ; but under Bent’s
Gaming Act only 20 days of trottingper annum are allowed
round Melbourne, which days the Chief Secretary appor-
tions. So Chief Secretary John Weary was dragged into
the dispute, and having listened till he was more tired
than ever, ukased that the days parcelled out for
trotting should remain as at present distributed, and
that the Association should give Wren’s club and
the associated owners a trifle more representation
than they had hitherto. Also all disqualifications on
account of the friction—as Mr. Barney Reynolds called it
—should be removed. Moreover, the Ass. withdrew all
imputations and insinuations against Billy Whitburn—-on
condition of his abandoning his law action against it—
remitted all fines and penalties imposed on him, restored
his license to ride, drive and train trotters, and also re-regis-
tered his prad Harold Boy, ever which all the trouble
occurred. So now all is sweet peace and holy calm, the
only unaccountable circumstance about the episode
being the failure of Mr. P. Boardman to rush into the
proceedings. Any uprising of Wren’s seems incomplete
without Mr. P. Boardman.
Caulfield (Vic.) racecourse seems safe for a little
while yet. Some of the suburb wants to abolish it; but
when the matter came “ up ” at the municipal election the
other day, Blunden, the abolitionist, was beaten by 51
votes in a poll of 695. Judging by the row that the stable
boys made, it looked as though Blunden was going to be
passed out by 690 votes, and the narrow margin leaves
various people feeling uncomfortable.
r ** * *
The 28th volume of the Australian Racing
Chronicle, just issued by J. J. Miller (Melb.), seems to be
somewhat bulkier than any previous volume of the series.
It contains the record of 856 race-meetings which hap-
pened in the Commonwealth between February 1 and
July 31, and the index shows that 1230 different horses
took part in the various meetings. A considerable num-
ber of those overworked animals ran in at least 10 races
during the six months. Some of them were saddled up
more than 20 times, but as to how many times they were
“on the job ” the Chronicle gives no information. On
ail other racing points it is a very informative publication.
In their match with Kent county, the other
week, the Australian Eleven met three of the bowlers—
Carr, Blythe and Woolley—who were expected to get
them out in the fifth test match. The collective average
of those three bowlers was two wickets for 198 ; but then
the two men who pasted the bowling and made 296
runs between them were Armstrong and Ransford, whilst
Bardsley, with 48, was the next highest scorer. The
awful humiliation of Kent was not surprising after all.
Figures up to July 10 showed that Armstrong, Ransford
and Bardsley were high in the list of English batting
averages. They were the only Australians among the
30 leading batsmen, and their places were first, third
and fifth respectively.
* * * *
The day this appears brudder Johnson will be
fighting Kauffman in California—unless the usual hitch
occurs. The scrap will not give either gentleman much
time to show his ferocity, as it is limited to 10 rounds;
and the really scientific fighter should be able to avoid
serious injury for 10 rounds. Somehow this writer does
not expect to hear that Johnson has dropped his cham-
pionship.
•* * * *
“ Snell ” : It was thought that Bob Green-
shields, the Vie. lightweight, had bucked up sufficiently
to put his hands all over Hughie Meeghan ; but to my
mind Greenshields never saw the day when he could
whip Meeghan, and is not likely to, bar accidents. Bob is a
bright boxer, but he lacks sting ; there’s not enough mis-
chief in his punch, and he never quite wakes up and
gets at an enemy with deadly intentions. Meeghan, on
the other hand, is as clever as Bob in the dealing-out
department, has better defence, and can hit like the kick
of a camel with either fist. All this was demonstrated in
the course of II rounds fought by the scientists in ques-
tion at the Melbourne Athletic Pavilion. Meeghan led
throughout, did all the forcing, and administered 90 per
cent, of the trouble. In the 11th section of the disturb-
ance he beat Bob so thoroughly that the latter’s corner
hung out the clothes to dry. It now remains for Meeghan
to recover the lightweight championship from Jones, of
Sydney.
• 9 9 *
“Silas Snell ” : I see that a British sociologist,
who has written a book on the condition of England, is
quoted with approval by The Bulletin’s “ Red Page.” One
quotation gives, as an instance of English alleged de-
generacy, the avidity of the people to purchase and gloat
over photographs of “ Brudder Johnsing” upper-cutting
little Tommy Burns. This is not the first time some
popular display of interest in the ring has been trotted
out to stress the assertion that J. Bull’s progeny are on
the down grade ; but I submit that pugilism as a sport
was never so unpopular in England as to-day; that
English pugilists are insignificant, and the game does
not attract money enough in Bull’s run to make it
worth a stout man’s while. This indifference to fist-
fighting, and not the imagined avidity is, if you like,
some evidence of the decadence of John Bull’s stock.
When Englishmen really loved the prize ring, and English-
men had no peers as pugilists, they were whipping the
world on the battlefield and in scores of industries, and
there was no hint anywhere but that the Bull calf was a
virile and magnificent beef. Possibly the Englishman’s
joy in a ring fight was in no way responsible for his per-
fection then-a-days ; but certainly avidity for a scrap is
no element in his present alleged defection, for he is no
longer a fist-fighter, and is now a less hearty patron of
fist-fights than the Parisian.
IN THE AIR !
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Saturday.—M. Henri Farman, on his aero-
plane, at Rheims, flew 112 miles in 3hr. 4min. 56J sec.,
and thereby won the Grand Prix, valued at £2ooo .—Cable.
There’s a sound of something crackling in the air !
There’s a vicious smell of petrol in the air 1
There is something swiftly swooping,
Soaring, hovering and drooping,
And a man is sitting steering in a chair,
And his hair
Stands up straight and gives him something of an air
Of despair—
Looks as if he knew he had no right up there
In the air!
Looks as if he thought the splutter,
And the irritating mutter
Of the motor might stop with a bang! and land him in
the gutter !
And his anxious, fleeting smile,
As he skims another mile,
And the blurred crowd reels below him,
And the people shout that know him,
Seems to indicate a doubt—
Just a shade of apprehension
Whether it will last it out
Or collapse. The state of tension
Is exhausting, and he watches from his swooping, skim-
ming ’plane
As it rattles, flaps and shudders, and sweeps round the
course again
Like a beetle—like a bird,
Like a dragon-fly absurd,
With an endless string of crackers tied adroitly to its
tail,
And exploding like blue blazes on its elevated trail
As it dives and floats and soars
To the mob’s delighted roars ;
As it clatters swiftly round,
Far above the teeming ground,
See ! he peers and steers and watches with anxiety pro-
found !
Till the time has come to stop,
When, with one loud final pop !
And-a flutter and a shudder, to the earth it does a drop ;
Then, like some vast flake of froth,
Or a new and awkward moth,
It squats down upon its wheels,
Hardly knowing how it feels —
Sits there, staring at the sky,
As if it would ask jt why
It was made to carry Farman and an oily smell on high.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

The usual cadging deputations that camped
on the Vic. Treasury doormat during Show week
would cheerfully hang a certain public servant if
it could identify him. He happens to be blessed
with a long memory, and about the time the
beggars were most persistent he remembered
something, and passed it on to the Minister. A
few years ago a certain country shire said it was
utterly ruined by floods; its bridges had been
carried away, its culverts smashed and its roads
reduced to the toilsome, difficult state of the
Narrow Way, and it casually asked for £1000 to
help it along. The then Public Works Minister
was so filled with sympathy that he sent this par-
ticular officer to see whether £1000 was quite
enough. What he found was that the roads were
in a vile condition from neglect, that the bridges
were as bad from pure shiftlessness, and that
the total flood damage could be repaired for £70.
The council got a grant of £35 ; but it was in no
way abashed, and has since then bobbed up
frequently.
“ Lost Ball ” writes :—
The recent death of John Oonway is another reminder
of the fact that notable identities of the cricket field
rarely make very old bones. Conway was one of the
foundation pupils at the Church of England Grammar
School in Melbourne in 1858, and he was still a Grammar
School boy when he was chosen to play against H. H.
Stephenson’s English Eleven in Sydney and Melbourne.
Yet of the Victorian team that met Stephenson’s crowd
on the M.C.C. ground, Conway was the last survivor.
He managed the first Australian Eleven in England, and
practically silenced for ever the question which was then
raised as to the “bona fide amateur”—haw ! —status of
visiting “colonials.” Being challenged to state whether
the Eleven were playing the game as “ gentlemen ” or
professionals, Conway wrote a letter to the Times stating
that their habits were respectable and their favorite
breakfast was chops.

Business, Robbery, etc.
The Sydney Harbor Trust has a lame assort
meat of odd jobs on its hands. It has to dredge up the
dead dog which is mysteriously cast into the port and
the crockery which goes overboard when the cook’s’mate
is too busy to wash-up ; it has to boss the navigation of
the port and provide wharves and jetties; and it has
to turn on the hose when a flurried vessel rushes in
vuth a fire under hatches. Along shore it has 27
other odd chores to do. Since the nhra, jtt
busUed the State Government into resuming some
of the foreshores the Trust has become one of
the big landlords of the city. It probably owns nearly as
many pubs as Tooth's or Toohev’s ; arid at one swoon
Lately it added 22 waterside workers’cottages to its lon£
list Most of these places are on the flat system exceut
that every flat has its own separate entrance. And for
the water or waterside worker who can’t for the moment
run to a flat or even a boarding-house, the Trust keeps a
doss-house at Miller’s Point, and puts up an avera£ of
close on 200 visitors per night. It doesn’t provided
tucker, but the dosser can get a scrub and a mop as well
as a decent bed. And the doss pays the Trust; in fact
dm mg the last two or three years, most of the Trust’s
side-lines have been paying. For a long while it showed
faun a U? ?u' r 0 Slde ev '? ry • year > bu tthe tide turned in
1906, and it has been running strong the other wav e'ver
since so strong that the Trust told Parliament latelv
that for the last 12 months it had £48,835 which it
wanted to drop into a sinking fund.

The taxi-cab has wrecked the horse-cab indus-
try in the Big Smoke. The pirate with the great!, ex
aggerated reputation for repartee is fleeing from the
wrath thatpursueth him; and the smart mechanic with
the taxi-meter is permanently taking his place There
was a big auction sale of cabs, horses and harness ~
London a month or so back, at which hansom-cabs sold
for 255. each In fact, £6 10s. was the top price Soon
the honk of the motor will be over the face of the earth
and the noble animal that makes rogues of the
honest men will be an object of interest in a glass case?

MATILDA ANN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Loaded deep with timber she’s
Coinin’ down the bay ;
Breeze is fair and sea is flat,
Thirty miles to-day.
Queer old fish her skipper is—
Ancient sailor-man—
And his rum old hooker’s named
The Matilda Ann.
She is somewhat elderly ;
Once had better days ;
Sails and spars and runnin’ "-ear’s
Patched in many ways.
Now she’s nosin<>-down the coast,
Doin’ pretty good ;
Thirty miles to do to-day,
Twenty ton of wood.
One old deck-hand is the crew
That she takes to sea ;
Cook and mate and cabin boy
He has got to be.
Skipper puts in over-time,
Pumps Matilda dry ;
For the hooker leaks a bit
When the white-tops fly.
Early morn Matilda Ann,
On a summerday ;
Drifted past the inner light,
Bound for Broken Bay.
But the light wind died awav,
Left her in the sun •
Rollin’ in the easy swell,
Twenty miles to run.
“ What a cool and pleasant change !”
City people say.
“ What a splendid southerly,
After yesterday ! ”
Just a piece of battered plank,
Painted dingy g-reen,
Swirling in the undertow.
Casually was seen.
Letters on the underside,
Read them if you can ;
Battered, old and knocked about,
The Matilda Ann.
N.S.W.

IN
BANANA LAND
The Transcontinental Railway and the
Proposed Great Queensland Grab.
It is hard to understand the mental attitude of those
who favor a transcontinental railway via Oodnadatta,
which would achieve the mighty accomplishment of
nothing.
It has been said that the Commonwealth is a federation,
not of men, but of acres. Well, a threadwork of railways
with a common objective, knitting up the different State
systems as filaments of a great skein of intercommuni-
cation, would conduce to that reciprocal knowledge of
one another which would make of parochial politics an
inipossibility.
To this continental system all existing systems must
form integral adjuncts. The bisection of the continent
is no great matter, and has, besides, the questionable
shape of a huge pastoral scheme for the exploitation of
Central Australia.
The transcontinental railway must be lifted out of the
realm of grab and speculative finance. It is a question,
not of the interests of capital, but of the interests of the
people.
All the capitals and large seaport towns must be linked
together, or good-bye to an effectual system of defence.
A railway from Bourke, via Hungerford, Thargomindah
and Camooweal, to Palmerston, in the Northern Terri-
tory, would be one of the surest steps towards the block-
ing of a Japanese invasion of the peaceful occupation of
tropical Australia.
The above extract is from the Brisbane Worker,
but it might equally well be an extract from a
speech by any one of Queensland’s three Tory
senators, Sayers and Chataway and St. Ledger, or
from the utterances of Premier Kids ton or his
new friend Robert Philp.
* * * *
There is a curious streak of humor about the
arguments on which certain Queenslanders base
their demand for the eastern deviation of the
transcontinental railway. It is propounded, in
the first place, that the Commonwealth should
take over the Northern Territory. On the abso-
lute necessity for doing that everybody is agreed,
for Australia’s unguarded back gate is a great and
constant danger. But, having taken over the
Territory at the expense of all Australia, it is
proposed that the Commonwealth should not at
once proceed to develop this great property to the
utmost for the benefit of all Australia. It is
rather proposed that it should build a railway
which will hurry with the greatest available velo-
city out of the Territory, and leave as much as
possible of that property undeveloped ; and pro-
ceed, at the cost of the Commonwealth, to
develop Queensland and (to some small extent)
N.S.W. According to the map of the proposed
eastern deviation, which has of late been lavishly
circulated throughout Australia, it is suggested
that, when the Commonwealth has taken over the
Territory at the expense of Australia generally,
these lines should be constructed:
660 miles of railway in the Territory
1313 ~ ~ Queensland
4SO ~ ~ New South Wales
And it is not proposed that any of the land values
which wiil be created within the borders of
Queensland and N.S.W. should goto the Common-
wealth, nor that these States should sell to the
Commonwealth any of the State lines connecting
with their ports (as S. A. is to do with its Oodna-
datta-Port Augusta dine), nor that they should
grant space for a Federal terminal port (as S.A.
is to do at Port Augusta.) Consequently, the
startling proposition calls for some inquiry.
* * * *■
The alleged object of the deviation is to “link
together ” the great capitals and seaports. The
straightest possible line (from Port Darwin to
Port Augusta) would, as a beginning, make the
shortest possible connection between the empty
and unprotected North and Adelaide. Adelaide
is, in point of population, the third city in the
Commonwealth (it has about 43,000 more people
than Brisbane). It is the third shipping port in
the Commonwealth, while Brisbane is the fifth.
And it stands for a State which beats Queensland
badly in commerce and runs it closely in manu-
factures. Then again, the shortest and cheapest
line—the one from Port Darwin to Port Augusta,
and thence to Adelaide and Melbourne—would
furnish Melbourne and Geelong with quite as
rapid communication with the Northern Terri-
tory as the proposed long and costly deviation,
and would also give Hobart and Launceston quite
as good a road (and a cheaper road) to the North.
Melbourne is the second city and the second sea-
port of the Commonwealth. Hobart, as a port,
ranks ahead of every Queensland coastal town’
save Brisbane and Townsville ; and Geelong is a
place of some little note. Sydney, the biggest
city and biggest port of Australasia, would” be
just about the same distance from Port Darwin
by either route. Also Albany and Fremantle (the
port of Perth), in Westralia, would be brought
closer to the North by the direct Port Darwin-
Port Augusta line than by the eastern deviation,
and if it is a question of linking together capitals
and seaport towns, it may be mentioned that Fre-
mantle comes immediately after Brisbane as a
port, and Albany comes immediately after Towns-
ville. Also, Providence placed them both nearer
to Port Darwin than Brisbane is, therefore, as a
matter of the defence of the North, their close con-
nection with Port Darwin is important. There is
something inexplicable about this clamor for a
railway from Port Darwin to remote Brisbane
when Fremantle, Adelaide, and Port Augusta are
all nearer, and therefore quite as worthy of
consideration.
*** * ’
According to the proposed eastern deviation of
the transcontinental line, N.S.W. would have to
build either a railway from Bourke to the Queens-
land border or one from Cobar to Broken Hili in
order to connect. If the straight line was con-
structed, N.S.W. would have to connect by
building a railway from Cobar to Broken Hill.
In that respect the position of N.S.W. seems
much the same either wav. On the other side of
the case, N.S.W. would have to pay a larger share
of the cost of the transcontinental railway if the
deviation was made, for the whole cost would be
greater. It Would get a line down through the
western (and the driest and least hopeful) part of
its territory, which line would serve chiefly to
give its western trade a short cut to Melbourne.
And the development of the Territory, in
which it would be the largest shareholder,
would be retarded to N.S.W.’s loss. Victoria
and Tasmania would be brought no nearer
to the Territory by the deviation, yet they
would have to pay more towards their share of a
longer and more costly railway ; the development
of the Territory in which they would be share-
holders would be hindered to their loss; and
instead of having ® part interest in a whole
Federal transcontinental line, and in the port at
each end, they would have an interest only in the
port at one end, and would be shareholders in a
Federal line which began at Port Darwin and
ended far inland on the banks of the Murray
or the Darling. S.A. and Westralia would both
lose by the deviation. And one naturally asks
who would gain ?
* * * *
So the main drawbacks of the eastern deviation
may be summarised thus :
(1) It would be longer and more costly.
(2) It couldn’t be of one gauge throughout unless the
eastern States are prepared to do a vast and general
reconstruction of their lines, for it is to have its sea out-
lets in the south and east over the Queensland 3ft. 6in.
line, the N.S.W. 4ft. SJin. line, and the Victorian sft. 3in.
line. So whatever gauge the Federal line is built upon it
must disagree with two of these State gauges. On the
other hand, along the straight line the Commonwealth
would own the railway all the way, and could make it
any gauge it pleased from sea to sea.
(3) The deviation would leave the southern end of the
Commonwealth railway to end somewhere in the interior
of the country, and it would have no access to the sea in
the south or east, save over the State lines.
(4) The deviation would not only destroy the idea of
having a Federal line all across Australia', hut also the
hope of having a Federal port at each end of that line.
(5) The deviation would bring one State capital
(Brisbane) closer to Port Darwin. It would possibly
bring another (Sydney) a shade closer. It would
bring-two (Melbourne and Hobart) no closer. It would
make two (Adelaide and Perth) much more remote. And
as a mere matter of distance, judged as the crow flies,
Adelaide and Perth are the capitals to which the North
must look first for assistance in case of invasion.
(6) The eastern deviation would involve building the
greater part of the transcontinental line, not through the
Territory for the development of the property of all Aus-
tralia, but through State lands.
(7) There is a sort of desert to be crossed ip any case,
but the deviation would go through more desert and
through quite as dry a desert as the direct line.
Therefore one asks, naturally, who is to profit by
the deviation, and it seems that the only profit—
and that may yet prove a wholly imaginary
profit—would go to Queensland. Queensland
would get many hundreds of miles, of rail-
way built in its State at the cost of the
Commonwealth. The transcontinental railway
would be brought so far east that Towns-
ville would be connected with Port Darwin with-
out Queensland building a single mile of railway
at its pwn cost. Rockhampton would (le con-
nected with Port Darwin by the mere building of
150 miles, and Brisbane by the mere building of
170 miles on to the existing State systerri In
other words, the back country of Queensland
would be opened up with a vast amount of line
built by Australia generally in State
Townsville, Rockhampton and Brisbane would fie
connected with each other, and they would ajl
WATER VIEW 100 MLEES WEST OF ALICE SPRINGS , CENTRAL AUSTRALIA,
NEAR THE PROPOSED PORT AUGUSTA-PORT DARWIN RAILWAY.
three be connected also with Sydney and Mel-
bourne, all at Australia’s expense. These Queens-
land ports would get a not much longer road
to the Northern Territory if the straight,
short, cheap transcontinental railway was built;
but then, instead of the Australian-owned
line diverging east at Australia’s expense
tOiConnect with the Queensland system, Queens-
land would require to build its lines westward at
its own expense to connect with the Australian
railway system. In this matter a little outside
State like Tasmania is apt to remark that it
dpesn’t see why it should contribute towards the
building of so many miles of line in Queensland,
seeing that it isn’t part-owner of Queensland ; and
it is inclined to add that Queensland can afford to
build its own lines.
* * * -*
The conspiracy to leave South Australia out in
the cold in this matter of the transcontinental
railway looks rather ungrateful. When neither
N.S.W. nor Queensland would have the Territory
at any price, S. A. recklessly shouldered the bur-
den, and probably saved the North from becoming
a nigger-infested appanage of India. The British
Government stipulated for the building of the
railway from Adelaide to Port Darwin as
one of the conditions on which it would perma-
nently transfer the Territory to Australia,
and but for the valiant (and foolish) effort which
S. A. made to fulfil that condition, the Terri-
tory would most likely have been resumed by
Britain long ago. Then in all human probability
there would have been no Commonwealth, no
White Australia, no Labor party (for with the
cheap nigger in the land there isn’t much room
for a Labor party), and no Brisbane Worker.
South Australia began to realise the White Aus-
tralia gospel fairly early; even as far back as 20
years ago it was keeping its own country and the
Territory fairly clean as compared with the con-
dition of Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria and
Maoriland. It never deserved the name of
Kanakaland as Queensland did. It led the way
in Factory legislation, and it is the only State
which has put a strong Labor Government into
power and kept it there for over four years. S. A.
deserves better luck than to find a Kanakaland
Labor paper going into camp against it along
with Sayers and Chataway and St. Ledger and
Philp and Kidston. It deserves better luck than
to have its unfinished transcontinental line left on
i fco ' hands as a bad debt, seeing that that line
represented a great effort to keep faith with the
British Government, and it was almost certainly
the attempt to keep faith which prevented the
Territory becoming a nigger-cursed Crown colony.
And as for the suggestion of the Brisbane Labor
paper that the only way to prevent the trans-
continental railway being “a huge pastoral scheme
for the exploitation of Central Australia ” is to
divert the line out of the State which produced
Price, McPherson, Kingston and many other
good democrats, and which also produced the first
graduated land-tax and the first .successful Labor
Ministry, and to bring it at grdst extra cost into
the State which produced Glassey, Dawson,
Philp, Drake, Kidston and the Kanaka policy,
it seems rather a low-down business. In fact it
looks an indecent rushing, from purely provincial
motives, into the camp of Sayers and Chataway
and St. Ledger, and people of that sort.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 4, 1909.
Mr Dear Thargominoah,—
Our Commander Richardson has been made a
captain and a commandant. It is rather a
distinction without a difference, as he seems
to have been at the same job for some time now.
But I suppose its nicer to have the official stamp
upon you. This sort of thing is like society : “To
be in it is merely a bore, but to be out of it is a
tragedy.” The powers that be might have given
the cheerful Curtis his other half stripe. Can it
be that these dark remarks about onions—but no !
Such a thing is impossible.
With the first break of spring the southern
flittings commence. The Cecil Palmers are off in
a week or so. and other flitters are the Knox
family and several more.
The jubilee effort of Queensland could not be
called an exhaustive one, but a few persons took
themselves seriously and got a move on. The
local Art Society, for instance, made a surprising
break, and its annual show is really a credit, not
the least interesting items being the caricatures of
Souter, “Hop” and Norman Lindsay. Franky
Payne shows some nice figure work and a little
glimpse of Paris ; but the feature of the local work
lies in the many exhibits of Miss Isobel Townley,
just back from the Straits Settlement. Her output
includes fakirs, Chinese josses, temple gates and
some really dainty,bits of heathenesse in general, all
done with a light and airy touch like unto that of
a fairy walking on eggs. After many years’ absence
Datillo Rubbo is again represented, while Lillian
Chauvel. Bessie Gibson and a host of other old
Brisbane girls assist.
The O’Reilly clan has been feeding the multi-
tude this week in honor of the coming brides,
Misses Callan and Macdonald. Miss Beryl Mant
is another of the tea-givers. Other diversions
since I wrote last have included a pleasant little
shed dance at the stronghold of the Commercials,
and a hop at the Willcocks mansion, whereat the
hours were chased on flying feet until the clock
struck (for higher wages), and the night failed (it
paid nothing in the £) and the day broke (its
neck, I expect).
Trust the dearly beloved brethren for getting
someone else to do the major portion of the toil.
Their d evices are as ingenious as those of
the heathen in his blindness. Recently the local
fire escapes wanted 10,000 circulars addressed.
Did they give the job to some hard-up little
typist ? Not they ! They selected some half-
dozen of their most attractive curates; and the
curates rounded up about 30 females, who, on
promise of having a curate each to play with, were
beguiled into doing the pen-scratching. A society
par in a local daily gravely chronicled the fact
that at Mrs. Blank Blankton’s tea the last of the
envelopes were superscribed.
The Staughton dame and her chick, who have-
been putting in August in Brisbane, have de-
parted to their native fields. The chick aforesaid
was a pleasure to look at, with her bright cheeks,
and well-cut wrappings. Also she had an air of
well-being, as if the world bloomed in bright tints
just for the asking.
Another monster bazaar threatens. This time
it is the Sisters of All Hallows that are the mov-
ing spirits, and the proceeds are to be for the com-
pletion of the new Mater Misericordiae Hospital.
The said building already constitutes a landmark:
upon the south side of Stephen’s Hill. The pro-
ject has the whole-hearted support of G. W. Gray..
E. B. Forrest, Peter Murphy, and such good,
workers in charity’s cause.
Those whose business it was to lighter the-
stranded Waipara had a fine exhibition, last,
week, of the shark power of Moreton Bay. Much
of the frozen mutton became so tired that it was.
a case of either throwing it overboard or the sal-
vage crew going on strike. It went over, and it is-
solemnly declared that the bay was paved with
sharks when the first carcase was half-way between,
the bulwarks and the water.
The German gunboat Condor that didn’t find!
the long-lost Seestern is back again, and the town,
is full of Kaiser Wilhelm’s cheerful, bearded
middies.
There was a very angry lady at a certain city tea-
house, the other day. A tea-fight was in full
bloom. The waitress had heen handing round- the
strawberries, and thus there was a pause in the
conversation. “Pardon me, madam,” said a
junior assistant, confidentially yet audibly, “but
your husband is waiting on the telephone.” The
lady looked slightly flustered, and fled to the wire.
On her return, she murmured to her hostess some-
thing about “dear George” having a letter tor
her, and that she must really be going, hare-
wells were said amid a general smile, the meaning
of which the lady was unable to unravel until she
caught sight of the husband aforesaid at a table
near the door, and in full sight of the party she
had just quitted. Who was it on the wire?
Parliament is prorogued and the dogs of war
are loose. Now comes that fine opportunity for
mutual plain speaking that is with us only once
in a while, when the enlightened voter is master
of the situation and can vent his or her feelings
without fear that lasting offence will arise through
his utterances. To bail-up your member in the
street on an ordinary summer day and tell him he
is a miserable ass would be actionable ; but just
now your voice is lost amid so many, all telling him
the same palpable truth, that nothing seriouscomes
of it. Already there is the usual rush for the spoil.
The Toowong-shunted Macartney is to be the
second horse in the Tory team for Brisbane North,
while up against this representation of Stoutness
will be, if all reports are true, Mayor Wilson, as
a straight-out Labor man. The second Laborite
has so far not been announced.
Old man Hanran. who for the past 10 years has
helped Pliilp to represent Townsville, threw up
things in general last week and retired in favor of
Fenwick. He is a kindly old soul, and few
in the House wasted less of the country’s time in
conversation. The party rallied round and told
him some nice things about himself, and cor
roborated them with a purse of sovereigns.
Hanran was much moved, and said exactly what
he has said in the House for 10 years—which is
nothing.
Sincerely yours,
MABIO.
One more trial shipment of 1500 quarters
of chilled Bananaland beef is being sent to John
Bull by the Marathon. Previous shipments from
Australia were complete failures, and two lots
from Maoriland were not much better. The
cattle will be killed four days before the vessel
leaves, and the voyage will take 60 days. If the
experiment is successful, it will mean a big thing
for the Commonwealth, which hitherto has had
to freeze its cow before exporting her; and freezing
is a much more costly business than chilling,
though it’s all the same to the cow.
In the Jap Quarter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
LOTUS land, lanterns alight
Spread in a gay colored row.
The “Who is it?” hour of night,
Azaleas that wave to and fro,
The flutter of fans, the glow
Of fabulous things, a scene
Smokers of opium know—
This is the soul of a screen.
The geisha, butterfly bright
With obi and wonderful bow,
Lorn for, the honorable knight,
Waits in the tea-house below
The bamboo bridge. ... He will go,
Two-sworded, reckless of mien,
To fight for his love, friend or foe.
This is the soul of a screen.
Passions that mortals excite
Are but as blossoms that blow
Dreams of desire and delight.
This was a love long ago. . .
Time is illusion. Andlo!
Softly the hours slip between,
Lulled by the tropic sea’s flow—
This is the soul of a screen.
We are but dream shapes, I trow,
Wafted through Fancy’s demesne.
Fleeting as Fuji’s soft snow —
This is the soul of a screen.
QUEENSLAND.
LOUIS ESSON.


THE INDUSTRIAL TWINS—COAL AND IRON.
“ Queensland coal-bearing strata are of vast extent
and wide distribution.” — Mr. Knibbs, Com-
monwealth Statistician.
In Ipswich and Burrum districts there is a coal-
bearing and coal-yielding territory larger than
either Servia, Greenland, Belgium, Denmark
or Switzerland, and as large as Greece.
With all these enormous deposits, Queensland only
produces about three-quarters of a million tons
of coal annually.
England, with only 9000 sq. miles, of coal-fields,
produces annually
251 MILLIONS OF METRIC TONS.
In one area of five square miles, the Government
Geologist estimates that there are■ 50 MIL-
LIONS OF TONS OF COAL in sight in
Queensland.
In one mound of 200 ft. high, near Cloncurry, with
a diameter of a mile, there are 10\ MILLION
TONS of pure iron ore.
Below the roots of this mountain are iron veins.
In America, kingly revenues arc poured into the
laps of individuals like Carnegie by the iron
and steel industries.
The value of the iron product in the U.S.A. was, in
1905, TWO HUNDRED MILLIONS sterling.
Queensland To-day: the Queen State
of the Commonwealth.
No. XXXVI.
FORTUNES IN COAL AND IRON.
By J. D. FITZGERALD. Barrister-at-Law.
“ Br-oop and iron ” were the ingredients employed
by the great Bismarck in making the German
Empire. “Coal and iron” are necessary in-
gredients in maintaining a nation which is
marked out for a great industrial future. How
stands Queensland in regard to these foundational
products? Says Mr. Knibbs, the Commonwealth
statist:—
In Queensland the coal-bearing strata are of vast ex-
tent and wide distribution, being noted under the greater
portion of the south-eastern districts, within 200 miles of
the sea, as far nortli as Cooktown, and under portions of
the far western interior.
The Ipswich and Burrum districts have 24,000
square miles of coal-bearing country, a territory
larger than Servia, Greenland, Belgium, Den-
mark, Switzerland, and as large as Greece.
There are also huge deposits of iron, which is
used in the reduction processes in connection with
gold and copper production. The Giant State
thus possesses the keel of a great industrial
future, firmly laid. One of these days she will
become the largest field in the world for the in-
vestor in industrial enterprises. The problems of
industrial advancement have not become acute
yet. We are still mainly primary producers ; but
we can imagine what the future will bring forth
in the exploitation of the hidden, or half-hidden,
wealth which lies beneath the surface of the soil,
ready to yield its spoils to the capitalist, and to
SAPPHIRE TOWN—WHERE THE LOVELY GEMS COME FROM.
Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer.
VICTORIA BRIDGE, OVER THE BRISBANE RIVER (AT THAT POINT
WIDER THAN THE THAMES).
give a market to the thews and sinews of the
worker. The principal coalfields at present under
exploitation are in the Ipswich district, near to a
fertile and beautiful agricultural, pastoral and
sugar country, and with easy access to the wharves
and shipping of Brisbane by land carriage.
The development of coal mining has been slow,
but a steadily-increasing output is coming as year
follows year/ That may be seen from the figures,
The production of coal in 1861 was 14,212 tons, in
1871, 17,000 tons. This small output grew to
65 6 i2 tons in 1881, to 271,603 in 1891; to 539.472
tons in 1901, 606,772 in 1906, and 683,272 in 1907.
The coal-mining industry is thus seen to he a
baby, with illimitable possibilities of growth in
the future. In this, as in other matters, the com-
niuna j services of democracy lend a hand. The
Government of the State, representing a busi-
ness democracy, allows concessions to ships which
coal at Queensland ports. The output will grow
PICKING SAPPHIRES IN THE GEM COUNTRY IN QUEENSLAND.
Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer.
WASHING SAPPHIRES.
Photo, by C. E. S. Fryer.
by leaps and bounds. When the industry gets
fairly going, the proximity of Queensland ports to
the great hives of the East will be found of the
greatest- advantage. Sea-carriage of coal from
Queensland ports to ports like Hongkong,
Manila, Tandjong Priok (Java), and Taudjong
Pagar (Singapore) must have an advantage in
cheapness over coal borne over longer distances.
And the quality of the Queensland coal is first-
class, especially the fine anthracite deposits of the
Central District.
In the Bundanba district, near Brisbane,
where the chief coal mining operations of the
State are carried on, a very high standard of
development exists. The most up-to-date
CARYOTA URENS PALMS, KAMERUNGA STATE NURSERY, CAIRNS, N!
Photo. by Mobsby.
machinery is in use, including electrical coal-
cutting machines. There are 1000 miners em-
ployed ; and these are but a regiment of the
army which will some day be employed in cutting
the coal from these beds. The quality of the coal
and its suitableness to the varied uses of the pro-
duct have now been well ascertained. The Wide
Bay and Maryborough coal measures are next to
Bundanba in point of development, producing
over 80,000 tons annually. The Central District
of Queensland produces anthracitic coal, which
has been subjected to severe tests by the British
Admiralty authorities, who report strongly in its
favor, and by the Mount Morgan Company, which
declares that, burned with forced draught, it is an
excellent coal. But we have the abnormal
element again in this, as in gold, in this
wonderful State. The Queensland Government
Geologist, after investigating the deposits of coal
at Callide Creek, not far from the Dawson River
District, estimates that in an area of five square
miles there is a wonderful deposit of fifty million
tons of coal. These only await the co-operation of
capital and labor and commercial methods to get
a share of tile markets of the world—or, at any
rate, of that quarter of the globe which looks out
on the seas flowing southwards to the break
against the Barrier Reef.
The capitalists of all countries generally com-
bine a certain amount of imagination with those
coarser, but very necessary, qualities of common-
sense and business acumen. This combination of
qualities has given the woi'ld a wonderful impulse
in wealth production, When we hear of the re-
strictions which are placed upon the development
of the native wealth of countries like New Cale-
donia and Siberia, owing to a fatuous political
system, and contrast the obstacles imposed upon
those new countries with the facilities and the
assistance offered to the investor by an intelligent
democratic Government, the wonder is that the
great resources of Queensland have been allowed
so long to remain in the bowels of the earth.
With the least imaginative faculty enabling them
to
Pip into the future, far as human eye can see,
See the vision of the world, and all the wonder that
will he,
they will speedily prepare a future for the great
Queensland mineral industries, which will keep a
prosperous people employed in raising yaw weal th
from the ground. And, when the coal and tiie
iron and the juxtaposed limestone (providential
geological combination) are won and “gotten”
from the ground, there will be a fresh circle of
home industries created, in the processes which
follow the bringing together of the raw ingredients
of industrial riches.
Again, in relation to iron, we have to face the
abnormal. Iron Island is, it is said by high
authority, capable of yielding 2% million tons of
hematite (iron ore) ; Mount Leviathan, at -Clon-
curry, from the base upwards to 200 ft. at the
summit, shows a deposit estimated at million
tons of pure hematite ore, and like Mount
Morgan, when the summit is levelled, that is only
the removal of the cap of the deposit, and the
roots of the mountain are great ore beds,
Chromite and magnetite exist in varying quanti-
ties in every district of the State. Immense
deposits of limestone occur in all the metalliferous
zones. It needs no special powers of imagination
to conjure up the wonderful industrial develop-
ment of the future. We may measure the possi-
bilities by the work done in Mount Morgan,
The superhuman energy applied in the wonder
mine by the hands of men, by the throbbing
machinery, and by the lubrication of the
works by the oil of stored-up capital, is
not exhausted in the search for gold and copper,
Gold is the more easily realisable product, doubt-
less. But larger fortunes have been made in the
iron and steel trades. Next to the oil men in the
United States, the ironmasters are the richest
citizens. Andrew Carnegie gives away universi-
ties and libraries, and builds shrines to the Inter-
national Peace idea, and all his wealth has come
to him from the huge returns won by the exploita-
tion of coal and iron.
These huge fortunes, these more than lungly
revenues, which have been poured into the laps of
individuals like Carnegie, are the fruits of the new
industrial age —the age of coal and iron and steel,
and machinery—the age of development and ex-
pansion of bridge-building and railway construc-
tion and leviathan steamships. If Queensland can
boast of her tens of thousands of square miles of
country with ascertained coal measures, the indus-
trial prophet can, with easy vaticination, point
out her marvellous future, remembering that
England produces annually, from 9000 square
miles of coal fields, no less than 251 millions metric
tons (of 22051 b); France, with 1800 miles of coal-
bearing country, 34 million tons ; and Germany,
with 3000 square miles, million tons.
In the same line of exploitation we see. the
tremendous strides made in iron and steel produc-
tion in the United [States. The capital in the
U.S.A. invested in this industry rose in five years,
between 1900 and 1905, from £110,000,000 to
£189,"00,000, while the. value of the product in the
last year named was approximately £200,000,000
sterling. It is this tremendous development of
natural wealth which has made the United States
the industrial portent which she is to-day—a
country where the individuals who have been
rewarded iri the industrial uplift with wealth be-
yond the dreams of avarice are now beginning to
learn how to use it wisely. Thus we have splen-
did endowments of American cities with parks
and noble institutions, universities, science research
colleges, slum destruction and scientific rebuild-
ng, and garden cities.
Queensland’s day is to come. There may be
poor lads to-day in Scotland who might become
the O-irnegies of the industrial future of Queens-
land if they immigrated. The era of the develop-
ment of these great resources is coming with a
sure crescendo. Before 20 years have gone over
our heads we will see the smoking chimneys and
hear the ringing hammers of a great industrial
hive in Queensland. We shall have our steel
works, rolling mills and Bessemer plants. In
beginning our iron industry in Queensland we
have not to feel our way blindly, as the Americans
had. We are the heirs of the ages in scientific
knowledge, and we shall start at the stage which
the United States, the United Kingdom, Belgium,
Germany and France have reached. Moreover,
to the foreign investor there is the encourage-
ment which he may expect to receive from a sym-
pathetic Government, whose services ajrid whose
officers are at his beck and call. It is the greatest
pity in the world that the concessionaries who
suffer discouragement from the crass governmental
policy of the French in New Caledonia do not
tear up their papers and cross to the great island
continent. If they did, they would find a wider
scope, richer fields, abundance of raw material, a
race of white workers who have performed with
their picks and shovels miracles like unto those of
the genii of the Arabian tales, and a settled and
intelligent system of government, which will give
them every possible assistance in the profitable
employment of their capital or their labor. Ihe
world has yet to learn the rich rewards that await
the bold investor in Queensland coal and iron
ventures.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages •which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures llie sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

TO KEEP YOU SICK!
THE first impulse of persons who find that they are ailing in any way is to
plunge into a chemist’s and purchase a box of pills or a bottle of some patent
medicine. . . Failing to get more than temporary relief from this they then
consult a doctor, and he writes a prescription which more than likely calls for the
same medicine, only in a different form from what the patient has already taken.
The same result, generally. Next, the patient comes to the conclusion that this
doctor didn’t diagnose the case right, and then goes to another, with the same
result. And so on.
Our modern medical system is a foolish system. We practically pay
our doctors to keep us ill. Their prosperity depends not on our health,
BUT ON OUR SICKNESS! . . . To-day very little is known of medicine
and its use. The wisest doctor can do tout little more than clear out
the system or accelerate the action of the heart. When he has done these
thing’s he has reached the end. MEDICINE CANNOT MAKE STRENGTH.
Now, just study yourself for a moment. The failure or breaking down of any
organic function means that it has not strength to do its work. If there was any
nourishment in medicine it might bring about a healthy change, but you know there
is not. Drugs, medicines, stimulants, narcotics and antidotes are POISONS, not
food. Food is needed to build up new energy. Electricity is nerve food, nerve life.
No pain or debility can exist in a body charged with Electric Life; you can have
no Weakness, no Inactive Parts, no Rheumatism, Indi-
gestion, Constipation' or Kidney Trouble, if you fill your
body with it, because the life generated by this grand force gives health and
strength to every organ, and
dr. McLaughlin s electric belt
is the newest and most successful method of applying it. It is a series of small batteries which you wear about your waist.
It worßs wßile you sleep, and generates its power constantly. It is worn as comfortably as your clothing,
adjusted in a minute, and made as strong or mild as you like by the pressure of a finger upon the regulator. With it
you can carry the current direct to the spot affected. You can get the full current at your ankles, feet, arms, shoulders,
head or any other part of your body. It has a Special Attachment for men. We have fixed a price for it which puts it
within the reach of everyone. Its price is as low as £1 in many cases.

The Victorian Government Statist has
issued another part or two of his Year Book ; and
one of them deals with the municipalities.
Among other things there is a statement of the
population of the shires into which the country
districts are cut up ; and this is how the first three
entries compare with the first three in the dead-
and-gone Hayter’s Year Book for 1892 :
Here and there the Victorian country population
has been added to during these last 16 years ; but
mostly the record reads like the first three entries
in the list. For 16 years Victoria has been crying
for a progressive land-tax ; for 16 years the State’s
own Year Book figures have been yelling louder
and louder for the same thing; but instead of a
progressive land-tax and population the country
gets T. Bent and John Weary. And they’re
mighty poor substitutes.

shareholders as follows :
We have examined the above balance-sheet with the books at head office, and have had access to the branch
accounts transmitted to the head office from the colonies. We have verified the correctness of the cash, money at
call and short notice, investments, and the bills of exchange held in London, and having obtained all the information
and explanations we have required, we are of opinion that such balance-sheet is properly drawn up so as to exhibit a
true and correct view of the state of the company’s affairs according to the best of our information and the explana-
tions given to us, and as shown by the books of the company and the said branch accounts.

A wandering Cornstalk who is earning his
rouble in Eastern Siberia sends a letter with
two octagonal stamps on it, and the Russian
part of the address —the part which carried
it as far as the place where the Australian
language begins—looks like “ Tepeze Mockby,
Abcmpaillia ”:—
Re “Trotter’s” remarks on fast expresses in Bulletin
of 15th April (that’s a long time ago, but The Bulletin
has just come in, so it is not my fault). I’m writing this
with my legs stretched along latitude 58° N. and my back
against the post that holds up the line of longitudewhich
runs through Alban’- (W.A.). Except for a few fleecy
clouds in the blue >_.f the skies I might be in Western
(Queensland on a typical winter’s day, for Siberia, con-
trary to popular opinion, is not always under snow.’
It’s about “ Trotter ” and his inclusion of the Moscow-
vladivostok express amongst the fastest trains of the
world. That “ express” gets along at 20 miles an hour,
avenge speed throughout the journey; and that is the
Intei tational Wagon-Lit Co. of Europe’s express, too. It
couldn't go any faster on the present track, anyhow. As
to comfort, it is fairly good, having a library (carrying at
least a dozen ancient English novels), piano, corridor
cars, usual dining saloon, electric light and bathroom—
the charge for the last named is 3s. a dip. I know
that bathroom, for one day I emerged from it to find
myself shunted off to a side track in mid-Siberia for
repairs to the car.
The _ cars have double windows and double door-
ways in winter, as protection against the cold of the
bleak Siberian plains, which I remember at Omsk
station sent the thermometer down to 25 degrees below
zero 1 That was at 6 p.m. on a January day. Blit these
trains don’t have the boudoir car of the Adelaide-Mel-
bourne and (I believe you have them also since my last
trip that way) the Melbourne-Sydney expresses," and
altogether are not nearly so comfortable and steady as
your two boss trains. The Moscow-Vladivostok trip takes
12 days, with one change at Irkutsk, seven days out from
Moscow ; and the Australian language goes on the first
section, but not, as a rule, .951,. the last. On that first
section I once foymd a waiter who could wait in six
languages!
Then “ Trotter," there is no through Paris-Berlin-St.
Petersburg-Moscow train, for coming eastward through
Germany one must change first at Wirballen, on the fron-
tier, where the gauge changes, and where they turn travel-
lers out into a bleak waiting-hall at midnight’and overhaul
baggage and passports. Also at St. Petersburg and
Moscow you arrive at one side of the city and continue
from the other side, driven across in a drosky by a
bearded and yelling moujik who knows no rule of the
road : for, unlike Paris and Berlin, the Russian cities have
not even a; devious suburban line to bring one round to
the other side of the city when passing through. But
then no one should go through these cities without a
stay. St. Petersburg, with its fine, wide, straight streets
and splendid buildings, is verily the Queen City of the
Aorth. Moscow’s streets are twisty, like those of
London and Sydney; but its magnificent old churches
and palaces are fascinatingly attractive.
That’s about all I want to say to “Trotter,” who, I !
trust, has not died in the long interval. I get The j
Bulletin posted from your end each week, just to see \
how Wade, Deakin and Co. are getting on, and receive I
them nearly all, mostly in batches, any time from two to I
three months after posting. I nearly summoned upenough 1
energy some time ago to put a contributor of yours right
about the fees paid at Tomsk Universitv, but have for-
gotten now what it was he said.
