Journey Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Lund liner Commonwealth arrived at Durban and
reported having- seen no trace of the missing steamer
Waratah.— Cable.
HEAR the whispers of the Steam !
Hear thepent and toiling Power !
Where the furnace throws its gleam,
Hark ! the secrets of the Steam,
Whilst the watchful servants cower,
And the clanging notes of shovels on the hot and
throbbing floor
Seem to ring a knell of labor ’midst the engines’
endless roar.
Driving on—driving on
Where a thousand ships have gone.
Lo ! the hidden giant singing
To the harsh-toned shovels’ ringing
Broods the conquered.miles upon,
And the burthen of its song—
Of its rolling thunder-song—
It is this: “My puny masters, I will carry you
■ along
To your small and vain desires,
So you tend my altar fires
Till by the quay you face the waiting throng ;
But though vast my strength, the Sea
Hath the Will of God behind it, and beneath
Eternity ! ”
Toiling on—toiling fast
While the deep-sea peace doth last,
Asking never ease or leisure,
Beating out an ocean measure
With a rhythmic grace and vast;
Thus the snow-white spirit Steam
Drives its sleek and gleaming team
And it softly sighs and whispers, making up its
journey dream
As its good slaves to one end
All their strength and swiftness blend
Till from their hidden work their voices seem
To proclaim tumultuously,
“Time and Distance do we conquer, but we
conquer not the Sea ! ”
Toiling on—toiling far
’Neath the; haggard morning star
''All the pilgrim voices blending
lii a hymn of toil ascending
Chant with never jolt nor jar,
And the great shaft’s solemn note
From its cavern dim, remote,
'Mid the lighter, higher voices like a warning
seems to float;
“ Heavy—heavy is my tell 1
O, my masters, give me oil
That I may moisten well my parched throat!
And remember, all of ye,
That we journev on for ever at the mercy of the
Sea ! ”
Hear the whispers of the Steam !
Hear the pent and toiling Power !
Where the furnace throws its gleam,
Hark ! the secrets of the Steam !
How the watchful servants cower !
And the clanging notes of shovels on the hot and
throbbing floor
Seem to ring a knell of labor’midst the engines’
endless roar.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.
Sam’s Seance.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'* T" OOK ’ere, Jerry,” said Mr. Sharp,
I i “ the Dook —a fellar I met larst night—
is raisin’ Cain over losin’ an ole copper
ticker. Torks o’ goin’ to the p’leece about it—
fancy that! The ’Ops, matey ! When I arsted ’im
if ’e wasn’t quite positive ’e ’adn’t on’y lent it to
’is dear uncle, you’d ha’ thought I’d er pinched it
orf of ’ini by the ugly look ’e give me.”
“Well, an’so you did, I s’pose, Sam,” rejoined
the matter-of-fact Mr. Maunder.
“Them’s ’arsh words wot one day you’ll be
sorry fur utterin’ of, Jerry ; you’d give ole worlds
to recall ’em back agin arter you ain’t got me with
yer no longer,” prophesied the sentimental Mr.
Sharp. “ But wot I wanted to say wos this ’ere —
I’ve promised the Dook to find out fur ’im ’oo it
wos prigged that luverly watch an’ chain of ’is, an’
you’ve gotter ’elp me, mate. It’s necessary ter
divert suspicion. We don’t want no John ’Ops.”
Mr. Maunder shook his head decisively.
“Not me, Sam,” he said, with firmness. “ You
kin do yer own dirty work. I’ve yanked you
outer a ’ole afore this pretty frequent, an’ small
thanks you ever give me fur doin’ of it.”
“ You wouldn’t tork that way if you on’y noo
’ow it pained me to ’ear you, Jerry,” retorted the
grieved Mr. Sharp. “It ain’t much I’m arstin’ of
you. I sor in the noospaper orl about a bloke—l
furgit wot they called ’im—wot went into trances
an’ them things. Well, there’d bin a ’orrible
murder, an’ ’e says as ’ow ’e’d put ’isself inner
trance an’ find out that way ’oo it wos done it. I
don’t rightly remember if it come to anytbink, but
readin’ give me a idea, an’ I’ve told the Dook an’
’is cobbers if they chooses to drop inter my dig-
gins at the ’Orns to r night ’bout nine, my pal, the
eminent meejutn, Mr. Jerry Maunder, ’ll trace
the watch quicker ’n orl the John ’Ops in
Melbourne.”
Mr. Maunder, grinned reluctantly.
“ ’Ow you goin’ to work it, Sam?”
Mr. Sharp coughed portentously.
“You shall’ave a drink fur bein’a good boy,
Jerry,” he remarked, evasively. “Comeon acrost
to the ’Orns. I must make it orright with Ginger
Jones for me to borrer the loan o’ that excluded
room of ’is.”
* * * *
At nine o’clock that evening Messrs. Sharp and
Maunder received their visitors in the small back
chamber wherein Publican Jones and his associ-
ates devoted much of their spare time to two-up
and other games of chance.
Mr. William Smithers, familiarly known as
“ The Dook,’’was one of the earliest to present
himself, and from his lowering countenance his
companions took their ciie. Indeed it needed all
gain’s magnetism to even slightly thaw the thick
coating of ice which bound his auditory together
in chilly comradeship. He concluded his few
words of welcome with a fervid peroration calcu-
lated to disarm the most hostile of critics.
“I don’t arst none o’you to listen to me,” he
said, with great earnestness. “Watch my ’ands
—that’s orl you gotter do; an’ then when ole
Jerry ’ere opens ’is lips, ’ang onter ’is words an’
don’t lose one of ’em. ’E’s wot we ’oo ’ave the
gift o’ ’ippytism calls a meejum—the’ole sperrit
push toilers ’im about constant, same as little
Mary’s lamb. I on’y leads ’im on be questions,
an’ everythink ’e says is Gawspel troo. Now are
you orl ready ? ”
“Git on, Sam,” counselled Mr. Jones, from the
doorway. “ The’ole boilin’ o’ you’ll ’ave to be
out o’ this in ’arf a’ hour’s time, ’cos I’m expectin’
a few pals to drop in promiscuss-like fur a prayer-
meetin’.”
The suspicious Mr. Smithers sniffed noisily, and
honored Sam with a basilisk stare. Sam doffed
his coat, and advancing towards the chair in
which his venerable colleague was seated, executed
several passes of a rapid but complicated descrip-
tion under his very nose.
“Time!” bellowed the ribald Mr. Jones, and
somebody sniggered.
Mr. Smithers snorted. Sam smiled cheerfully
as he readjusted his shirt-sleeves. Mr. Maunder’s
eyelids drooped languidly and closed.
“’E’s unconscientious,” explained Mr. Sharp,
thoughtfully. “ Are you asleep, Jerry?”
“ Yes,” replied the unhappy medium, taken by
surprise.
“Meantersay no, not quite.” He opened one
inflamed eye.
“’Ushaby baby, ’e ’s teethin’—give ’im a drop
o’gin to soothe’im,” recommended the sceptical
Ginger Jones, playing shamelessly to the gallery.
Sam waved his hands persuasively, and the
theatrical Mr. Maunder snored.
“’E’s orf,” whispered Sam confidently. “Now
I’ll arst ’rn any questions you like, Bill.” He
turned to the baleful Mr. Smithers.
“Pinch ’im fust, to see if’e ain’t shammin’,” im-
plored the irrepressible Mr. Jones.
An expectant auditory requested the interrupter
to put his head in a bag without delay. The
Dook cursed at random.
“ Arst ’im furst ’oo sneaked my eighteen-carat
gold repeatin’ ticker, an’ wot ’e done with it ? ” he
demanded, hoarsely. Sam turned to the slum-
bering Jerry.
“There’s a gen’leman ’ere ’as lost ’is watch.
Kin you kindly tell if it were stole —if so, by ’oo ? ”
he inquired, with dramatic emphasis. No answer.
“’E’s far away in the sperrit world, surrounded
by all the asterisk influences,” explained the
psychical Mr. Sharp. “’E’s,in wot we call the
seventh 'Eaven ; I must summons ’im down to a
lower plane.” He gesticulated with abandon.
Mr. Maunder grunted. “ Are you there, Jerry ? ”
Sam’s tone was insinuating.
“No, ’e’s bushed—send a black tracker arter
’im,” suggested Ginger Jones.
“ Stow it! Go an’ boil yer fat ’ead ! ” besought
the general assembly with warmth.
“Yes, I’m ’ere, Samuel.” The voice of Mr.
Maunder sounded faint and far away.
Mr. Sharp repeated his query relating to the
Dook’s timepiece.
“I kin see a tall man with a ugly mug, a big
’ook nose, flap ears an’ a squint,” began the
sleeping medium.
Sam nodded triumphantly at the incensed Mr.
Smithers.
“Yes, that’s right enuff, Jerry. Well, wot
about ’im ?” he said.
“’E’s torkin’ to a female,” pursued Mr.
Maunder, dreamily.
“Is it ’is wife, Jerry?” asked Mr. Sharp,
acutely.
“ She’s gotter back tome,” continued the pains-
taking Mr. Maunder. “I carn’t see ’er face.
Now, she’s turnin’ round. No, it ain’t ’is missus—
wife, meantersay—but a fat, ’orrid-lookin’, red-
’aired, freckled gurl inner green plinth dress with
a yeller rose stuck inter it.”
“’Oo is it, Jerry?” inquired Mr. Sharp.
Eor a moment there was a dead silence, broken
only by the troubled respiration of the unfaithful
Mr. Smithers. Then Mr. Maunder resumed —
“ It’s the noo barmaid at the ‘ Hanker in ’Ope.’
’E’s arstin’ ’er if she loves ’im ” (the Dook started
violently), “an’ she says she kep’ a pet monkey
once, on’y the pore thin’ took to consumption an’
died. ’E puts ’is ’and inter ’is pocket an’ pulls
out ’is watch an’ chain. ’E shows ’er the works —
jew’ls in four ’oles an’ all marked 18-claret. She
shakes’er’ead. ’E takes’er’and. She smiles up
at ’im. ’E give ’er the watch an’ chain. She
give ’im the rose out of ’er bosom. Now their lips
meet —ah ! I kin see no more. Wait—yes—wdt’s
this ’ere? A small little woman with false teeth
wot ain’t bin paid fur—it’s ’is wife. She arsts
’im the time o’ day. ’E says ’is watch ’as bin
stole from’im in Collins-street; she fetches ’im a
clip acrost the ear-’ole”
“It’s a lie! It’s a damned lie!” roared the
exasperated Mr. Smithers, trying to assert him-
self above a storm of laughter.
“That’ll do, Jerry. You kin wake up now,”
said Mr. Sharp, shaking his aged comrade by the
shoulder.
At that moment the door was flung violently
back, and a stout, breathless gentleman with pro-
nounced Semitic features darted into the room.
“ Anyone see’d Sam Sharp?” he demanded, in
a voice husky with emotion.
“’Ere I am. Wot is it, Ikey?” inquired that
individual, blandly. “Carn’t you see me an’orl
these good peoples is ’oldin’ a little scientifical
meetin’ ? An’ yet you come bouncin’ inter the
room bellerin’ like a bull in a Chinaman’s shop.”
The infuriated Mr. Cohen opened his mouth,
gasped, sought words and found them not. Then
he thrust his hand into his capacious pocket and
produced an obsolete timepiece, bearing all the
evidences of extreme eld.
“Wodger mean by bringin’a thing like this
’ere round to my place? Wot you know I
wouldn’t give as much as a ’arf a crown fur not if
you wos starvin’ on yer bended knees, and Solly
so shikkered he couldn’t tell a ticker from a fry-
pan ? You didn’t know I wos out —git away with
you ! You watched me go, and that flat-footed
son of a dingo give you a suvrindown on the nail.
’Oly Moses ! I’ll ’ave it in fur you over this, Sam
Sharp, you see ! ”
Mr. Sharp shook his head reproachfully.
“Ho, ’ow kin you say sech’ard thin’s, Ikey?”
he asked, more in sorrow than in anger. “ Wosn’t
that ’ere watch a presentiment one give me fur
savin’ the life of a pore ole man wot fell inter the
’Arbor at Perth, over in the West, an’ lost’s right
leg as fur as the knee tea shark? Would I’ave
sold it to you fur a ’undred thousan’ pound if it
’adn’t a bin that the dear mother of me oldest pal,
wot’s dead an’ gorn. wanted the price of a pint —
meal, meant’say. Well, I know it ain’t gold—
more shame to them as give it me unner false
pretences. Didn’t I tell young Solly, with
the tears rainin’ down me eyes, that it weren’t
worth above three bob at the very outside ? Begged
an’ prayed of ’im, I did, not to tempt me be
offerin’ a a’penny more ’n two an’ eleven. An’
wot did e’ say? ‘You’ve a noble ’eart, Sam,’
says ’e; ‘ you risked yer unval’able life savin’ a

by a thirsty retinue.

Scobie’s Tenner.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rPHE .trooper climbed lazily from the
I saddle and, dropping the reins over a
fence post, strolled over to the hut.
Seobie, with his pipe jutting out at a sharp
angle from his mouth, stood leaning against the
door post awaiting his visitor.
“G’morniu’, Seobie.”
“Mornin’,” replied that gentleman, laconically.
“Findin’ things a bit slow,” asked the trap.
“Oh, they’re not too bad. What’s the
matter'?”
“ Nothing much. I happened to dream that ye
had a bit of a look-in in the trouble on the road,
the other night, and as that tenner’s not turned
up yet I’ve come over to see if it happened to fall
into the turn-up o’ your pants. I’d advise ye
now as Seobie pulled his pipe out of his gash
of a mouth indignantly—“ not t’ bother me, or
I’ll be takin’ t’ you, me lad. Come in and take
things easy while I have a look around.” So say-
ing, the trooper pushed Seobie gently inside the
hut and closed the door.
“Might’s well save yerself the trouble,” said
the suspect, peering at the policeman from under
his hairy brows. “Ye’ll find nothin’, and I.d
like t know what liar put yer on ter me.”
“No one, me dear. If I didn’t know ye so well
I wouldn’t ’a’ bothered ye, but—well, we’ve done
business together before, ain’t we, Seobie ? ”
Seobie smiled., “ Yer puts it delicate,” he said.
The trooper was in a happy mood. “ Well,” he
said, “ there’s no need f’ me t’ be rude about it.
I mark ye understand me, anyhow. Ye’re an in-
telligent man gone wrong, Seobie. Now,'if ye’d
only been caught young— h—ll.’’
In pushing his hand beneath the mattress,
Elliot had scraped it on a nail that stuck through
the floor of the bunk, and the red track showed
deep along the palm.
Scobie was all solicitude.
“ Be th’ livin’ Peter, it’s a snorter of a scratch,
ain’t it ?” he murmured feelingly. “Here. Dip
yer flipper in this, while I fish up a bandage for
yer. Ye’re a careless man, Elliot. Now, if ye’d
only been caught young ”
“Shut up, y’ fool,” barked the injured one.
“ Are ye going to be all day with that bandage ?”
Scobie smiled very sweetly. He seemed pleased
over something, did Scobie, and as he tied up the
wound his face glowed with the light of an arc
lamp.
What’re ye grinnin’ at, ye goat ?” demanded
the exasperated trooper, “I notice that a fellow
can never hurt himself without amusin’ the idiot
population.”
“ Ah, take it easy now, Elliot. I was only
smilin’ because of the sweet relief I was dolin’ out
to ”
“Sweet rats!” And the trooper, all his good
nature gone, re-commenced his search, while
Scobie sat on the edge of the table and watched
him. He made his temper worse by offering to
assist in moving the “furniture,” etc., or by
sarcastically suggesting a few places that hadn’t
been searched. For a good hour the officer pried
and hunted, till not an inch of the place was left
unnoticed. Then, after an unsuccessful search of
the genial Scobie, the trooper reluctantly gave up
the quest, and prepared to take his departure.
“Hold on, Ellior,” said the suspect, “have a
bite and a taste of something before ye leave
us. ”
“Oh, be hanged t’ ye,” responded the officer.
“ Don’t want anything. And mark me, me man,”
he continued truculently, “ye’ve got a plant
somewhere, and this time ye might’ve beaten me.
But never again, me lad—never again.”
• -Scobie looked hurt.
“Surely -ye don’t suspect me after all yer
searchin’, Elliot,” he complained. “Be just,
man.”
“ Oh, be damned.” The trooper turned to
leave.
“ Well, let’s put on a clean bandage before ye
go, Elliot. Ye’ve got that one all dirty, and its
worth while lookin’ out for blood-poisonin’, ye
know. We can’t afford to lose any of the few real
energetic officers we’ve got.”
Elliot was inclined to refuse the proffered kind-
ness, till he remembered that he intended to call
in at Harry MeGlade’s on his way back to the
township, and, of course, Harry’s pretty sister
would in all probability be about, and the bandage
was certainly dirty— too dirty for the spick-
and-span Trooper Elliot. So he reluctantly held
out his hand to the sycophant for treatment.
Scobie skilfully rebandaged the damaged mem-
ber, and flinging the dirty linen into the empty
fireplace, he accompanied his visitor out. to his
horse.
The trooper had thawed visibly under Scobie’s
kind treatment, and his good temper was return-
ing. Looking down at the man, he spoke with a
kindly twinkle in his blue eyes. “Why don’t ye
go a bit straighter, me boy?” be asked. “It’s
always the prad that pulls straight and steady
gets the least whip. Think it over, lad.”
Scobie smiled—an impish, good-natured sort of
grin it was.
“That’s good enough advice f’ a colt, Elliot;
but I’m no colt now, and I was badly broke in, I
was. So long.”
Then Scobie walked back to the hut, and going
to the fireplace, took up the cast-off bandage and
rescued out of it the missing note.
“That was a streak of luck,” he remarked to
himself.
POLSTAN.
The Milestones.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T HE splendid sun of noonday
Is sloping to the west
As I lay down my burden
A little while and rest.
The road that I have travelled
I view with yearnings vain.
Would God that I could travel
That long, long road again.
It ran ’midst shining meadows,
By ever-singing streams,
And through the Wood Enchanted,
And through the Land of Dreams.
And up the hill rejoicing
It ran, and reached the crest
That overlooks the plain lands
And valleys of the West.
Once I stood bravely singing
Upon that green hill’s brow—
The bird within my bosom
Sings not.so bravely now.
Beside that road a milestone
Shines, far off, in the sun;
’Tis wreathed with faded garlands
And numbered—Twenty-one.
The road that I have travelled
I view with yearnings vain.
Would God that I could travel
That long, long road again.
Alas, this fond repining
Is like, for all it yields,
The wind that chases shadows
Across forsaken fields.
The road that lies before me
Is dimmer than a dream,
And deeper grow the shadows,
The milestones closer seem.
And, in the dust-veiled distance,
There glimmers, ghostly white,
The last of all the milestones,
Upon the verge of Night.
A name thereon is graven,
A number too is there ;
Ah me ! That stone so mournful,
What number does it bear ?
VIOTOR J. DALEY.
